Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
The Category Objection: Knowledge as Quality vs. Known as Substance
Presents and refutes the category objection to the identity of knowledge and known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the identity of knowledge and the known was challenged on categorical grounds and how the distinction between mental and external existence resolves it.

Core Idea
Knowledge cannot be identical to the known if they belong to different categories of existence.

A major objection against identifying knowledge with the known is that knowledge is a psychic quality (accident), while the known can be a substance. The reply introduces the crucial distinction: the known exists in the mind as a shadow, not as an external accident. This lesson unpacks that objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage carefully to identify the objection: knowledge is an accident (quality) while the known may be a substance, so they cannot be identical without contradiction.
The objection is presented in the first few lines after 'فيه بحث'.
2
Notice the reply using mental existence
The reply argues that the known exists in the mind with a shadow existence, not as an external accident. This avoids the categorical contradiction because mental existence is not bound by external categories.
The key phrase is 'وجود ظلي'.
3
Reflect on the resolution
Consider why the distinction between mental and external existence preserves the unity of knowledge and known in essence while allowing categorical differences in external existence.

Source Text

قوله : ( ولا) تمايز إلخ لأنه صفة ثبوتية يقتضي ثبوت المثبت له وللمناقشة فيه مجال . قوله: (فإذن لا حقيقة (له أي لا ماهية ثابتة لذلك النفي الصرف إلا الأمر الموجود في الذهن، إذ لا ثبوت إلا في الخارج أو في الذهن. قوله : (هو العلم فيه أن ما تقدم إنما يدل على أنه لا بد في العلم من أمر موجود في الذهن، وأما أنه هو العلم فكلا فلا بد من ضم مقدمة وهي أن التعلق ليس بعلم، لأن العلم يوصف بالمطابقة واللامطابقة، والتعلق لا يوصف بهما، فإذن العلم هو ذلك الأمر الموجود إذ لا ثالث هاهنا . قوله (علم) موجود بوجود أصلي كسائر الكيفيات النفسانية، يترتب عليه الآثار في الخارج، ككون محله عالماً مثلاً وعرض وكيف وباعتبار نفسه من حيث هو معلوم موجود بوجود قوله: (ولا تمايز إلا بأن يكون إلخ) قيل : مذهب الحكماء أن لكل حادث وجوداً، إما في الخارج أو في الذهن فله قبل وجوده معدات متعاقبة تقربه إلى الوجود على مراتب متفاوتة، فلولا أنه يمتاز عما عداه في تلك الحالة التي هي حالة العدم المحض كيف نعقل أن المعد قرب إياه دون غيره، وما يوجد بعد تمام المعدات هو دون غيره، وقد مر بنا في بحث الوجود ما يتقصى به عن أمثال هذه الاعتراضات فليرجع إليه . قوله : ( فالعلم والمعلوم متحدان بالذات إلخ فيه بحث، أما أولاً : فلان العلم عرض من

الأعراض، وباعتبار آخر من أخرى منها فلا محذور قلنا : المعتبر في كون الشيء جوهراً أو عرضاً وجوده الخارجي كما يتبادر من إطلاق لفظ الوجود ولا نزاع لأحد في ذلك، وإلا لزم أن يكون الواجب تعالى عرضاً من وجه ولا يقول به أحد، وأما ثانياً : فلان العلم من الأعراض النفسانية كما اعترفوا به فيكون موجوداً بوجود أصيل قائماً بالنفس موجباً لاتصاف النفس به وكون محله النفس لا يوجب أن يكون وجوده ذهنياً، ولا ينافي أن عندهم غير موجب لاتصاف النفس بها فكيف يكون أحدهما الآخر، وإنما قلنا : الماهية، لكونها معلومة غير موجودة بوجود أصيل، إذ لو كانت موجودة به لزم أن يكون صور المعدومات والممتنعات من قبيل الموجودات الخارجية، فتكون ماهياتها متصفة بالوجود الخارجي، والفرق الاعتباري لا يجدي لأن كون الشيء الواحد موجوداً ومعدوماً في حالة واحدة، ولو كان بحسب الاعتبار لا يعقل فإن قلت يكفي في وجود العلم اتصاف طبيعته به في ضمن بعض أفرادها كصور الموجودات . قلت : الفرق بين أفرادها وجوداً وعدماً تحكم يأباه الوجدان الصحيح على أن إطلاقاتهم تدل على قولهم بوجود جميع أفراد العلم . قوله : ( إذ لا اختلاف بين أفراد حقيقة واحدة (نوعية فيه إيماء إلى أن العلم طبيعة نوعية، وقد سبق منه في المرصد الثاني من الموقف الأول منع كون العلم ذاتياً لما تحته فضلاً عن النوعية، فكأنه سكت هاهنا عن المنع اعتماداً على ما سبق، أو على كون الماهية النوعية للعلوم عين طبيعة العلم ولا شك في بعده، والأظهر ما قالوا من أن الوجدان يحكم بعدم الفرق بين بموجود وعلمنا بمعدوم، فإذا كان أحدهما بالانطباع كان الآخر كذلك، وأما حديث حجة الوجدان فقد مر تحقيقه فيما سبق . قوله : ( متصفة بالوجود (الخارجي) إذا حكم على مفهوم كلي بأنه موجود في الخارج أو علمنا
His statement: 'and no distinction etc.' because it is an affirmative attribute requiring affirmation of the subject, and there is room for discussion. His statement: 'then no reality for it' i.e., no fixed essence for that pure negation except the thing existing in the mind, since there is no affirmation except in the exterior or in the mind. His statement: 'it is knowledge' in it is that what preceded only indicates that knowledge must involve something existing in the mind, but that it is knowledge itself? No, so it is necessary to add a premise: that the relation is not knowledge, because knowledge is described as conformity and non-conformity, while relation is not described by them. Therefore knowledge is that existing thing since there is no third here. His statement: 'knowledge exists with an original existence like other psychic qualities, producing effects in the exterior, e.g., its locus being knowing, and it is an accident and quality, and considered in itself as it is known, it exists with an existence...' His statement: 'and no distinction except that each has some affirmation' — it is said: the doctrine of the philosophers is that every contingent has an existence either in the exterior or in the mind, so before its existence it has successive preparations that bring it closer to existence on varying degrees. Were it not that it is distinguished from others in that state of pure non-existence, how could we conceive that the preparation brought it near rather than another? And what exists after the completion of preparations is itself and not another. And we have already passed in the discussion of existence what suffices to refute such objections, so refer to it. His statement: 'knowledge and the known are united in essence etc.' — in it there is investigation. Firstly, because knowledge is an accident from...
The second type - first goal: the relation between the knower and the known by consideration. And if knowledge of external non-existents is in this state, then knowledge of all knowns must be likewise, since there is no difference among individuals of one specific reality. Then, the thing existing in the mind may correspond to a thing in the exterior, such that that essence which is characterized by mental existence is also characterized by external existence, and may not correspond to it, as a shadow when compared to external existence. So it may be a substance and may be an accident, and there is no contradiction in one thing being substance and accident by two considerations, so reflect for it is a slippery slope. It is of the category of quality as before, and the known essence need not be an accident; and if it is an accident, it need not agree with knowledge in category, so their union would be impossible because it would entail the thing being both substance and accident, or an accident from two categories, both of which are impossible. If it is said: the impossible is for a thing to be substance and accident simultaneously or an accident from two categories from one aspect, and here that is not required, for the known is an accident from the aspect of its subsistence in the subject which is the soul, and it is an essence that if found externally would be not in a subject, and there is no contradiction in this nor in the case where by the first consideration it is from a category of accidents and by another consideration from another category, so no problem. We say: the determinant in calling something substance or accident is its external existence, as is understood from the usage of the word existence, and no one disputes that; otherwise the Necessary Exalted would be an accident in some respect, which no one holds. Secondly, because knowledge is of the psychic accidents as they admit, so it exists with an original existence subsisting in the soul, causing the soul to be qualified by it; and the fact that its locus is the soul does not entail that its existence is mental, nor does it contradict its being original and external, given what you know of their meanings. For all psychic qualities like power and the like, though their locus is the soul, are external existents. The essence as known is not present in the soul with an original existence but with a shadow existence according to them, which does not cause the soul to be qualified by it, so how can one be the other? And we only said: the essence as known is not present with an original existence, because if it were present with it, then forms of non-existents and impossibles would be among external existents, so their essences would be characterized by external existence; and the difference by consideration is of no use because one thing being existent and non-existent in a single state, even if by consideration, is inconceivable. If you say: it suffices for the existence of knowledge that its nature be instantiated in some of its individuals like forms of existents. I say: the difference between its individuals in existence and non-existence is a judgment repudiated by sound intuition. Moreover, their usages indicate their saying that all individuals of knowledge have existence. His statement: 'since there is no difference among individuals of one specific reality' — in it is an indication that knowledge is a specific nature; and he has already in the second observatory of the first stand prevented knowledge being essential to what is under it, let alone being a species. So it is as if he is silent here about the prevention, relying on what preceded, or on the species-essence of knowledges being identical to the nature of knowledge, and there is no doubt of its remoteness. The most apparent is what they said: that intuition judges no difference between our knowledge of an existent and our knowledge of a non-existent, so if one of them is by imprinting, the other is likewise. As for the discussion of the intuition argument, its verification has passed earlier. His statement: 'characterized by external existence' — if one judges of a universal concept that it exists externally or our knowledge...
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and answers regarding the nature of knowledge and its identity with the known. It begins with glosses on the previous argument, clarifying that the relation is not knowledge and that knowledge requires an existing thing in the mind. Then it presents a major objection: if knowledge and the known are identical, then since knowledge is an accident (from the category of quality), the known would also have to be an accident, but the known can be a substance, etc. The answer distinguishes between mental and external existence: the known exists in the mind as a mental entity (shadow existence), not as an external accident. The passage also discusses that mental existence may correspond to external existence or not. It refutes the idea that the same thing could be both substance and accident in the same respect, and argues that the categories are based on external existence. Finally, it addresses the uniformity of knowledge across existents and non-existents, relying on intuition.

Key Terms
الجوهر
العرض
الوجود الذهني
الوجود الخارجي
الوجود الظلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that mental existence makes the known an accident in the mind, so knowledge and known would both be accidents, avoiding conflict.
Correction: The passage clarifies that mental existence is shadow existence, not original, and does not qualify the soul. The known as mental is not an accident in the external sense.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can’t knowledge and the known be identical if knowledge is an accident?
A) Because the known is always a substance
B) Because the known does not have external existence
C) Because knowledge is in the category of quality while the known can be in any category
D) Because the known is a mental entity

Key Takeaway
The identity of knowledge and the known is preserved by recognizing that the known exists in the mind with a shadow existence, avoiding categorical conflict.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You mentally represent many things—yourself, others, Allah—as concepts in your mind. Are you ever guilty of treating those mental shadows as if they were the external realities themselves? What contradictions arise when you conflate the two, and how can this lesson's distinction protect you from such errors?
?
This week, identify one belief you hold about someone or something that you have not verified externally. How much of that belief is your own mental construction (shadow) versus the actual external reality? Commit to examining one such belief and seeing if the contradiction between your mental image and reality has led you to injustice or misunderstanding.

Rational Judgments
intermediate
5 min
Mental Existence and Uniformity of Knowledge
Discusses correspondence of mental existence with external existence and argues for uniformity of knowledge across all knowns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how mental existence can correspond or not to external existence, and why knowledge of existents and non-existents must be uniform.

Core Idea
Mental existence is a shadow that may or may not reflect external reality, but knowledge of all things is uniform.

After establishing the distinction between mental and external existence, the author discusses how mental existence can correspond to external existence (as in knowledge of actual existents) or not (as in knowledge of non-existents). He then argues that knowledge is a single nature, so knowledge of an existent and a non-existent must be of the same kind. This lesson explores these points and the implications for the theory of mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand correspondence
Read the passage to grasp the idea that mental existence may match external existence (when the essence exists both in mind and externally) or not (when it does not).
The phrase 'قد يطابقه' and 'قد لا يطابقه' are key.
2
Consider uniformity of knowledge
Notice the claim that knowledge is a single specific nature, so knowledge of a existent must be like knowledge of a non-existent. The author relies on intuition (وجدان) for this.
The passage references a previous discussion on intuition.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this uniformity is important: if knowledge of non-existents were different, the identity of knowledge as a single nature would be undermined. The shadow existence theory ensures consistency.

إيماء إلى أن العلم طبيعة نوعية، وقد سبق منه في المرصد الثاني من الموقف الأول منع كون العلم ذاتياً لما تحته فضلاً عن النوعية، فكأنه سكت هاهنا عن المنع اعتماداً على ما سبق، أو على كون الماهية النوعية للعلوم عين طبيعة العلم ولا شك في بعده، والأظهر ما قالوا من أن الوجدان يحكم بعدم الفرق بين بموجود وعلمنا بمعدوم، فإذا كان أحدهما بالانطباع كان الآخر كذلك، وأما حديث حجة الوجدان فقد مر تحقيقه فيما سبق . His statement: 'and no distinction etc.' because it is an affirmative attribute requiring affirmation of the subject, and there is room for discussion. His statement: 'then no reality for it' i.e., no fixed essence for that pure negation except the thing existing in the mind, since there is no affirmation except in the exterior or in the mind. His statement: 'it is knowledge' in it is that what preceded only indicates that knowledge must involve something existing in the mind, but that it is knowledge itself? No, so it is necessary to add a premise: that the relation is not knowledge, because knowledge is described as conformity and non-conformity, while relation is not described by them. Therefore knowledge is that existing thing since there is no third here. His statement: 'knowledge exists with an original existence like other psychic qualities, producing effects in the exterior, e.g., its locus being knowing, and it is an accident and quality, and considered in itself as it is known, it exists with an existence...' His statement: 'and no distinction except that each has some affirmation' — it is said: the doctrine of the philosophers is that every contingent has an existence either in the exterior or in the mind, so before its existence it has successive preparations that bring it closer to existence on varying degrees. Were it not that it is distinguished from others in that state of pure non-existence, how could we conceive that the preparation brought it near rather than another? And what exists after the completion of preparations is itself and not another. And we have already passed in the discussion of existence what suffices to refute such objections, so refer to it. His statement: 'knowledge and the known are united in essence etc.' — in it there is investigation. Firstly, because knowledge is an accident from...
The second type - first goal: the relation between the knower and the known by consideration. And if knowledge of external non-existents is in this state, then knowledge of all knowns must be likewise, since there is no difference among individuals of one specific reality. Then, the thing existing in the mind may correspond to a thing in the exterior, such that that essence which is characterized by mental existence is also characterized by external existence, and may not correspond to it, as a shadow when compared to external existence. So it may be a substance and may be an accident, and there is no contradiction in one thing being substance and accident by two considerations, so reflect for it is a slippery slope. It is of the category of quality as before, and the known essence need not be an accident; and if it is an accident, it need not agree with knowledge in category, so their union would be impossible because it would entail the thing being both substance and accident, or an accident from two categories, both of which are impossible. If it is said: the impossible is for a thing to be substance and accident simultaneously or an accident from two categories from one aspect, and here that is not required, for the known is an accident from the aspect of its subsistence in the subject which is the soul, and it is an essence that if found externally would be not in a subject, and there is no contradiction in this nor in the case where by the first consideration it is from a category of accidents and by another consideration from another category, so no problem. We say: the determinant in calling something substance or accident is its external existence, as is understood from the usage of the word existence, and no one disputes that; otherwise the Necessary Exalted would be an accident in some respect, which no one holds. Secondly, because knowledge is of the psychic accidents as they admit, so it exists with an original existence subsisting in the soul, causing the soul to be qualified by it; and the fact that its locus is the soul does not entail that its existence is mental, nor does it contradict its being original and external, given what you know of their meanings. For all psychic qualities like power and the like, though their locus is the soul, are external existents. The essence as known is not present in the soul with an original existence but with a shadow existence according to them, which does not cause the soul to be qualified by it, so how can one be the other? And we only said: the essence as known is not present with an original existence, because if it were present with it, then forms of non-existents and impossibles would be among external existents, so their essences would be characterized by external existence; and the difference by consideration is of no use because one thing being existent and non-existent in a single state, even if by consideration, is inconceivable. If you say: it suffices for the existence of knowledge that its nature be instantiated in some of its individuals like forms of existents. I say: the difference between its individuals in existence and non-existence is a judgment repudiated by sound intuition. Moreover, their usages indicate their saying that all individuals of knowledge have existence. His statement: 'since there is no difference among individuals of one specific reality' — in it is an indication that knowledge is a specific nature; and he has already in the second observatory of the first stand prevented knowledge being essential to what is under it, let alone being a species. So it is as if he is silent here about the prevention, relying on what preceded, or on the species-essence of knowledges being identical to the nature of knowledge, and there is no doubt of its remoteness. The most apparent is what they said: that intuition judges no difference between our knowledge of an existent and our knowledge of a non-existent, so if one of them is by imprinting, the other is likewise. As for the discussion of the intuition argument, its verification has passed earlier. His statement: 'characterized by external existence' — if one judges of a universal concept that it exists externally or our knowledge...
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and answers regarding the nature of knowledge and its identity with the known. It begins with glosses on the previous argument, clarifying that the relation is not knowledge and that knowledge requires an existing thing in the mind. Then it presents a major objection: if knowledge and the known are identical, then since knowledge is an accident (from the category of quality), the known would also have to be an accident, but the known can be a substance, etc. The answer distinguishes between mental and external existence: the known exists in the mind as a mental entity (shadow existence), not as an external accident. The passage also discusses that mental existence may correspond to external existence or not. It refutes the idea that the same thing could be both substance and accident in the same respect, and argues that the categories are based on external existence. Finally, it addresses the uniformity of knowledge across existents and non-existents, relying on intuition.

Key Terms

الوجود الذهني
الوجود الخارجي
الماهية
الوجدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowledge of non-existents is a different kind of knowledge than knowledge of existents.
Correction: The passage argues that intuition (وجدان) shows no difference, so knowledge is uniform across all objects.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what supports the claim that knowledge of existents and non-existents is the same?
A) Logical deduction
B) Sound intuition (وجدان)
C) The authority of previous scholars
D) The correspondence theory

Key Takeaway
Mental existence is a shadow that may correspond to external existence or not, but knowledge itself is a single uniform nature across all objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often assume that your knowledge of things that exist is more real than your knowledge of things that are absent. How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what does it mean for your certainty about Allah's knowledge of your unseen actions?
?
When you think of Allah—who is not a created existent—does your mental image feel different than when you think of a tree? The author says knowledge is uniform. Does that comfort you or trouble you? How will you adjust your understanding of your own knowledge of the unseen?
?
The author relies on intuition (وجدان) to claim no difference between knowledge of an existent and a non-existent. Test your own intuition: do you truly perceive a difference in the nature of your knowledge when you think of something real versus something imaginary? If your intuition agrees with the author, will you trust it as a basis for theological certainty, or will you demand more proof?

The Uniformity of Knowledge and the Objection of Mental Existence
States the uniformity principle and the objection concerning substance-accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author asserts that all knowledge is similar and the objection that mental existence could make a thing both substance and accident.

Core Idea
All knowledge is uniform; mental existence does not change substance-accident categories.

This lesson introduces the principle that knowledge of all things is alike, followed by a key objection: if something exists mentally, it could be both substance and accident, which is absurd. The author lays the groundwork for a crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is dense; focus on the flow of argument.
2
Identify the principle of uniformity
The passage begins by stating that knowledge of external non-existents implies knowledge of all things is similar, because all individuals of a nature are alike. Notice the reasoning.
This principle is the foundation of the objection.
3
Understand the objection
The objection is that if mental existence is a shadow, then a single quiddity could be both substance (externally) and accident (in the mind), which is impossible. Find where this contradiction is drawn.
The objector argues that this leads to two impossibilities: being substance and accident together, or accident from two categories.

النوع الثاني - المقصد الأول : إضافة ما بين العالم والمعلوم بالاعتبار، وإذا كان العلم بالمعدومات الخارجية على هذه الحالة وجب أن يكون العلم بسائر المعلومات كذلك، إذ لا اختلاف بين أفراد حقيقة واحدة نوعية، ( ثم) إن الأمر الموجود في الذهن (قد يطابقه أمر في الخارج بأن تكون تلك الماهية التي اتصفت بالوجود الذهني متصفة بالوجود الخارجي أيضاً، (وقد لا يطابقه) بأن لا ظلي إذا قيس إلى الوجود الخارجي، فقد يكون جوهراً وقد يكون عرضاً ولا منافاة بين كون شيء واحد جوهراً وعرضاً بالاعتبارين فتدبر فإنه من المزالق . هو مقولة الكيف كما سبق، والماهية المعلومة لا يلزم أن تكون عرضاً، وإذا كانت عرضاً لا يلزم أن تكون موافقة للعلم في المقولة فيمتنع اتحداهما، لأنه يلزم منه كون الشيء جوهراً وعرضاً معاً، أو عرضاً من مقولتين وكلاهما محالان، فإن قيل : المحال كون الشيء جوهراً وعرضاً معاً أو عرضاً من مقولتين من جهة واحدة وهاهنا لا يلزم ذلك فإن المعلوم عرض من جهة قيامه بالموضوع الذي النفس وجوهو من حيث أنه ماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت لا في موضوع، ولا منافاة في هذا ولا فيما إذا كان بالاعتبار الأول من مقولة من الأعراض، وباعتبار آخر من أخرى منها فلا محذور قلنا : المعتبر في كون الشيء جوهراً أو عرضاً وجوده الخارجي كما يتبادر من إطلاق لفظ الوجود ولا نزاع لأحد في ذلك، وإلا لزم أن يكون الواجب تعالى عرضاً من وجه ولا يقول به أحد، وأما ثانياً : فلان العلم من الأعراض النفسانية كما اعترفوا به فيكون موجوداً بوجود أصيل قائماً بالنفس موجباً لاتصاف النفس به وكون محله النفس لا يوجب أن يكون وجوده ذهنياً، ولا ينافي أن يكون خارجياً أصيلاً لما علمت من معناهما، فإنَّ جميع الكيفيات النفسانية مثل: القدرة ونحوها وإن كان محلها النفس لكنها موجودات خارجية، والماهية لكونها معلومة غير موجودة في النفس بوجود أصيل بل بوجود ظلي عندهم غير موجب لاتصاف النفس بها فكيف يكون أحدهما الآخر، وإنما قلنا : الماهية، لكونها معلومة غير موجودة بوجود أصيل، إذ لو كانت موجودة به لزم أن يكون صور المعدومات والممتنعات من قبيل الموجودات الخارجية، فتكون ماهياتها متصفة بالوجود الخارجي، والفرق الاعتباري لا يجدي لأن كون الشيء الواحد موجوداً ومعدوماً في حالة واحدة، ولو كان بحسب الاعتبار لا يعقل فإن قلت يكفي في وجود العلم اتصاف طبيعته به في ضمن بعض أفرادها كصور الموجودات . قلت : الفرق بين أفرادها وجودا وعدماً تحكم يأباه الوجدان الصحيح على أن إطلاقاتهم تدل على قولهم بوجود جميع أفراد العلم . قوله : ( إذ لا اختلاف بين أفراد حقيقة واحدة (نوعية فيه إيماء إلى أن العلم طبيعة نوعية، وقد سبق منه في المرصد الثاني من الموقف الأول منع كون العلم ذاتياً لما تحته فضلاً عن النوعية، فكأنه سكت هاهنا عن المنع اعتماداً على ما سبق، أو على كون الماهية النوعية للعلوم عين طبيعة العلم ولا شك في بعده، والأظهر ما قالوا من أن الوجدان يحكم بعدم الفرق بين بموجود وعلمنا بمعدوم، فإذا كان أحدهما بالانطباع كان الآخر كذلك، وأما حديث حجة الوجدان فقد مر تحقيقه فيما سبق .
Second Type - First Objective: The relation between the knower and the known by consideration. And if knowledge of external non-existents is in this state, then knowledge of all known things must be similar, because there is no difference among individuals of one specific nature. (Then) the thing existing in the mind (may be matched by a thing in the outside) by that quiddity which is characterized by mental existence also being characterized by external existence, (and may not be matched) by not being a shadow when compared to external existence, so it may be substance and may be accident, and there is no contradiction in one thing being substance and accident by two considerations, so ponder, for it is a slippery slope. It is the category of quality as mentioned, and the known quiddity does not necessarily have to be an accident, and if it is an accident it does not necessarily have to agree with knowledge in category, so their union is impossible, because it would entail the thing being both substance and accident, or accident from two categories, both impossible. If it is said: The impossible is the thing being substance and accident together or accident from two categories from one aspect, but here it does not entail that, because the known is an accident from the aspect of its subsistence in the subject which the soul..., and from the perspective that it is a quiddity that if it exists externally it would not be in a subject, and there is no contradiction in this, nor in the case where by the first consideration it is from a category of accidents and by another from another, so there is no objection. We say: The criterion for a thing being substance or accident is its external existence, as is understood from the unrestricted use of the word existence, and no one disputes that, otherwise the Necessary Exalted would be an accident in some respect, which no one holds. And secondly, because knowledge is among the psychological accidents as they acknowledged, so it exists with a real existence subsisting in the soul, causing the soul to be qualified by it, and the fact that its locus is the soul does not necessitate that its existence is mental, nor does it contradict its being real and external, given what you know of their meanings. For all psychological qualities such as power and the like, even if their locus is the soul, they are external existents. The quiddity, because it is known, does not exist in the soul with a real existence but with a shadow existence according to them, not causing the soul to be qualified by it, so how can one be the other? And we only said: the quiddity, because it is known, does not exist with a real existence, for if it did, then the forms of non-existents and impossibles would be of the class of external existents, so their quiddities would be characterized by external existence, and the conceptual distinction is of no use, because one thing being existent and non-existent in a single state, even if by consideration, is not rationally conceivable. If you say: It is sufficient for the existence of knowledge that its nature be characterized by it within some of its individuals, like the forms of existents. I say: The difference among its individuals in existence and non-existence is a judgment rejected by sound intuition, especially since their usages indicate their assertion of the existence of all individuals of knowledge. His statement: (Since there is no difference among individuals of a single specific nature) contains a suggestion that knowledge is a specific nature, and he has already in the Second Observatory of the First Posture denied that knowledge is essential for what falls under it, let alone being a species, so it seems he remained silent here about the denial relying on what preceded, or on the fact that the specific quiddity of the sciences is the same as the nature of knowledge, and there is no doubt about its remoteness. The most manifest is what they said: that sound intuition judges no difference between an existent and our knowledge of a non-existent, so if one is by impression, the other is likewise. As for the argument of intuition, its investigation has passed earlier.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section within a larger discussion of mental existence. It first states the principle that all knowledge must be similar because there is no difference among individuals of a single specific nature. The author then faces the objection: if mental images are mere shadows compared to external existence, then the same thing could be both substance and accident (e.g., an accident in the mind but substance externally), which is impossible. The objector argues that the impossibility only arises from one aspect, and here the aspects differ (mental vs. external consideration). The answer is that the substance/accident distinction is based only on external existence; mental existence does not change the essence. Furthermore, knowledge itself is a psychological quality (accident) existing really in the soul, not a mere mental existence, so the known quiddity (which is in the mind as a shadow) cannot be identical to knowledge. The commentator then explains that the original text's phrase about no difference among individuals of one nature hints that knowledge is a specific nature, though the author previously denied knowledge being a genus or species; the reconciliation is that the denial was based on an earlier discussion, or that the specific nature here is identical to the nature of knowledge itself. The passage concludes with the intuitive judgment that knowledge of an existent and knowledge of a non-existent are both by impression, hence similar.

وجود خارجي
جوهر
عرض
ماهية
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental existence alone can make a thing both substance and accident.
Correction: The passage shows that substance and accident are based on external existence, not mental; the impossibility arises only if one conflates the two.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must knowledge of all things be similar?
A) Because knowledge is a psychological accident.
B) Because there is no difference among individuals of a single specific nature.
C) Because mental existence makes them alike.
D) Because external existence is uniform.

Key Takeaway
All knowledge belongs to the same nature, so its individuals share the same principle; the objection warns against conflating mental and external categories.

?

The Answer: Distinction of Aspects and Criterion of External Existence
Answers the objection by establishing the criterion of external existence and explaining the nature of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author resolves the objection by clarifying that substance and accident are based on external existence, and that knowledge is a real psychological accident, not a mere mental shadow.

Core Idea
Substance and accident are judged by external existence; mental existence is only a shadow.

In this lesson, we follow the author's detailed answer to the objection. He asserts that the substance-accident distinction only applies externally, and knowledge itself is a real psychological quality. The known quiddity exists only as a shadow in the mind, preserving the consistency of the categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'فإن قيل' to the end. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a counter-objection, then the author's reply with multiple points.
The answer is layered; stay with the argument.
2
Grasp the criterion for substance/accident
The author states that the criterion is external existence only. This is key to resolving the earlier objection. Find this claim in the text.
If substance/accident is based on external existence, mental existence does not affect the category.
3
Understand the nature of knowledge
Knowledge is a psychological accident (quality) with real existence in the soul. The known quiddity exists only as a shadow, not with real existence, so it cannot be identical to knowledge.
This distinction saves the consistency of the categories.
4
Reflect on the intuitive argument
The passage ends with an appeal to sound intuition: if knowledge of an existent is by impression, so is knowledge of a non-existent. This reinforces the uniformity principle.
The commentator adds a note about the specific nature of knowledge.

And if knowledge of external non-existents is in this state, then knowledge of all known things must be similar, because there is no difference among individuals of one specific nature. (Then) the thing existing in the mind (may be matched by a thing in the outside) by that quiddity which is characterized by mental existence also being characterized by external existence, (and may not be matched) by not being a shadow when compared to external existence, so it may be substance and may be accident, and there is no contradiction in one thing being substance and accident by two considerations, so ponder, for it is a slippery slope. It is the category of quality as mentioned, and the known quiddity does not necessarily have to be an accident, and if it is an accident it does not necessarily have to agree with knowledge in category, so their union is impossible, because it would entail the thing being both substance and accident, or accident from two categories, both impossible. If it is said: The impossible is the thing being substance and accident together or accident from two categories from one aspect, but here it does not entail that, because the known is an accident from the aspect of its subsistence in the subject which the soul..., and from the perspective that it is a quiddity that if it exists externally it would not be in a subject, and there is no contradiction in this, nor in the case where by the first consideration it is from a category of accidents and by another from another, so there is no objection. We say: The criterion for a thing being substance or accident is its external existence, as is understood from the unrestricted use of the word existence, and no one disputes that, otherwise the Necessary Exalted would be an accident in some respect, which no one holds. And secondly, because knowledge is among the psychological accidents as they acknowledged, so it exists with a real existence subsisting in the soul, causing the soul to be qualified by it, and the fact that its locus is the soul does not necessitate that its existence is mental, nor does it contradict its being real and external, given what you know of their meanings. For all psychological qualities such as power and the like, even if their locus is the soul, they are external existents. The quiddity, because it is known, does not exist in the soul with a real existence but with a shadow existence according to them, not causing the soul to be qualified by it, so how can one be the other? And we only said: the quiddity, because it is known, does not exist with a real existence, for if it did, then the forms of non-existents and impossibles would be of the class of external existents, so their quiddities would be characterized by external existence, and the conceptual distinction is of no use, because one thing being existent and non-existent in a single state, even if by consideration, is not rationally conceivable. If you say: It is sufficient for the existence of knowledge that its nature be characterized by it within some of its individuals, like the forms of existents. I say: The difference among its individuals in existence and non-existence is a judgment rejected by sound intuition, especially since their usages indicate their assertion of the existence of all individuals of knowledge. His statement: (Since there is no difference among individuals of a single specific nature) contains a suggestion that knowledge is a specific nature, and he has already in the Second Observatory of the First Posture denied that knowledge is essential for what falls under it, let alone being a species, so it seems he remained silent here about the denial relying on what preceded, or on the fact that the specific quiddity of the sciences is the same as the nature of knowledge, and there is no doubt about its remoteness. The most manifest is what they said: that sound intuition judges no difference between an existent and our knowledge of a non-existent, so if one is by impression, the other is likewise. As for the argument of intuition, its investigation has passed earlier.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section within a larger discussion of mental existence. It first states the principle that all knowledge must be similar because there is no difference among individuals of a single specific nature. The author then faces the objection: if mental images are mere shadows compared to external existence, then the same thing could be both substance and accident (e.g., an accident in the mind but substance externally), which is impossible. The objector argues that the impossibility only arises from one aspect, and here the aspects differ (mental vs. external consideration). The answer is that the substance/accident distinction is based only on external existence; mental existence does not change the essence. Furthermore, knowledge itself is a psychological quality (accident) existing really in the soul, not a mere mental existence, so the known quiddity (which is in the mind as a shadow) cannot be identical to knowledge. The commentator then explains that the original text's phrase about no difference among individuals of one nature hints that knowledge is a specific nature, though the author previously denied knowledge being a genus or species; the reconciliation is that the denial was based on an earlier discussion, or that the specific nature here is identical to the nature of knowledge itself. The passage concludes with the intuitive judgment that knowledge of an existent and knowledge of a non-existent are both by impression, hence similar.

وجود خارجي
جوهر

كيف نفساني
انطباع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental existence makes the known quiddity a real accident in the soul.
Correction: The known quiddity exists only as a shadow (not real existence); knowledge itself is real but as a psychological accident, not as the quiddity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the criterion for a thing being substance or accident?
A) Its mental existence.
B) Its external existence.
C) Its quiddity.
D) Its being known.

Key Takeaway
The substance/accident distinction depends only on external existence; knowledge itself is a real psychological quality, while the known quiddity is a mere shadow, preserving the consistency of categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you know that Allah exists. Is that knowledge a real quality that subsists in your soul and moves your limbs, or is it merely a mental shadow that leaves you unchanged? How would your life look different today if that knowledge were truly real? What one action can you take this week to prove to yourself that your knowledge of Allah is not just a shadow?
?
The passage states that knowledge of a non-existent (like the Day of Judgment) is still real knowledge by impression. Does your conviction about the Day of Judgment feel as vivid and present as your knowledge of something you see right now? If not, what has dulled its reality in your heart, and what can you do to restore it?
?
If shadow existence were enough to make something real, then impossible things would be real. Are there any false beliefs or impossible hopes you have entertained as if they were real? How can the clear criterion of external existence help you distinguish between what truly exists (like your duties) and what is merely a shadow (like idle fantasies)?

The Two Interpretations of Correspondence in Mental Existence
Explains two interpretations of how mental existence receives external judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible interpretations of how a mental existent can be said to receive external judgments, and why the second interpretation is more consistent with the overall argument.

Core Idea
Mental existence receives external judgments either by actual correspondence or by the intellect's application regardless of correspondence.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a crucial phrase from the original text: 'by this consideration.' Two different interpretations emerge, each with distinct implications for how we understand mental entities and their relationship to external reality. The first interpretation takes correspondence literally, while the second considers the mental quiddity purely in itself, allowing both true and false applications. This lesson walks through both views and the reasoning behind preferring the second.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
Read the passage and note the two possible meanings for 'بهذا الاعتبار': one based on actual correspondence, the other based on the mental quiddity considered without condition.
The phrase appears in the original text; the commentator is explaining its ambiguity.
2
Understand the first interpretation
The first interpretation ties external judgments to the actual external existence of the quiddity. External attributes like blackness only apply when the quiddity exists outside the mind.
This is the apparent meaning from common usage.
3
Understand the second interpretation
The second interpretation treats the mental existent as a neutral subject; the intellect can apply external predicates truly or falsely. This allows for a general possibility of correspondence or non-correspondence.
Here the mental quiddity is considered as such, without any condition.
4
See why the second is preferred
The commentator argues that the second interpretation better fits the original statement that 'from the aspect of being in the mind, it has no judgment,' because 'no judgment' means the intellect cannot apply a judgment based solely on mental existence, not that the mental image lacks accidents.
The original text supports a reading where the mind cannot judge based on mental existence alone, aligns with the second interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( متصفة بالوجود (الخارجي) إذا حكم على مفهوم كلي بأنه موجود في الخارج أو علمنا أن تكون تلك الماهية موجودة في الخارج، وبهذا الاعتبار) أي باعتبار المطابقة (تلحقه ) أي ذلك الموجود الذهني ( الأحكام الخارجية ) من السواد والبياض والحركة والسكون ونظائرها فإن الماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج لم تخل من أمور تعرض لها بحسب هذا الوجود وتختص به فلا تكون عارضة لها حال كونها موجودة أَنَّ في الذهن ويحتمل أن يراد بهذا الاعتبار ؛ اعتبار المطابقة واللامطابقة، على معنى الموجود الذهني بمجرد حصوله فيه ملحوظ من حيث هو هو، ومن هذه الحيثية يجوز أن يكون له مطابق في الخارج وأن لا يكون، ويمكن للعقل أن يجري عليه أحكاماً خارجية صادقة أو كاذبة، وهذا الاحتمال أنسب بقوله : ( وأما من حيث هو موجود ) في الذهن ( فلا حكم (له أي لا يمكن للعقل أن يحكم عليه من هذه الحيثية، إلا بأن يتصور مرة ثانية من حيث أنه في الذهن، فيحكم عليه بأحكام أخر) مخالفة للأحكام قوله : ( أي باعتبار المطابقة) أي باعتبار أنه موجود في الخارج. قوله : (فإن الماهية إلخ) فعلى هذا التوجيه اللحوق بمعنى العروض، والأحكام الخارجية بمعنى المحمولات التي تتصف بها الأشياء في الخارج، وهو الظاهر المتبادر من الحجارة، ولذا قدمه وعلى التوجيه الثاني يكون اللحوق عبارة عن إجراء العقل عليه تلك المحمولات سواء كانت صادقة أو كاذبة يعني باعتبار صحة اتصافه بالمطابقة واللامطابقة وهو اعتباره من حيث هو، فإنَّ الماهية لا بشرط شيء يمكن أن يوجد وأن لا يوجد ، يمكن للعقل إجراء المحمولات الخارجية عليها صادقة كانت أو كاذبة بعد الإجراء وهذا التوجيه وإن كان صرفاً للحوق عن المعنى المتبادر ، لكنه أنسب ،بقوله، وأما من حيث هو موجود في الذهن فلا حكم له، إذ معناه لا يمكن للعقل أن يجري عليه حكماً، لا أنه لا يعرض له حكم، فإنَّ الأمر الموجود في الذهن له عوارض ذهنية، وإن لم يحكم العقل بها عليه مرة، وقوله : ومحصول الكلام أي على التوجيه الثاني .
His statement: (Characterized by external existence) if one judges of a universal concept that it exists externally, or we know that quiddity to exist externally, and by this consideration, i.e., by consideration of correspondence, the mental existent is attached to it—i.e., that mental existent—the external judgments, such as blackness, whiteness, motion, rest, and their likes. For the quiddity, when it exists externally, is not devoid of things that attach to it according to this existence and are specific to it, so they are not accidents to it while it exists in the mind. And it is possible that by this consideration is intended the consideration of correspondence and non-correspondence, meaning that the mental existent, merely by its occurrence in the mind, is observed in itself, and from this aspect it is possible that it has a match in the outside and that it does not, and the intellect can apply to it external judgments, true or false. This probability is more suitable to his statement: (And as for from the aspect that it exists in the mind, it has no judgment, i.e., the intellect cannot judge about it from this aspect, except by conceiving it a second time from the aspect that it is in the mind, and then judges it with other judgments) contrary to the judgments. His statement: (i.e., by consideration of correspondence) i.e., by consideration that it exists externally. His statement: (For the quiddity, etc.) So according to this direction, the attachment means the occurrence, and the external judgments mean the predicates by which things are characterized externally, and that is the apparent meaning from the [common?] stones, so he presented it. And according to the second direction, the attachment is an expression of the intellect's application of those predicates to it, whether they are true or false, meaning by consideration of the validity of its being characterized by correspondence and non-correspondence, and it is its consideration as it is, for the quiddity without condition can exist and can not exist; the intellect can apply external predicates to it, true or false, after the application. And this direction, even though it turns the attachment from the apparent meaning, is more suitable, because of his statement: And as for from the aspect that it exists in the mind, it has no judgment, for its meaning is that the intellect cannot apply a judgment to it, not that no judgment occurs to it, for the thing existing in the mind has mental accidents, even if the intellect does not apply them to it at one time. And his statement: The summary of the speech is according to the second direction.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of two possible interpretations of the phrase 'بهذا الاعتبار' (by this consideration) in the original text regarding mental existence and external judgments. The commentator first clarifies that when a mental quiddity is characterized by external existence (i.e., it corresponds to something external), then it receives external judgments like blackness, whiteness, etc.—these are attributes that belong only to the external thing, not to the mental image. Then he presents a second interpretation: the consideration is of correspondence and non-correspondence in general. Under this view, the mental existent is considered purely in itself, without regard to whether it matches external reality. From this perspective, it is possible that it corresponds or not, and the intellect can apply external predicates (like 'black') either truly or falsely. The commentator argues that this second interpretation is more suitable to the original text's later statement that 'from the aspect of being in the mind, it has no judgment,' because here 'no judgment' means the intellect cannot apply a judgment based on that mental existence alone—not that the mental image lacks all attributes (it still has mental accidents). The passage then contrasts the two directions: the first takes 'attachment' (لحوق) as the actual occurrence of external predicates in the external thing, while the second takes it as the mind's application of those predicates to the quiddity considered without condition. The commentator notes that the second is less obvious but more consistent with the overall argument.

Key Terms
اعتبار المطابقة
اعتبار اللامطابقة
موجود ذهني
أحكام خارجية
ماهية لا بشرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mental image itself literally possesses external properties like blackness or whiteness.
Correction: External judgments are either applied to the external counterpart (first interpretation) or to the quiddity considered unconditionally by the intellect (second interpretation); the mental image itself has only mental accidents.

Checkpoint
What is the author's preferred interpretation of 'بهذا الاعتبار' (by this consideration)?
A) It refers to the actual existence of the quiddity in the external world.
B) It refers to the consideration of the mental existent purely in itself, allowing correspondence or non-correspondence.
C) It refers to the mental accident of blackness.
D) It refers to the judgment of the intellect that the quiddity exists externally.

Key Takeaway
The second interpretation, which views the mental existent as a neutral subject for the intellect's application of predicates, is more consistent with the overall argument about the limitations of judgments based solely on mental existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you conceive of a concept like 'the mercy of Allah', do you automatically assume that your mental image corresponds exactly to the external reality? How might the second interpretation in this lesson challenge the way you relate your thoughts to divine attributes? What would change if you consciously considered your mental concepts as neutral, allowing for both true and false applications?
?
Think of a belief you hold about the unseen world (e.g., about the afterlife or angels). Are you willing to admit that your mental judgment about it is an application of your intellect, not a necessary consequence of the mental concept itself? If the second interpretation is correct, how would that affect your level of certainty or humility regarding your convictions?
?
Have you ever confused your mental conception of something with the thing itself, assuming that because it makes sense in your mind, it must be true externally? This lesson suggests that the intellect can apply predicates even if they are false. Reflect on a specific instance where you might have done this. How can you train yourself to distinguish between the mental existence of an idea and its external reality?

The Intellect's Judgment on the Quiddity
Explains the intellect's ability to judge quiddity with external predicates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intellect can apply external predicates to the quiddity considered without condition, and the two interpretations of 'judgments' in this context.

Core Idea
The intellect judges the quiddity itself, not the mental image.

This passage refines our understanding of how the intellect applies external predicates. The commentator clarifies that the subject of such judgment is the quiddity without condition—the essence considered in itself—not the mental existent or the external particular. Two interpretations of 'judgments' are then compared, with the preferred one aligning with the intellect's ability to predicate externally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of judgment
Read the passage and find the phrase that defines what the intellect judges. Notice that it is 'the quiddity without condition' (الماهية لا بشرط شيء).
This is the key to understanding the rest.
2
Understand the objection and response
The commentator addresses a potential confusion: if the quiddity is not externally existent, then the judgment might seem to apply to individuals. See how he resolves this by distinguishing between the universal concept and external particulars.
3
Compare the two interpretations of 'judgments'
The passage presents two ways to understand 'judgments' (أحكام) in the author's phrase. Identify each interpretation and note why the second is considered more suitable.
This involves the distinction between the act of judging and the thing judged.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ويمكن للعقل إلخ) وذلك لأن المحكوم عليه بالأحكام الخارجية الماهية لا بشرط شيء وهي ملحوظة قصداً فيمكن الحكم عليه بها ، وإن كان عروض تلك العوارض باعتبار الوجود الخارجي . ليس بموجود فيه كان ذلك حكماً على ما صدق عليه من الأفراد، وإلا فلا اشتباه أن الموجود في الخارج هو الأشخاص لا المفهوم الكلي. قوله: (ويمكن للعقل أن يجري عليه أحكاماً (خارجية فالأنسب أن الأحكام في عبارة المصنف على هذا التوجيه بمعناها الظاهر، ووصفها بالخارجية باعتبار تعلقها بالمحمولات التي تعرض باعتبار الوجود الخارجي، وباعتبار الوجود الخارجي وأما على التوجيه الأول فبمعنى المحكوم به، وهي الأحوال التي تكون لها في الخارج. قوله : ( وهذا الاحتمال أنسب بقوله إلخ) وجه الانسبية أن الحكم في هذا القول بمعنى
His statement: (And the intellect can, etc.) That is because the subject of external judgments is the quiddity without condition, and it is intentionally observed, so it is possible to judge it by them, even if the occurrence of those accidents is by consideration of external existence. It is not existent in it; that would be a judgment on what it applies to from individuals, otherwise there is no doubt that what exists externally is particulars, not the universal concept. His statement: (And the intellect can apply external judgments to it) so the more suitable is that 'judgments' in the author's phrase according to this direction are in their apparent meaning, and their description as external is by consideration of their connection to predicates that occur by consideration of external existence, and by consideration of external existence. As for the first direction, it is in the meaning of the thing judged, which are the states that occur to it externally. His statement: (And this probability is more suitable to his statement, etc.) The aspect of suitability is that 'judgment' in this statement means...
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the two interpretations, focusing on the intellect's ability to judge. The commentator explains that when we say 'the intellect can apply external judgments,' the subject of such judgments is the quiddity considered without condition (لا بشرط شيء)—that is, the quiddity in its essence, not as existing in the mind or externally. This quiddity is directly observed by the intellect, so it can be predicated of it, even though the attributes (like blackness) properly belong to external existence. The commentator clarifies an objection: if the quiddity is not externally existent in the thing, then the judgment would be on the individuals that fall under it, but that's not the case; external existence belongs to particulars, not the universal concept. Then he refines the term 'judgments' (أحكام) in the two directions: on the second interpretation (the one preferred), 'judgments' means the predicates in their usual sense, described as 'external' because they relate to predicates that arise from external existence. On the first interpretation, 'judgments' means the thing judged—i.e., the actual states that the object has externally. The suitability of the second interpretation is that 'judgment' in the original text is used in a sense consistent with the intellect's application of predicates.

Key Terms

ماهية لا بشرط
أحكام خارجية
مفهوم كلي
أفراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the subject of external judgments is the mental image or the external particular.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the subject is the quiddity without condition (الماهية لا بشرط), not the mental existent or the external individual.

Key Takeaway
The intellect can judge the quiddity itself, unqualified by existence, and the term 'judgments' is best understood as the act of predicating external attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If external judgments are about the quiddity, not individuals, then how should this affect your habit of judging others' actions? Commit to judging the act itself, not the person, and repent for any judgments you have made against individuals.

First Interpretation: Correspondence and External Judgments
Explains the first interpretation of 'consideration' with correspondence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first interpretation of 'consideration' in relation to mental existents, where external judgments attach due to correspondence with external reality.

Core Idea
When a mental existent corresponds to external reality, external judgments attach to it.

Al-Ījī and al-Jurjānī discuss how mental concepts relate to the external world. The first interpretation of 'consideration' focuses on correspondence: the mental existent carries judgments that belong to its external counterpart. This lesson unpacks that view, showing why the quiddity's external existence makes those judgments applicable.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once to get the gist, then compare with the translation to ensure understanding.
Pay attention to the terms 'اعتبار المطابقة' and 'أحكام خارجية'.
2
Identify the meaning of 'consideration of correspondence'
Notice that the mental existent is considered as a representation of something external. This is the key to the first interpretation.
3
Understand why external judgments attach
External judgments like blackness attach because the quiddity has those properties in external reality; they do not occur in the mind alone.
The commentator uses the term 'تلحقه' (attach) to indicate occurrence.
4
Recognize the gloss on 'attachment'
The commentary clarifies that 'attachment' here means 'occurrence' ('urud), and external judgments are predicates that characterize things externally.
This is the apparent meaning of the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تكون تلك الماهية موجودة في الخارج، وبهذا الاعتبار (أي باعتبار المطابقة) تلحقه (أي ذلك الموجود الذهني) الأحكام الخارجية (من السواد والبياض والحركة والسكون ونظائرها) فإن الماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج لم تخل من أمور تعرض لها بحسب هذا الوجود وتختص به فلا تكون عارضة لها حال كونها موجودة في الذهن. ويحتمل أن يراد بهذا الاعتبار: اعتبار المطابقة واللامطابقة، على معنى الموجود الذهني بمجرد حصوله فيه ملحوظ من حيث هو هو، ومن هذه الحيثية يجوز أن يكون له مطابق في الخارج وأن لا يكون، ويمكن للعقل أن يجري عليه أحكاماً خارجية صادقة أو كاذبة، وهذا الاحتمال أنسب بقوله: (وأما من حيث هو موجود) في الذهن (فلا حكم له) أي لا يمكن للعقل أن يحكم عليه من هذه الحيثية، إلا بأن يتصور مرة ثانية من حيث أنه في الذهن، فيحكم عليه بأحكام أخر مخالفة للأحكام. قوله: (أي باعتبار المطابقة) أي باعتبار أنه موجود في الخارج. قوله: (فإن الماهية إلخ) فعلى هذا التوجيه اللحوق بمعنى العروض، والأحكام الخارجية بمعنى المحمولات التي تتصف بها الأشياء في الخارج، وهو الظاهر المتبادر من الحجارة، ولذا قدمه. وعلى التوجيه الثاني يكون اللحوق عبارة عن إجراء العقل عليه تلك المحمولات سواء كانت صادقة أو كاذبة يعني باعتبار صحة اتصافه بالمطابقة واللامطابقة وهو اعتباره من حيث هو، فإن الماهية لا بشرط شيء يمكن أن يوجد وأن لا يوجد، يمكن للعقل إجراء المحمولات الخارجية عليها صادقة كانت أو كاذبة بعد الإجراء وهذا التوجيه وإن كان صرفاً للحوق عن المعنى المتبادر، لكنه أنسب بقوله: وأما من حيث هو موجود في الذهن فلا حكم له، إذ معناه لا يمكن للعقل أن يجري عليه حكماً، لا أنه لا يعرض له حكم، فإن الأمر الموجود في الذهن له عوارض ذهنية، وإن لم يحكم العقل بها عليه مرة، وقوله: ومحصول الكلام أي على التوجيه الثاني. قوله: (ويمكن للعقل إلخ) وذلك لأن المحكوم عليه بالأحكام الخارجية الماهية لا بشرط شيء وهي ملحوظة قصداً فيمكن الحكم عليه بها، وإن كان عروض تلك العوارض باعتبار الوجود الخارجي.
That quiddity exists in external reality, and by this consideration (i.e., by consideration of correspondence) the external judgments (like blackness, whiteness, motion, rest, and the like) attach to it (i.e., to that mental existent), for when the quiddity exists in external reality it is not devoid of things that occur to it according to this existence and are specific to it, so they do not occur to it while it is existing in the mind. And it is possible that what is meant by this consideration is the consideration of correspondence and non-correspondence, meaning that the mental existent, by the mere fact of its occurrence in the mind, is considered in itself; and from this perspective it may have a correspondent in external reality or not, and the intellect can apply to it external judgments, true or false. And this probability is more suitable to his statement: 'And as for it being existent in the mind, it has no judgment', i.e., the intellect cannot judge it from this perspective, except by conceptualizing it a second time from the perspective that it is in the mind, so it judges it with other judgments contrary to the judgments. His statement: (i.e., by consideration of correspondence) meaning by consideration that it exists in external reality. His statement: (for the quiddity etc.) So according to this interpretation, 'attachment' means occurrence, and 'external judgments' means the predicates that things are characterized by in external reality, and this is the apparent and obvious meaning, and therefore he mentioned it first. And according to the second interpretation, 'attachment' is the intellect's application of those predicates to it, whether they are true or false, meaning by consideration of the validity of its being characterized by correspondence and non-correspondence, which is considering it in itself; for the quiddity, unconditioned by anything, can exist and not exist; the intellect can apply external predicates to it, true or false, after application. And this interpretation, although it removes 'attachment' from its apparent meaning, is more suitable to his statement: 'And as for it being existent in the mind, it has no judgment', because its meaning is that the intellect cannot apply a judgment to it, not that no judgment occurs to it, for the thing existing in the mind has mental properties, even if the intellect does not judge them upon it once. And his statement: 'and the gist of the discourse' means according to the second interpretation. His statement: (and the intellect can etc.) And that is because that which is judged by external judgments is the quiddity unconditioned by anything, and it is considered intentionally, so it can be judged by them, even if the occurrence of those properties is by consideration of external existence.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory discussion of two possible interpretations of 'consideration' (i'tibar) regarding the mental existent. The commentator first presents the first interpretation: the mental existent is considered in terms of its correspondence (mutabaqa) to external reality, meaning that external judgments (like blackness, whiteness, etc.) attach to it because it represents something that exists externally. He explains that when the quiddity exists externally, it has properties that are specific to that external existence, so those properties do not occur to it while it is merely in the mind. Then he presents the second interpretation: the mental existent is considered purely in itself (min haythu huwa huwa), without regard to correspondence. In this case, the intellect can apply external judgments (true or false) to it because it may or may not have a match in external reality. The commentator notes that this second interpretation is more suitable (ansab) to the base text's statement that 'as for it being existent in the mind, it has no judgment', meaning the intellect cannot judge it from that perspective directly; to judge it, one must conceptualize it a second time as a mental existent. The commentator then explains the glosses on the first interpretation, clarifying that 'attachment' (luhuq) in that sense means occurrence ('urud), and external judgments are predicates that things are characterized by in external reality. For the second interpretation, 'attachment' means the intellect's application of those predicates. He concludes that the second interpretation, though it removes 'attachment' from its apparent meaning, is more appropriate because it aligns with the base text's statement that the mental existent has no judgment (i.e., no judgment is applied by the intellect), not that no judgment occurs to it (since mental existents do have mental properties). The passage also glosses a phrase from the base text, emphasizing that the quiddity when considered unconditioned (la bi-shart shay') is the object of judgment and can receive external predicates, even if those predicates occur by virtue of external existence.

Key Terms

مطابقة
ماهية
موجود ذهني
أحكام خارجية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation, why do external judgments attach to the mental existent?
A) Because the mental existent has no intrinsic properties.
B) Because the mental existent corresponds to an external reality.
C) Because the intellect imposes them.
D) Because the mental existent is itself external.

Key Takeaway
The first interpretation holds that external judgments attach to mental existents due to their correspondence to external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you form a belief in your mind, do you tend to assume it corresponds to reality without checking? How willing are you to subject your most certain inner convictions to the test of external evidence?
?
The lesson says that external judgments do not attach to quiddity while it is merely mental. Reflect on a time you realized a mental image or assumption did not match the way things actually are. How did that feel, and what did you learn about the gap between your thoughts and reality?
?
If your mental constructs only gain validity through correspondence with external reality, what does that demand of you regarding your understanding of God, truth, and your own knowledge? Are you ready to examine honestly which of your mental judgments are merely internal and which are grounded in reality?

Second Interpretation: The Mental Existent in Itself
Explains the second interpretation of 'consideration' with the mental existent in itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second interpretation of 'consideration', where the mental existent is considered in itself, allowing the intellect to apply external judgments true or false.

Core Idea
Considered in itself, the mental existent permits the intellect to apply external judgments regardless of correspondence.

The commentator now presents a more nuanced interpretation: the mental existent can be considered purely in itself (min haythu huwa huwa), without regard to whether it matches anything outside. From this perspective, the intellect can apply external judgments to it, and these may be true or false. This interpretation is deemed more suitable to the base text's claim that a mental existent 'has no judgment', meaning the intellect does not directly judge it.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Focus on the distinction between considering the mental existent in itself versus considering it as corresponding to something external.
Note the phrase 'محلوق من حيث هو هو'.
2
Understand the second interpretation
Here, the mental existent is considered in its own essence. The intellect can apply external judgments, but they may be true or false depending on correspondence.
3
Grasp why this interpretation is more suitable
The base text says the mental existent 'has no judgment' – this is interpreted as the intellect not applying a judgment directly, not that the mental existent lacks attributes.
The commentator clarifies that mental existents have mental properties (عوارض ذهنية).
4
Distinguish between 'occurrence' and 'application'
In the second interpretation, 'attachment' (لحوق) means the intellect's application of predicates, not actual occurrence. The quiddity unconditioned (لا بشرط شيء) can receive both true and false judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

That quiddity exists in external reality, and by this consideration (i.e., by consideration of correspondence) the external judgments (like blackness, whiteness, motion, rest, and the like) attach to it (i.e., to that mental existent), for when the quiddity exists in external reality it is not devoid of things that occur to it according to this existence and are specific to it, so they do not occur to it while it is existing in the mind. His statement: (i.e., by consideration of correspondence) meaning by consideration that it exists in external reality. His statement: (for the quiddity etc.) So according to this interpretation, 'attachment' means occurrence, and 'external judgments' means the predicates that things are characterized by in external reality, and this is the apparent and obvious meaning, and therefore he mentioned it first. And according to the second interpretation, 'attachment' is the intellect's application of those predicates to it, whether they are true or false, meaning by consideration of the validity of its being characterized by correspondence and non-correspondence, which is considering it in itself; for the quiddity, unconditioned by anything, can exist and not exist; the intellect can apply external predicates to it, true or false, after application. Commentary
This passage is an explanatory discussion of two possible interpretations of 'consideration' (i'tibar) regarding the mental existent. The commentator first presents the first interpretation: the mental existent is considered in terms of its correspondence (mutabaqa) to external reality, meaning that external judgments (like blackness, whiteness, etc.) attach to it because it represents something that exists externally. He explains that when the quiddity exists externally, it has properties that are specific to that external existence, so those properties do not occur to it while it is merely in the mind. Then he presents the second interpretation: the mental existent is considered purely in itself (min haythu huwa huwa), without regard to correspondence. In this case, the intellect can apply external judgments (true or false) to it because it may or may not have a match in external reality. The commentator then explains the glosses on the first interpretation, clarifying that 'attachment' (luhuq) in that sense means occurrence ('urud), and external judgments are predicates that things are characterized by in external reality. For the second interpretation, 'attachment' means the intellect's application of those predicates. He concludes that the second interpretation, though it removes 'attachment' from its apparent meaning, is more appropriate because it aligns with the base text's statement that the mental existent has no judgment (i.e., no judgment is applied by the intellect), not that no judgment occurs to it (since mental existents do have mental properties). Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'no judgment' means the mental existent has no attributes at all.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that 'no judgment' means the intellect does not apply a judgment from that perspective, not that the mental existent lacks mental properties. In fact, mental existents have mental properties (عوارض ذهنية).

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say the second interpretation is more suitable to the statement 'as for it being existent in the mind, it has no judgment'?
A) Because the mental existent has no properties at all.
B) Because the intellect cannot apply a judgment to the mental existent from that perspective, though it has mental properties.
C) Because the mental existent is a perfect copy of the external existent.
D) Because the intellect can only apply judgments to external existents.

Key Takeaway
The second interpretation allows the intellect to apply external judgments (true or false) to the mental existent considered in itself, and better explains why the base text says the mental existent has no judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever made a judgment about something in your mind—a memory, a concept, or an assumption—and treated it as if it were external reality, only to later discover your judgment was false? How will you use this lesson's distinction between the mental existent in itself and external correspondence to become more honest with yourself today about the difference between your mental constructs and reality? Choose one area where you often confuse the two and commit to verifying it against external reality before acting.
?
This lesson teaches that the intellect can apply external judgments to the quiddity unconditioned, and those judgments can be true or false. Consider one of your deeply held beliefs about yourself, others, or Allah. Is it a judgment applied to a mental construct? Are you willing to examine that belief critically, knowing that it could be false? What would it cost you to let go of that belief if it is false? Commit to re-examining that specific belief in a specific way (e.g., by reading a relevant source, asking a trusted person, or testing it in action) by the end of this week.
?
The passage says the mental existent 'has no judgment' until the intellect deliberately considers it a second time. In your daily life, how often do you react to your own thoughts and mental images as if they carried automatic truth, without that second, deliberate consideration? Identify one specific recurring thought (e.g., 'I am not good enough' or 'That person dislikes me') that you now realize you have been treating as a given judgment. What is one concrete step you will take to pause, consider that thought as a mental existent in itself, and then apply an external judgment with truth-seeking honesty?

Rational Judgments
