The Distinction Between External and Mental Judgments
Summarizes the distinction between external and mental judgments and the need for a second mental act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mental judgments require a second mental act of conceptualization, and the special status of the quiddity's necessary properties.

Core Idea
To judge something as mental, you must first see it as mental.

This passage from a classical commentary on logic clarifies a subtle point: judgments about things in the mind (like universality) are not the same as judgments about things in external reality (like writing). The author explains that to apply mental predicates, the object must be consciously considered as existing in the mind, requiring a second act of conceptualization. This distinction is crucial for understanding second intelligibles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The passage begins by explaining why the second interpretation of 'judgment' (as external judgment) is more suitable. Recognize that 'judgment' here refers to second intelligibles like universality, particularity, etc.
This sets the stage for the main discussion.
2
Distinguish two types of accidents
Identify the two types: external accidents (which occur to the quiddity in external existence) and mental accidents (which occur only to the mental existent as such). Understand that the quiddity in the mind can receive both, but differently.
External accidents can be applied to the quiddity without a second act, but mental accidents require a second conceptualization.
3
Grasp the need for second conceptualization
Focus on the reasoning: the intellect's judgment on something is based on an intentional consideration of that thing. Mental accidents pertain to the mental existent qua mental, so one must first conceptualize the mental existent intentionally as mental to apply them.
The example of 'man is a writer' illustrates an external judgment applied to the quiddity unconditioned.
4
Recognize the exception
Note that the necessities (lawazim) of the quiddity as such occur in both existence and mind, so they can be judged in either consideration without a second act. This exception confirms the rule.
The commentator stresses that these are not separable from the quiddity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وهذا الاحتمال أنسب بقوله إلخ) وجه الانسبية أن الحكم في هذا القول بمعنى الخارجية، كالكلية والجزئية والذاتية والعرضية والجنسية والفصلية إلى غير ذلك من أشباهها، ويسمى مثل ذلك معقولات ثانية، ومحصول الكلام أن الماهية إذا وجدت في الذهن كانت ملحوظة في نفسها وصالحة لأن يحكم عليها بأمور لا تعرض لها إلا في الخارج وهي المسماة بالعوارض الخارجية، وغير صالحة لأن يحكم عليها بأمور لا تعرض لها إلا في الذهن بل لا بد لهذا الحكم من تصورها مرة ثانية ليلاحظ عروض هذه العوارض لها فيحكم بها عليها، وأما لوازم الماهية من حيث هي هي عارضة لها في الوجودين فيصح أن يحكم بها عليها في كل واحدة من الملاحظتين، وإنما قوله: (إلا) بأن يتصور مرة ثانية إلخ لأن المحكوم عليه الموجود الذهني من حيث وجوده فيه، وحكم العقل على شيء باعتبار من الاعتبارات فرع تصوره بذلك الاعتبار قصداً، لأن النفس مجبولة على أن لا يحكم على شيء إلا بعد تصوره ذلك الشيء قصداً. قوله: (وصالحة لأن يحكم عليها إلخ) لما عرفت أن المحكوم عليه بها هي الماهية لا بشرط شيء، وهي ملحوظة قصداً، وإن كان في عروضها مدخل للوجود الخارجي، ألا ترى أن الحكم على الإنسان بأنه كاتب هو على الإنسان من حيث هو لا الإنسان الموجود في الخارج، وإن كان اتصافه به مشروطاً بوجوده بخلاف العوارض الذهنية، فإن المحكوم عليه بها هو الموجود الذهني من حيث وجوده فيه، فلا يمكن الحكم بها عليه إلا بعد ملاحظته قصداً من حيث أنه موجود في الذهن فتدبر، فإنه السر في الفرق بينهما فقد زل فيه أقدام بعض الناظرين. قوله: (وأما لوازم إلخ) اختار لفظ اللوازم وإن كان الظاهر عوارض الماهية إشارة إلى أنها لا تكون مفارقة. قوله: (عارضة لها في الوجودين المحققين أو المقدرين فيدخل فيه لوازم الماهية التي لا حكم العقل قطعاً، فحسن المقابلة يقتضي أن يكون الحكم فيما سبق بمعناه أيضاً، وقد عرفت من السياق أن حمل الحكم على حكم العقل إنما يناسب الاحتمال الثاني فتأمل.
His statement: (And this probability is more suitable to his statement etc.) The aspect of suitability is that 'judgment' in this saying means external judgments, such as universality, particularity, essentiality, accidentally, generality, specificity, and the like, which are called second intelligibles. And the gist of the discourse is that when the quiddity occurs in the mind, it is considered in itself and is fit to be judged by things that only occur to it in external reality, which are called external accidents; and it is not fit to be judged by things that only occur to it in the mind, rather this judgment requires conceptualizing it a second time to observe the occurrence of these accidents to it and then judge by them. As for the necessities of the quiddity in itself, they occur to it in both existence, so it is correct to judge by them upon it in each of the two considerations. And his statement: (except by conceptualizing it a second time etc.) because that which is judged is the mental existent from the perspective of its existence in the mind, and the intellect's judgment of something according to a consideration is a branch of conceptualizing it with that consideration intentionally, for the soul is disposed not to judge anything except after conceptualizing that thing intentionally. His statement: (and is fit to be judged upon etc.) because you know that that which is judged by them is the quiddity unconditioned by anything, and it is considered intentionally, even if external existence has a role in their occurrence. Do you not see that the judgment that man is a writer is upon man in himself, not upon man existing externally, even if his characterization by it is conditioned by his existence, unlike mental accidents? For that which is judged by them is the mental existent from the perspective of its existence in the mind, so it cannot be judged by them except after an intentional observation of it from the perspective that it is in the mind. So reflect, for this is the secret of the difference between them, and some observers have slipped in it. His statement: (And as for necessities etc.) He chose the term 'necessities' although the apparent term would be 'accidents of the quiddity', indicating that they are not separable. His statement: (occurring to it in both realized or estimated existence) so it includes the necessities of the quiddity that are not [subject to] the intellect's judgment absolutely. So the suitability of the contrast requires that 'judgment' in the preceding also be in its meaning, and you have known from the context that interpreting 'judgment' as the intellect's judgment is only appropriate for the second probability, so reflect.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a summary and further explanation of the distinction between external and mental judgments. It begins by explaining why the second interpretation is more suitable: because 'judgment' in the base text refers to external judgments, which are 'second intelligibles' (ma'qulat thaniya) like universality, particularity, etc. The gist (muhassal) is that when the quiddity exists in the mind, it is considered in itself and can receive judgments about things that only occur in external reality (external accidents), but to receive judgments about things that only occur in the mind (mental accidents), it must be conceptualized a second time as a mental existent. The commentator then addresses an apparent exception: the necessities (lawazim) of the quiddity as such occur to it in both modes of existence, so they can be judged in either consideration without a second act. He then explains the need for a second conceptualization: the intellect's judgment on something is based on an intentional consideration of that thing, and since mental judgments require the thing to be considered precisely as mental, a second act is needed. He illustrates this with the example of 'man is a writer': the judgment is on man in himself, not on the external individual, even though writing depends on external existence. In contrast, mental accidents (like universality) are properties of the mental existent as such, so they require focusing on the mental existent intentionally. He warns that some have slipped in this subtle point. Finally, he notes that the author chose 'necessities' to indicate inseparability, and that the contrast requires 'judgment' to be understood consistently as the intellect's judgment, which aligns with the second interpretation.

Key Terms
معقولات ثانية
عوارض خارجية
عوارض ذهنية
لوازم الماهية
ملاحظة قصدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that external and mental judgments apply to the same object in the same way, without requiring different considerations.
Correction: The passage shows that external judgments apply to the quiddity unconditioned, while mental judgments require the quiddity to be considered intentionally as a mental existent, necessitating a second act of conceptualization.

Checkpoint
According to the gist of the discourse, why do mental judgments require conceptualizing the mental existent a second time?
A) Because the mental existent does not exist in reality.
B) Because mental accidents only occur to the mental existent when it is considered intentionally as a mental existent.
C) Because the quiddity cannot be judged at all in the mind.
D) Because the intellect cannot judge without an external object.

Key Takeaway
Mental judgments require a second act of conceptualization because they are about the object precisely as a mental existent, not about the quiddity unconditioned.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you judge ideas, people, or situations solely based on your mental constructs of them, without considering the distinction between their external reality and your internal representation? When was the last time you realized you were treating a mental category (like 'universality') as if it were a concrete external property? What would it take for you to intentionally observe your own mental acts before passing judgment?
?
This lesson teaches that mental judgments require a second act of conscious attention. In your daily life, are there areas where you habitually confuse your mental conceptions with external reality—for example, assuming that because something is 'true in your mind' it must be true externally? Identify one such area and commit to pausing to separate the two before acting.
?
The commentator warns that 'some observers have slipped' on this subtle point. Where in your own thinking have you slipped by mixing up the conditions for judgment? Are there beliefs you hold about yourself or others that you have never examined as mental accidents (concepts in your mind) versus external facts? Write down one such belief and challenge it using the distinction from this passage.

The Mirror Analogy: Why Mental Judgments Need a Second Act
Explains the need for a second consideration of the mental existent using a mirror analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why judgments about mental concomitants require a second consideration of the quiddity, using the mirror analogy.

Core Idea
The quiddity is a mirror: to judge the mirror itself, you must look again.

Sanusi addresses an important subtlety: why do we need to reconsider the quiddity when making mental judgments, but not when making external ones? He anticipates an objection and answers with a striking analogy of a mirror. This lesson unpacks that analogy and its implications for how we understand mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: an objection, an answer, an analogy, and a conclusion.
The passage is dense; read slowly to catch the logical flow.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on technical terms like 'mental concomitants', 'external concomitants', 'intentional observation', and 'secondary observation'. Understand how they function in the argument.
These terms are crucial for grasping the distinction.
3
Analyze the mirror analogy
Map each part of the analogy: the mirror represents the quiddity in the mind, the reflected object represents external individuals, and the judgment of the mirror's properties represents mental judgments.
Reflect on why you cannot judge the mirror while focusing on the reflection.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion states that mental judgments require a second conceptualization of the mental existent. Connect this back to the mirror: to judge the mirror, you must turn your attention to it again.
This solidifies the need for a 'second act' in mental judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ومحصول الكلام أن الماهية إلخ فإن قلت: ما السر في أن الماهية إذا حكم عليها باللواحق الذهنية تحتاج إلى ملاحظتها ثانيا، وإذا حكم عليها باللواحق الخارجية لم يحتج إليها، بل يكفي ملاحظتها ابتداء من حيث هي؟ قلت: السر فيه أن الحكم على الشيء يستدعي التوجه إليه وملاحظته قصداً، فإذا جزدت الماهية عن التشخصات وحصلت في الذهن كانت مرآة يشاهد بها الهويات، وكان المتوجه إليه حينئذ تلك الهويات، فيمكن الحكم عليها باللواحق الخارجية التي تعرض لها، ولا يمكن في هذه الملاحظة أن يحكم على نفس الماهية بشيء، لأنها ملحوظة تبعاً، وهذا كما أنك إذا نظرت إلى المرآة لتعرف حال المرئي، أمكنك الحكم عليه بأنه حسن أو قبيح، ولا يمكنك أن تحكم حينئذ على المرآة بأنها مستوية الأجزاء، أو فيها حشوش أو نحوهما، بل يحتاج بهذا الحكم إلى توجه مستأنف إلى المرآة نفسها، وهذا ظاهر بالوجدان، وبهذا التحقيق يظهر أن الواجب في الحكم باللواحق الذهنية تصور الحاصل في الذهن مرة ثانية
His statement: (And the gist of the discourse is that the quiddity etc.) For if you say: What is the secret that when the quiddity is judged by mental concomitants it needs to be considered a second time, but when it is judged by external concomitants it does not need that, rather its initial consideration in itself suffices? I say: The secret in it is that judgment upon a thing requires attention to it and an intentional observation. So when the quiddity is stripped of individuations and occurs in the mind, it becomes a mirror by which the identities (huwiyyat) are observed, and the attention at that time is to those identities. So it is possible to judge them by external concomitants that occur to them, but it is not possible in this observation to judge the quiddity itself by anything, because it is observed secondarily. This is like when you look at a mirror to know the state of what is reflected; you can judge it as beautiful or ugly, but you cannot at that time judge the mirror as being uniform in parts, or having blemishes, etc. Rather, this judgment requires a renewed attention to the mirror itself. And this is evident by direct experience, and by this investigation it becomes clear that what is necessary in judging by mental concomitants is to conceptualize what is obtained in the mind a second time.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The commentator anticipates an objection: why do judgments of mental concomitants require a second consideration, while judgments of external concomitants do not? The answer uses a powerful analogy. The secret is that to judge something, one must intentionally attend to it. When the quiddity is abstracted from individuations and present in the mind, it acts as a mirror through which we observe external individuals (huwiyyat). In this first observation, our attention is on those external individuals, so we can judge them (e.g., as beautiful or ugly) using external predicates (which apply to them). But we cannot at the same time judge the quiddity (the mirror itself) because it is only observed secondarily (taba'an). To judge the quiddity's mental properties (e.g., universality, particularity), we must refocus our attention on the quiddity itself, i.e., consider it a second time as a mental existent. The analogy of a mirror is clear: when you look at a mirror to see the reflection, you can judge the reflected object, but to judge the mirror's own qualities (like smoothness or defects), you must turn your attention to the mirror itself. This is evident by direct experience (wijdan). The conclusion is that for mental judgments, one must conceptualize the mental existent a second time.

Key Terms
لواحق ذهنية
لواحق خارجية
ملاحظة قصدية
ملحوظة تبعاً
مرآة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The quiddity in the mind is directly perceived as a subject for judgment.
Correction: The analogy shows the quiddity is observed secondarily when used as a mirror for individuals; to judge it directly, one must attend to it anew.

Checkpoint
In the mirror analogy, what does the mirror itself represent?
A) The external individual.
B) The mental quiddity stripped of individuations.
C) The intellect's judgment.
D) The mental accident.

Key Takeaway
Mental judgments require a second conceptualization of the mental existent because the quiddity is initially observed only secondarily when used as a mirror for external individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you judge your own beliefs — for example, 'This is true' — do you pause to consider that the belief itself is a mental existent (the mirror) that needs a second look? What habit might you change to ensure you are not confusing the reflection (the external object) for the mirror (your mental construct)?
?
Think of a recent time you made a hasty judgment about your own thought process or a deeply held conviction. How might a 'second look' — intentionally redirecting your attention to the mental state itself — have prevented an error or deepened your understanding? Commit to one specific situation this week where you will consciously apply this lesson.
?
The mirror analogy reveals that we often observe the quiddity only secondarily when our minds are focused on external things. In your daily life, does your self-awareness take a backseat to awareness of events and people around you? What would it look like to intentionally turn attention to your own mental states as primary subjects, not just as windows to the world?

First Objection to Knowledge as Mental Existence and Its Answer
Objection and answer regarding knowledge as mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first objection against knowledge as mental existence fails and how the distinction between mental and external existence resolves it.

Core Idea
Mental forms are not external things; they lack physical properties.

This lesson examines the first major objection raised by theologians against the theory that knowledge is mental existence. The objection claims absurd consequences, but the response clarifies a crucial distinction. Understanding this exchange is key to grasping the nature of mental existence in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the objection's conditional structure: 'if knowledge is mental existence, then...'
2
Identify the objection's logical structure
The objection argues that if knowledge consisted of the quiddity obtaining in the mind, then conceiving black and white would require both quiddities present, making the mind black and white—an absurdity. It also notes that opposites would coexist.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the answer's distinction
The answer distinguishes between external identity (huwiyya) and mental form (ṣūra 'aqliyya). Only external identities have physical properties; mental forms do not. So the mind does not become black or white, and opposites do not coexist in a contradictory way.
The key is the difference between al-wujūd al-‘aynī and al-wujūd al-ẓillī.
4
Reflect on why the alternative answer is rejected
The commentator mentions a weaker answer (locative vs. attributive) and dismisses it because obtaining a form in the mind does entail an attribute (knowledge). This reinforces the strength of the main answer.
This step is optional but deepens understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال المتكلمون : هو ( أي كون العلم عبارة عن الوجود الذهني ) باطل لوجهين : الأول : لو كان التعقل بحصول ماهية المعقول ) في ذهن العاقل ( فمن عقل السواد والبياض ) وحكم بتضادهما ( يكون قد حصل في ذهنه السواد والبياض فيكون الذهن أسود وأبيض)، إذ لا معنى للأسود والأبيض إلا ما حصل فيه ماهية السواد والبياض، لكنه باطل قطعاً، لأن هذه الصفات منتفية عنه، ( وأيضاً يجتمع الضدان ) في محل واحد وهو سفسطة. (وجواب) الوجه الأول أنه إنما يلزم كون الذهن أبيض وأسود لو حصل فيه هوية السواد والبياض ) أي ماهيتهما الموجودة بالوجود العيني المسمى بالوجود الخارجي، الذي هو مصدر للآثار الخارجية ومظهر للأحكام، لا ماهيتهما) الموجودة بالوجود الظلي المسمى بالوجود الذهني ( إذ قد علمت في مباحث الوجود الذهني ( أنه لا معنى للماهية إلا الصورة العقلية ) المتصفة بوجود غير أصيل، (و) علمت أيضا ( أنها ) أي الصورة العقلية ) مخالفة للهويات الخارجية ) المتصفة بوجودات أصيلة (في (اللوازم التي تكون للموجود الخارجي باعتبار خصوصية مدخل فيها، كما تنبهت له من قبل وكون المحل أسود وأبيض وكذلك التضاد من قبيل ما للوجود الخارجي مدخل فيه فلا يلزم اتصاف الذهن بما هو منتف عنه قطعا، ولا اجتماع الضدين. قوله : ( وجواب الأول (الخ وقد يجاب بالفرق بين الحصول في الذهن والحصول في المحل بأن الأول ظرفي. والثاني اتصافي وليس بشيء لأن حصول الصورة في الذهن يوجب الاتصاف بكونه عالماً . نعم يتم ذلك لو كان العلم غير الصورة الحاصلة.
And the theologians said: It (i.e., that knowledge is mental existence) is false for two reasons. First: If intellection occurred by obtaining the quiddity of the intelligible in the mind of the intelligent, then whoever understands black and white and judges them to be opposites would have obtained black and white in his mind, so the mind would be black and white—since there is no meaning of 'black' and 'white' except that in which the quiddity of black and white obtains. But this is absolutely false because these attributes are negated of it, and also the two opposites would exist in one place, which is sophistry. The answer to the first reason: This would only entail that the mind is white and black if the identity of black and white obtained in it—i.e., their quiddities existing in real existence called external existence, which is the source of external effects and the locus of judgments—not their quiddities existing in shadowy existence called mental existence. For you have learned in the discussions of mental existence that there is no meaning of quiddity except the mental form characterized by non-principal existence, and you have also learned that it (i.e., the mental form) is different from external identities characterized by principal existences, in the necessary concomitants that belong to the external existent by virtue of the particularity of its inclusion, as you have been warned before. So the locus being black and white, and likewise the conjunction of opposites, are among things in which external existence has a role, so it does not entail that the mind is attributed with what is certainly negated of it, nor the conjunction of opposites. His saying: (Answer to the first, etc.) And it may be answered by the distinction between obtaining in the mind and obtaining in a substrate: the first is spatial (ẓarfī) and the second is attributive (ittiṣāfī). But this is nothing because obtaining a form in the mind necessitates being described as knowing. However, this would be valid if knowledge were other than the form obtained.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection raised by the theologians (al-mutakallimūn) against the view that knowledge is mental existence (al-wujūd al-dhihnī). The objection argues that if knowledge consisted of the quiddity of the known obtaining in the mind, then when one conceives black and white, both quiddities would be present in the mind, making the mind itself black and white—an absurdity. Moreover, opposites would coexist in the same subject, which is sophistry. The answer distinguishes between external existence (al-wujūd al-‘aynī) and mental existence (al-wujūd al-ẓillī). The objection only holds if the actual external identities (huwiyyāt) of black and white were present, but in the mind we have only mental forms (ṣuwar ‘aqliyya) that lack the external effects (āthār) and concomitants (lawāzim) such as being black or being opposite. The mind is not a physical substrate; it does not become colored or hold opposites spatially. The commentary then mentions another possible answer differentiating 'obtaining in the mind' (al-ḥuṣūl fī al-dhihn) as merely locative (ẓarfī) versus 'obtaining in a substrate' (al-ḥuṣūl fī al-maḥall) as attributive (ittiṣāfī). However, the commentator dismisses this alternative because obtaining a form in the mind does entail an attribute—knowledge—so the locative/attributive distinction does not fully resolve the problem, unless knowledge were a separate entity from the form. The main answer already provided is thus more robust.

Key Terms
الوجود الذهني
الوجود الخارجي
الهوية
الصورة العقلية
التضاد
اللوازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a quiddity is present in the mind, it must have the same properties as when it exists externally.
Correction: The text shows that mental existence is 'shadowy' (ẓillī) and does not carry the physical concomitants (lawāzim) of external existence. The mind can conceive black without becoming black.

Checkpoint
According to the answer given, why does conceiving black and white not make the mind black and white?
A) Because the mind is immaterial and cannot receive colors.
B) Because the quiddities present in the mind are mental forms, not the external identities with their physical effects.
C) Because black and white are not really opposites in the mind.
D) Because the mind can only hold one quiddity at a time.

Key Takeaway
Mental existence is not the same as external existence; mental forms do not carry physical properties like color or spatial coexistence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine committing a sin, does your mind make it feel harmless or unreal? How does the distinction between mental existence and external existence expose the deception of thinking that a sin is 'just a thought'? Will you now treat every sinful thought as a dangerous shadow that can take root in your heart if you dwell on it?
?
You carry in your mind a mental form of Allah's names and attributes—but is it a shadowy image lacking awe? How can you bridge the gap between conceptual knowledge (mental existence) and a reality that makes your heart tremble? What action will you take today to transform a mental truth into a living conviction that changes how you live?
?
Consider a good deed you intend to do. Have you ever felt satisfied just by imagining it, as if the reward were already yours? How does this lesson's teaching about mental existence being merely a shadow—not the real thing—expose the laziness of such self-satisfaction? What specific good deed will you accomplish before the day ends, moving it from your mind to the external world?

Second Objection and Response: Large Quiddities and the Mirror Analogy
The author presents the second objection and its answer, including the mirror analogy and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to mental existence and how the response distinguishes between external and shadowy existence using the mirror analogy.

Core Idea
Mental existence is shadowy, not literal, so size is irrelevant.

The second objection argues that huge quiddities like a mountain cannot fit in the mind. The response explains that the mind holds only a representation, not the actual object, using the analogy of an elephant in a grain of copper. The mirror objection is refuted by distinguishing reflection from impression, leading to a key distinction between external and shadowy existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the initial response
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the objection that large quiddities cannot be in the mind, and the answer using the copper grain analogy.
The answer says the mental image is not the actual identity of the mountain.
2
Examine the mirror objection and its refutation
The objector uses a mirror as an example. Read how the response denies impression in the mirror and explains reflection, with the evidence of moving image.
This refutation is crucial to understand the shadowy nature of mental existence.
3
Understand the summary distinction
The commentary summarizes that the key is distinguishing external existence (with effects) from shadowy existence (without effects). Reflect on why this solves the objection.
Even necessary concomitants have both existences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : حصول ماهية الجبل والسماء في ذهننا معلوم الانتفاء بالضرورة ) وتجويزه وجود لها أصلاً في كل واحدة من الملاحظتين، لأنَّ المحكوم عليه بها هو نفس الماهية، وهو ملحوظ قصداً في كل واحدة منهما . (و) جواب الوجه الثاني أن الممتنع حصول هوية الجبل مخصوصة يكون لها مناسبة مخصوصة بها يكون مرآة لمشاهدته فكلا كما تتخذ صورة الفيل في حبة من نحاس . قلت : فيه اعتراف بأن ليس الحاصل فيه ماهية الجبل وهو المطلوب وبهذا ظهر الجواب عما قيل : إنه ينطبع في المرآة مع صغرها صورة الجبل والسماء على أن الانطباع فيها ممنوع . هذا منقوض بالمرآة فإنه ينطبع فيها قريب من نصف كرة العالم فلا يسمع دعوى الضرورة في بطلانه، ويجاب بمنع انطباع المرئي في المرآة بل الرؤية بها بطريق الانعكاس والدليل عليه أن من رأى صورة شيء واقع في مقابلة المرآة في موضع معين منها، ثم انتقل الرائي من مكانه إلى مكان آخر من غير انتقال من المرآة وما وقع في مقابلتها يرى تلك الصورة في موضع آخر من المرآة ولو كانت الصورة منطبعة لاستحال ذلك . قوله : ( وجواب الوجه الثاني إلخ) خلاصة الجوابين الفرق بين الوجودين الخارجي والظلي بأن يترتب الآثار من التضاد والعظم والصغر والاتصاف بما هو مسلوب عن الذهن مخصوص بالوجود الخارجي. وما قيل : إِنَّ هذا الجواب لا يجري لو أورد الإشكال بلوازم الماهية، فمدفوع بأن المراد بالوجود الخارجي الأصيلي، ولا شك أن لوازم الماهية لها وجود أصيلي تترتب عليه الآثار، ووجود ظلي فيجري الفرق بين الوجودين فيها أيضاً . قوله : ( وجواب الوجه الأول قد عرفت في بحث الوجود الذهني ما في هذا الجواب فارجع إليه . قوله : ( ولا معنى للماهية إلا الصورة (العقلية أي لا معنى لها في هذا المقام إلا تلك، فلا يكون هذا الحصر مخلفاً لما سيجيء من أن الماهية تطلق بالاشتراك على معنيين.
The second reason: The obtaining of the quiddity of the mountain and sky in our mind is known by necessity to be impossible, and its allowance [would imply] existence for them in each of the two observations, because that which is judged by them is the very quiddity, and it is intentionally observed in each of them. The answer to the second reason: It is impossible to obtain the identity of the mountain having a particular proportion that would be a mirror for observing it; rather, just as the image of an elephant is taken in a grain of copper. I said: In this there is an admission that what obtains is not the quiddity of the mountain, which is the desired point, and thus the answer to what was said appears: that the image of the mountain and sky are impressed in the mirror despite its smallness, but the impression [in the mirror] is impossible. This is refuted by [the example of] the mirror, for it impresses nearly half the celestial sphere, so the claim of necessity in its impossibility is not accepted. And the answer is the denial of the impression of the visible object in the mirror; rather, vision by it is by reflection. The evidence for this is that if someone sees the image of something placed opposite the mirror at a particular location in it, then the viewer moves from his place to another place without the mirror or its opposite moving, he sees that image in another location in the mirror; if the image were impressed, this would be impossible. His saying: (Answer to the second reason, etc.) The summary of the two answers is the distinction between external and shadowy existence, in that the effects of opposition, bigness, smallness, and being described with what is negated of the mind are particular to external existence. And what is said: that this answer does not apply if the objection is raised regarding the necessary concomitants of the quiddity, is refuted by the fact that by 'external existence' is meant the principal existence, and there is no doubt that the necessary concomitants of the quiddity have a principal existence upon which effects follow, and a shadowy existence, so the distinction between the two existences runs in them as well. His saying: (Answer to the first reason, you have known in the discussion of mental existence what is in this answer, so refer to it. His saying: (And there is no meaning of quiddity except the mental form, i.e., there is no meaning for it in this context except that, so this restriction does not contradict what will come that quiddity is used in two senses.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection: that it is known by necessity that the quiddity of a huge object like a mountain cannot obtain in the mind. To allow it would imply that the mountain exists in the mind in its full magnitude. The answer explains that it is impossible to obtain the actual identity (huwiyya) of the mountain in the mind, because that would require the mountain's specific proportions to be present. Instead, the mind contains a representation (mithāl) like the image of an elephant engraved in a grain of copper—a small likeness, not the actual mountain. A counter-objection using a mirror is raised: a mirror can contain the image of a large object like the sky, so why could the mind not contain a mountain? The response denies that reflection in a mirror is true impression (inṭibā‘); it is merely reflection (in‘ikās), as proven by the fact that the image changes location with the viewer's movement, which would not happen if the image were actually impressed. The commentator then summarizes that the core distinction is between external existence (al-wujūd al-khārijī) and shadowy existence (al-wujūd al-ẓillī), with external effects (āthār) like size, opposition, and attributes belonging only to external existence. Even necessary concomitants (lawāzim) of quiddities have both principal and shadowy existence, so the distinction holds. Finally, the commentator refers the reader to earlier discussions for the answer to the first objection and clarifies that the statement 'quiddity is nothing but the mental form' is specific to this context and does not contradict the broader usage of 'quiddity' in two senses (mental and external).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental representation implies actual physical presence of the object in the mind.
Correction: The text clarifies that mental existence is shadowy and lacks physical effects like size and opposition.

Checkpoint
How does the response refute the mirror objection that large objects can be impressed in a small mirror?
A) By denying that any impression occurs in a mirror, explaining vision by reflection.
B) By arguing that the mirror only shows a small part of the object.
C) By claiming the mind is smaller than a mirror.
D) By stating that the mirror example is irrelevant because mirrors are physical.

Key Takeaway
The mind contains only shadowy representations of quiddities, not the actual objects with their physical effects, as shown by the mirror analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you think about something you desire intensely, do you treat the mental image as if it were the actual thing? How can you remind yourself that your mind's representation is a shadow, not the reality, and act accordingly?
?
The mirror analogy shows that reflection is not the same as presence. In your spiritual life, are you settling for a 'reflection' of faith—external rituals without internal transformation—rather than the direct presence of tawḥīd in your heart? What step can you take today to move from shadowy knowledge to real conviction?

The Argument from Non-Priority Refuted
Presents and refutes the argument from non-priority against multiple objects of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from non-priority against a single knowledge relating to multiple objects and its refutation.

Core Idea
An argument against multiple objects of knowledge is refuted by denying its key premise.

This passage presents a theological argument that attempts to prove that a single knowledge cannot relate to multiple known things, based on the idea that if it could relate to two, it could relate to an infinite number, which is absurd. The refutation challenges the premise that no number is prior to another and also turns the argument against the Mu'tazilite concept of power.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the argument and refutation come through.
Pay attention to the parentheses in the Arabic; they likely indicate the original matn.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The argument claims: if a single knowledge could relate to two known things, then no number would have priority, so it could relate to infinite things—which is impossible. Thus it cannot relate to even two.
This is a 'slippery slope' style argument.
3
Understand the refutation's two prongs
The refutation first denies that there is no priority in reality—it is possible that two is the limit. Second, even if there is no priority in our estimation, that doesn't prove impossibility.
4
See the self-defeating application to Mu'tazilite power
If the arguer is a Mu'tazilite, a similar problem arises for their concept of a single temporally originated power that can relate to multiple actions but not infinite ones, so the argument proves too much.
This is a tu quoque refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس عدد أولى من عدد فيلزم من جواز تعلقه بأكثر من واحد ( تعلقه) بل جواز تعلقه ( بأمور غير متناهية)، فيلزم أن يجوز كون أحدنا عالماً بعلم واحد بمعلومات لا تتناهى وهو باطل قطعاً، ( وقد عرفته ) وأنه ضعيف جداً، لأن عدم الأولوية في نفس الأمر ممنوع، وعدمها عندنا لا يجدي شيئاً، والمحتج بهذه الحجة إن كان معتزلياً ورد عليه القدرة الواحدة الحادثة فإنها على أصله يجوز تعلقها بمقدورين وأكثر، ولا يجوز تعلقها بمقدورات لا تتناهى
No number is prior to another number, so from the permissibility of its relation to more than one (its relation) necessarily follows the permissibility of its relation to infinite things, and thus it would be permissible for one of us to know, with a single knowledge, infinite known things, and that is absolutely false. (And you have known it) and that it is very weak, because the absence of priority in reality is denied, and its absence in our estimation is of no avail. And the one who argues with this proof, if he is a Mu'tazilite, is refuted by the single temporally originated power, for on his principle it is permissible for it to relate to two or more actions, but it is not permissible for it to relate to infinite actions.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes a key argument against the possibility of a single knowledge relating to multiple known things. The argument claims: if a single knowledge could relate to two known things, then no number would have priority over another, so it could relate to an infinite number — which is impossible. Therefore, it cannot relate to even two. The refutation responds: the premise that no number is prior in reality is denied; the lack of priority in our estimation is irrelevant. Moreover, if the arguer is a Mu'tazilite, a similar problem arises for their concept of a single temporally originated power that can relate to multiple actions but not infinite ones — thus the argument is self-defeating.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that a single knowledge cannot relate to multiple objects at all.
Correction: The argument is refuted because its premise that no number is prior is denied, and even if accepted, it does not rule out a finite limit. Additionally, a similar problem arises for the Mu'tazilite concept of power.

Checkpoint

D) Because knowledge can relate to infinite things.

Key Takeaway
The absence of priority does not entail the possibility of infinite relation; the argument is weak and self-defeating against Mu'tazilites.

?
The Mu'tazilite faced a contradiction between his own principles and the argument he used. Are there areas in your life where your beliefs or values conflict with each other, yet you ignore the inconsistency? What would it cost you to resolve that conflict honestly before Allah?
?
This lesson shows that what seems like a sound proof can be refuted by denying a single hidden assumption. Is there a false belief you cling to because you've accepted a 'given' that is not actually true? What would it take for you to let go of that assumption and submit to the truth, even if it changes your worldview?

Bahili's Position on Single Knowledge and Two Objects
Bahili's ruling on a single knowledge relating to two theoretical vs. necessary objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili's position on whether a single knowledge can relate to two theoretical objects or two necessary objects, and the reasoning behind each claim.

Core Idea
A single knowledge cannot embrace two theoretical objects but can embrace two necessary ones.

This lesson presents the third position in the debate, from the Ash'ari theologian Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili. He draws a sharp distinction between theoretical and necessary objects of knowledge, allowing the latter but forbidding the former. The passage also addresses an objection and critiques an earlier analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice Bahili's main claim and the structure of his argument.
Pay attention to the terms 'nazar' (mental consideration) and 'daruri/naẓari'.
2
Identify the two parts of Bahili's position
Find where Bahili states the prohibition for theoretical objects and the permission for necessary objects. Note the reasoning given for each.
The prohibition is based on the impossibility of two simultaneous mental considerations; the permission relies on an analogy with God's knowledge.
3
Examine the response to the objection
Read the answer Bahili gives to the objection about the coexistence of two mental considerations. Understand how he argues that a single mental consideration can cover both theoretical objects.
The commentator adds a note that the analogy to God's knowledge is invalid, so consider that critique.

The third: the position of Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili from the Ash'aris, which is that it is not permissible for it to relate, i.e. for a single knowledge to relate to two theoretical (objects), i.e. two theoretical known things, because it necessitates the coexistence of two mental considerations in a single state, and that is impossible by necessary intuitive experience. And it is permissible for it to relate to two necessary (objects) because of what has passed in the first position regarding the analogy to God's knowledge, and you have known the invalidity of this analogy. As for the answer regarding the coexistence of two mental considerations, it is what he mentioned by saying: we say, we can know them, i.e. the two theoretical known things, by a single mental consideration just as we know them by a single knowledge, for when the knowledge of them is one.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents the third position on the issue, attributed to Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili of the Ash'aris. He argues that a single knowledge cannot relate to two theoretical objects because that would require two mental considerations simultaneously, which is intuitively impossible. However, it can relate to two necessary objects, based on an earlier argument using God's knowledge (though the commentator notes the weakness of that analogy). The response to the objection about two mental considerations is that we can know two theoretical objects by a single mental consideration, just as we know them by a single knowledge — because when the knowledge is one, the consideration can also be one.

Al-Bahili's Doctrine on Single Knowledge and Two Knowns
Al-Bahili's position with preliminary refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Bahili's position that a single knowledge cannot relate to two theoretical knowns but can relate to two necessary knowns, and why the multiplicity of relations does not necessitate multiplicity in the essence.

Core Idea
A single knowledge can relate to two necessary knowns but not two theoretical ones.

This passage introduces the third opinion within the Ash'ari school regarding the relation of a single knowledge to multiple known things. Al-Bahili argues that while a single knowledge can encompass multiple necessary truths, it cannot do so with theoretical truths because that would demand two simultaneous acts of reasoning. Before stating his view, the author refutes a previous objection by showing that multiple relations do not imply multiple essences. Let's examine his argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning of the lesson to the statement of al-Bahili's view. Then read the translation. Notice how the preliminary refutation sets the stage for the position.
The passage starts with the general topic and a refutation about quiddities (huwiyyāt).
2
Identify the core claim
Find al-Bahili's exact statement: a single knowledge cannot relate to two theoretical knowns but can to two necessary ones. Note the reasoning given: it would necessitate the conjunction of two acts of theoretical reasoning.
The claim is about the relation (taʿalluq) of knowledge, not its essence.
3
Understand the preliminary refutation
The author argues that a single blackness has multiple relations (to agent, substrate, time) but remains one. This shows that multiplicity of relations does not necessitate multiplicity in the essence. This refutes an earlier objection.
This is a key principle: relations are not part of the essence.
4
Note the comparison to God's knowledge
Al-Bahili justifies the permissibility for necessary knowns by analogy to God's knowledge, but the author notes this analogy is corrupt. This prepares for later discussion.
The analogy is from the first opinion (early Ash'aris).

النوع الثاني - المقصد الثاني : العلم الواحد وتعلقه بمعلومين
ونقض أيضاً بسائر (الهويات المتعلقة بأشياء متعددة كالسواد الواحد، فإن له تعلقاً بالفاعل الموجد، وتعلقاً آخر بالمحل القابل، وتعلقاً ثالثاً بالزمان الذي وجد فيه، إلى غير ذلك . فتعدد التعلقات لا يقتضي تعددا في الذات وليس يلزم من وحدة الذات أن تكون هي مأخوذة مع تعلق مخصوص سادة مسدها مأخوذة مع تعلق آخر، ( الثالث : مذهب أبي الحسن الباهلي ) من الأشاعرة وهو أنه : ( لا يجوز تعلقه)، أي تعلق العلم الواحد بنظريين)، أي بمعلومين نظريين، لأنه يستلزم اجتماع نظرين)، في ( حالة واحدة ) وهو محال ) بالضرورة الوجدانية، ( ويجوز تعلقه بضروريين لما مر في المذهب الأول من القياس على علم الله تعالى وقد عرفت فساد هذا القياس، وأما الجواب عن اجتماع النظرين فهو ما ذكره بقوله : ( قلنا : قد نعلمهما ) أي المعلومين النظريين، بنظر واحد كما نعلمهما بعلم واحد، فإنه إذا كان العلم بهما واحداً

قوله: (ويجوز تعلقه بضروريين) التخصيص بالضروريين إشارة إلى أنّه لم ينقل من صاحب هذا المذهب في جواز تعلق العلم الواحد بنظري وضروري شيء، وأما بالنظر إلى دليله، فيجوز ذلك لعدم لزوم اجتماع النظرين قيل : الحق عدم الجواز لأنه يلزم حصول علم واحد بالنظر وبدونه، وفيه أنَّ النظر لحصول التعلق لا لنفس العلم بتعليق واحد بالنظر وآخر بدونه . قوله : أي) المعلومين إلخ إشارة إلى أن النظري ليس هاهنا بالمعنى المتعارف فإنّه صفة
العلم.
قوله: (وبسائر الهويات قال الأبهري: وقد يدفع بأنَّ التعلق داخل في حقيقة العلم وماهيته دون سائر الهويات .
قوله: (بنظريين) قيل : كذلك بنظري وضروري لأنّ الضروري يحصل بلا نظر بخلاف النظري فلو تعلق علم واحد بمعلومين نظري وضروري لزم تحقق النظر وعدمه وفيه نظر لأنّ بعض الضروريات قد لا يحصل إلا بعد النظر وإن لم يحصل بالنظر كالعلم بأن لنا لذة من هذا النظر فما ذكر لا يدل على عدم جواز تعلق العلم الواحد بالنظري والضروري المذكور. قوله: (لأنه يستلزم اجتماع (نظرين فيه بحث لجواز أن يكون التعلقان متفرقين فكذا النظران، ولا شك أنَّ النظر إنما يستلزم العلم من حيث تعلقه لا من حيث ذاته حتى يلزم تحصيل الحاصل في النظر الثاني فإن قلت : العلم لا ينفك عن تعلقه، قلت: ممنوع بل قد البعض بأن من يديم العلم بأن زيداً سيدخل البلد غداً إلى غد علم بهذا العلم أنه دخل الآن ويمكن أن يدفع بأن يبنى الكلام على عدم بقاء الأعراض .
صرح
كفاه نظر واحد، فاجتماع النظرين إنّما يلزم إذا لم يجز تعلق علم واحد بهما، وذلك مصادرة .
The second type - the second objective: the single knowledge and its relation to two known things. And also a refutation with the rest of the quiddities related to multiple things, like a single blackness, for it has a relation to the agent that brings it into existence, and another relation to the receptive substrate, and a third relation to the time in which it existed, and so on. So multiplicity of relations does not necessitate multiplicity in the essence, and it does not follow from the unity of the essence that it is taken with a specific relation serving in place of it taken with another relation. (Third: the opinion of Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili) from the Ashʿarites, which is that: (it is not permissible for it to relate), i.e., the relation of a single knowledge to two theoretical things, i.e., two theoretical known things, because it necessitates the conjunction of two acts of theoretical reasoning in one state, which is impossible by intuitive necessity, and (it is permissible for it to relate to two necessary things) due to what was mentioned in the first opinion from the analogy to God's knowledge, and you have known the corruption of this analogy. As for the answer concerning the conjunction of two acts of theoretical reasoning, it is what he mentioned by his saying: (We say: we can know both of them), i.e., the two theoretical known things, by a single theoretical reasoning just as we know them by a single knowledge, for if the knowledge of them is one... His saying: (It necessitates conjunction etc.) This is apparent on the assumption that theoretical reasoning is beneficial for the essence of knowledge, but if it is beneficial for its relation, then the conjunction does not follow, because it is permissible for one theoretical reasoning to benefit its relation to a known thing at one time, and another theoretical reasoning to benefit its relation to another known thing at another time. His saying: (And it is permissible for it to relate to two necessary things) The specification to necessary things indicates that nothing has been transmitted from the proponent of this opinion concerning the permissibility of a single knowledge relating to a theoretical and a necessary thing. But according to his proof, it is permissible due to the non-necessity of the conjunction of two theoretical reasonings. It was said: The correct view is the impermissibility, because it necessitates obtaining a single knowledge with theoretical reasoning and without it. And in it, that theoretical reasoning is for obtaining the relation, not for the essence of knowledge, by one relation with theoretical reasoning and another without it. His saying: (i.e., the two known things, etc.) indicates that 'theoretical' here is not in the conventional sense, for it is an attribute of knowledge. His saying: (And with the rest of the quiddities) al-Abhari said: It can be repelled by [saying] that relation is within the reality and quiddity of knowledge, unlike the rest of the quiddities. His saying: (to two theoretical things) It was said: likewise to a theoretical and a necessary thing, because the necessary is obtained without theoretical reasoning unlike the theoretical, so if a single knowledge relates to two known things, one theoretical and one necessary, the realization of theoretical reasoning and its absence would be necessary. And in it there is a consideration, because some necessary things may not be obtained except after theoretical reasoning, even if not obtained by theoretical reasoning, like the knowledge that we have pleasure from this theoretical reasoning. So what was mentioned does not indicate the impermissibility of a single knowledge relating to the aforementioned theoretical and necessary. His saying: (because it necessitates the conjunction of two theoretical reasonings) contains discussion, because it is permissible for the two relations to be separate, and likewise the two acts of theoretical reasoning, and there is no doubt that theoretical reasoning only necessitates knowledge from the perspective of its relation, not from the perspective of its essence, such that the obtaining of the already obtained would be necessary in the second theoretical reasoning. If you say: knowledge is inseparable from its relation, I say: denied; rather some [argue] that whoever continually knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow until tomorrow, knows by this knowledge that he has entered now, and it can be repelled by building the discourse on the non-permanence of accidents. He stated: one theoretical reasoning suffices him, so the conjunction of two theoretical reasonings would only follow if a single knowledge relating to both is not permissible, and that is begging the question.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents the third position of Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili (an Ash′arite) regarding whether a single knowledge (al-ʿilm al-wāḥid) can relate to two known things (maʿlūmāt). It begins with a preliminary refutation against a previous objection concerning multiple relations to a single essence, arguing that multiplicity of relations does not necessitate multiplicity in essence. Then al-Bahili's doctrine is stated: a single knowledge cannot relate to two theoretical known things (naẓariyyān) because that would require two simultaneous acts of theoretical reasoning (naẓar), which is intuitively impossible. However, it can relate to two necessary known things (ḍarūriyyān), analogously to God's knowledge—though the author notes this analogy is flawed. The commentator then explains various aspects: the distinction between theoretical reasoning benefiting the essence of knowledge versus its relation, the specification to necessary things, and the meaning of 'theoretical' as an attribute of knowledge. Several objections are raised and answered: e.g., the impossibility of combining theoretical reasoning and its absence, the possibility of separate relations, and the nature of necessary knowledge after theoretical reasoning. The passage concludes that the objection of conjunction is circular if one assumes the impermissibility of a single knowledge relating to both knowns. The entire discussion is a careful logical analysis of the relationship between knowledge, its objects, and the act of reasoning.

تعلق
معلوم
نظري
ضروري
هويات
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if one knowledge relates to two things, the essence of knowledge must become multiple.
Correction: The passage clarifies that multiplicity of relations does not necessitate multiplicity in the essence; a single essence can have many relations.

Checkpoint
According to al-Bahili, why cannot a single knowledge relate to two theoretical known things?
A) Because it would require two simultaneous acts of theoretical reasoning, which is impossible.
B) Because theoretical knowledge is always derived from necessary knowledge.
C) Because God's knowledge cannot relate to two things at once.
D) Because the two known things would have to be identical.

Key Takeaway
Al-Bahili's core doctrine: a single knowledge can relate to two necessary knowns but not to two theoretical knowns, and multiple relations do not imply a multiple essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a single belief you hold that you consider 'necessary'—so obvious you never question it. Now think of a belief that required effortful reasoning. How does al-Bahili's distinction between necessary and theoretical knowns reveal a difference in how your own mind relates to those truths? Which type do you rely on more in your daily life? Are you comfortable with that?
?
The passage argues that multiple relations do not fragment a single essence. In your own experience, do you ever feel that your core convictions (your single 'knowledge') are threatened by the many contexts they apply to? Where have you mistakenly assumed that changing circumstances mean your certainty is broken? What would it mean to hold your faith as a single essence with many relations, not a fractured thing?
?
Al-Bahili rejected an analogy to God's knowledge. Have you ever assumed that because God knows all things in a single act, you should be able to know all things easily? Where does your expectation of effortless knowledge clash with the reality that some truths require painful reasoning? Will you stop making excuses and do the work of theoretical reflection, or will you continue to demand necessary knowledge where only theoretical is possible?

Refining the Argument: Theoretical Reasoning and Objections
Detailed defense and objections to al-Bahili's view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the conjunction of theoretical reasoning is avoided and why the relation of knowledge to theoretical and necessary knowns is treated differently.

Core Idea
Theoretical reasoning benefits the relation of knowledge, not its essence, allowing a single knowledge to be gained by one reasoning.

This part of the passage delves into the details of al-Bahili's argument, responding to objections about the conjunction of two theoretical reasonings. The commentator explains that if theoretical reasoning is seen as beneficial for the relation of knowledge rather than its essence, the conjunction is avoided. It also clarifies that 'theoretical' here is an attribute of knowledge, not of the known. Several objections and counterarguments are examined, including the possibility of combining theoretical and necessary knowledge, and the circularity in the objection itself. Let's follow the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the detailed response
Read from 'قلنا : قد نعلمهما' to the end. Note the distinction between theoretical reasoning benefiting the essence of knowledge versus its relation.
The commentator offers a way to avoid the conjunction: the two reasonings could be separate in time.
2
Examine the specification to necessarys
Notice that al-Bahili only allowed relations to two necessarys, not to a theoretical and necessary. The commentator speculates that according to his proof, it might be permissible, but then raises an objection about combining things obtained with and without reasoning.
This shows the nuance in the doctrine.
3
Understand the counter-objection
Some argue that even necessary things may require reasoning to be known (e.g., knowing pleasure from reasoning). So the objection fails.
This is a deeper point about the nature of necessary knowledge.
4
Analyze the circularity objection
The passage concludes that the objection of conjunction is circular if it assumes the impermissibility of a single knowledge relating to both knowns.
This is a dialectical move: the objection begs the question.

His saying: (It necessitates conjunction etc.) This is apparent on the assumption that theoretical reasoning is beneficial for the essence of knowledge, but if it is beneficial for its relation, then the conjunction does not follow, because it is permissible for one theoretical reasoning to benefit its relation to a known thing at one time, and another theoretical reasoning to benefit its relation to another known thing at another time. His saying: (And it is permissible for it to relate to two necessary things) The specification to necessary things indicates that nothing has been transmitted from the proponent of this opinion concerning the permissibility of a single knowledge relating to a theoretical and a necessary thing. It was said: The correct view is the impermissibility, because it necessitates obtaining a single knowledge with theoretical reasoning and without it. And in it, that theoretical reasoning is for obtaining the relation, not for the essence of knowledge, by one relation with theoretical reasoning and another without it. His saying: (i.e., the two known things, etc.) indicates that 'theoretical' here is not in the conventional sense, for it is an attribute of knowledge. His saying: (And with the rest of the quiddities) al-Abhari said: It can be repelled by [saying] that relation is within the reality and quiddity of knowledge, unlike the rest of the quiddities. His saying: (to two theoretical things) It was said: likewise to a theoretical and a necessary thing, because the necessary is obtained without theoretical reasoning unlike the theoretical, so if a single knowledge relates to two known things, one theoretical and one necessary, the realization of theoretical reasoning and its absence would be necessary. And in it there is a consideration, because some necessary things may not be obtained except after theoretical reasoning, even if not obtained by theoretical reasoning, like the knowledge that we have pleasure from this theoretical reasoning. So what was mentioned does not indicate the impermissibility of a single knowledge relating to the aforementioned theoretical and necessary. His saying: (because it necessitates the conjunction of two theoretical reasonings) contains discussion, because it is permissible for the two relations to be separate, and likewise the two acts of theoretical reasoning, and there is no doubt that theoretical reasoning only necessitates knowledge from the perspective of its relation, not from the perspective of its essence, such that the obtaining of the already obtained would be necessary in the second theoretical reasoning. If you say: knowledge is inseparable from its relation, I say: denied; rather some [argue] that whoever continually knows that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow until tomorrow, knows by this knowledge that he has entered now, and it can be repelled by building the discourse on the non-permanence of accidents. He stated: one theoretical reasoning suffices him, so the conjunction of two theoretical reasonings would only follow if a single knowledge relating to both is not permissible, and that is begging the question.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents the third position of Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili (an Ash′arite) regarding whether a single knowledge (al-ʿilm al-wāḥid) can relate to two known things (maʿlūmāt). It begins with a preliminary refutation against a previous objection concerning multiple relations to a single essence, arguing that multiplicity of relations does not necessitate multiplicity in essence. Then al-Bahili's doctrine is stated: a single knowledge cannot relate to two theoretical known things (naẓariyyān) because that would require two simultaneous acts of theoretical reasoning (naẓar), which is intuitively impossible. However, it can relate to two necessary known things (ḍarūriyyān), analogously to God's knowledge—though the author notes this analogy is flawed. The commentator then explains various aspects: the distinction between theoretical reasoning benefiting the essence of knowledge versus its relation, the specification to necessary things, and the meaning of 'theoretical' as an attribute of knowledge. Several objections are raised and answered: e.g., the impossibility of combining theoretical reasoning and its absence, the possibility of separate relations, and the nature of necessary knowledge after theoretical reasoning. The passage concludes that the objection of conjunction is circular if one assumes the impermissibility of a single knowledge relating to both knowns. The entire discussion is a careful logical analysis of the relationship between knowledge, its objects, and the act of reasoning.

Key Takeaway
The defense of al-Bahili's view rests on distinguishing whether theoretical reasoning benefits the essence of knowledge or its relation, and showing that objections often assume what is to be proved.

Single Knowledge and Two Separable Knowns
Presents the view that single knowledge cannot relate to two separable knowns, with a detailed refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn argue that a single knowledge cannot relate to two knowns that can be known independently, and how objections to this argument are addressed.

Core Idea
A single knowledge cannot know two separable things, or it would be separable from itself.

The scholars debated whether one act of knowledge can encompass multiple distinct objects. The fourth position, attributed to al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn, argues that if two knowns are separable in principle—like blackness and whiteness—a single knowledge cannot relate to both. This lesson unpacks their argument and the subsequent discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes objections and clarifications.
2
Identify the core claim and its justification
The passage states that if two knowns can be known separately, a single knowledge cannot relate to both; otherwise, it would be separable from itself. Find this argument in the text.
3
Examine the objection and response
An objector says the implication only holds if separation is absolutely permissible. The response: separation is permissible only when known by two knowledges, not one. Trace this exchange.
4
Consider the implications for divine attributes
The passage addresses whether this would dispense with multiple attributes. It argues that a single knowledge would still produce only one attribute of omniscience. Reflect on this.
The discussion is about theoretical knowns.

The fourth opinion: and it is the choice of al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn: it is not permissible for it to relate to two known things, where (the separation of knowledge of them is permissible), i.e., any two known things such that it is conceivable to know one of them while it is possible not to know the other, like the eternal and the temporal, and blackness and whiteness. For it is not permissible for a single knowledge to relate to both, (otherwise the separation of the thing from itself would be permissible) because the permissibility of separation between the knowledge of them is assumed, so if that knowledge is one, its separation from itself would be permissible. (We say: what you mentioned would only follow if the separation between the knowledge of blackness and the knowledge of whiteness were absolutely permissible, and that is denied, for one may say: if they are known by two knowledges, separation between the knowledge of them is permissible, but if they are known by one knowledge, then that separation is not conceivable. And to this refers His saying: (we may know what you have mentioned), i.e., the two known things between which separation of knowledge is permissible, (sometimes by one knowledge), so that separation is not permissible, and sometimes by two knowledges, so separation is permissible, and there is no impossibility in that, because the permissibility of a state in one case and its impermissibility in another. And from that, i.e., from the permissibility of a single knowledge relating to those two known things sometimes, and two knowledges relating to them another time, (the dispensability of multiplicity of attributes) does not follow, by saying: if it were permissible for a single knowledge to be productive of omniscience regarding blackness and omniscience... it is agreed upon that if knowledge of them is multiple, it would be productive of two omnisciences also, then a single attribute would be productive of two different rulings like multiple attributes, and then separation would be permissible with whiteness, along with... His saying: (the separation of the thing from itself would be permissible) is based on the assumption that separation between the two knowledges is permissible. His saying: (what you mentioned would only follow, etc.) And as for the one who denies it, omniscience with him is the being qualified by knowledge, so it does not follow with him that a single knowledge necessitates two omnisciences such that the dispensability of multiplicity of attributes would follow. Meanwhile, His saying: (i.e., the two theoretical known things) contains an indication that what the author of al-Maqāṣid mentioned in the answer, that there is no impossibility in obtaining multiple things by a single theoretical reasoning, like the result and the negation of the objection and... because the discussion is about the two theoretical known things, and the two latter knowledges are necessary even if obtained after theoretical reasoning. His saying: (the separation of it from itself would be permissible) is based on the non-separation of the relation of knowledge from it, as we indicated. His saying: (what you mentioned would only follow if separation is permissible, etc.) For if you say: the permissibility of separation is its very possibility, and possibility for what is possible is perpetual, so separation is always permissible, and in it the desired [conclusion] is obtained. I say: yes, except that it does not contradict the impossibility by another [factor], which is being known by a single knowledge, for when the single knowledge relates to both, the permissibility of separation remains as it is, by [the possibility] that two knowledges relate to them.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth position attributed to al-Qadi (ʿAbd al-Jabbār?) and Imam al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī). They hold that a single knowledge cannot relate to two known things if those two known things are such that it is possible to know one independently of the other—for example, the eternal and the temporal, or blackness and whiteness. The argument is that if it were possible for a single knowledge to relate to both, then since it is assumed that the two knowledges of these things can be separated (i.e., one can be known without the other), the single knowledge would be separable from itself, which is absurd. The commentator then records an objection: the claimed implication only holds if separation is absolutely permissible, i.e., regardless of whether the knowledge is single or multiple. But the proponent can respond: separation is permissible when the knowns are known by two knowledges, but when they are known by a single knowledge, that separation is not conceivable. This is analogous to saying that the same two objects can be known sometimes by one knowledge and sometimes by two, and the permissibility varies accordingly. The commentator further discusses the issue of whether this would dispense with the need for multiple attributes (ṣifāt), arguing that it does not, because a single knowledge would still produce only one attribute of omniscience. There is also a clarification that the discussion is specifically about theoretical knowns, and that the two latter knowledges (the result and the negation of the objection) are necessary, even if obtained after theoretical reasoning. Finally, an objection about the perpetuity of possibility is answered: the possibility of separation is not negated, but it is blocked by the external factor of being known by a single knowledge.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument applies to any two knowns, not only those that can be known independently.
Correction: The argument specifically targets knowns that are separable in principle, like blackness and whiteness, not inseparable ones like God's attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth position, why cannot a single knowledge relate to blackness and whiteness?
A) Because blackness and whiteness are opposites.
B) Because it is possible to know one without the other, so a single knowledge would be separable from itself.
C) Because God's knowledge cannot encompass both.
D) Because they are both necessary known things.

Key Takeaway
A single knowledge cannot relate to two separable knowns, or it would be separable from itself; this does not apply when the knowns are known by two knowledges.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Fourth Madhab on a Single Knowledge Attaching to Two Knowns
Al-Jurjani states the fourth madhab and refutes an objection about self-separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth madhab's ruling on whether a single knowledge can attach to two knowns and how an objection is refuted.

Core Idea
Knowledge's separability depends on how it is known.

Al-Jurjani presents the fourth position, chosen by al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn, that a single knowledge cannot attach to two knowns if separation is conceivable. He then anticipates and refutes an objection by distinguishing between cases where knowledge is one versus two.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fourth madhab's position
Read the passage and locate the stated position: that a single knowledge cannot attach to two knowns if separation of knowledge is possible.
Note the examples: eternal/originated, black/white.
2
Understand the reason given
The reason is that otherwise separation of the thing from itself would be allowed. Reflect on why that would be absurd.
This is the core argument.
3
Follow the objection
The passage presents an objection: the consequence only follows if separation is absolute, but it may not be when knowledge is one.
The objector challenges the universality of the premise.
4
See the refutation
Al-Jurjani clarifies that permissibility in one scenario does not entail permissibility in another, using the distinction between knowing by one or two knowledges.
This resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذهب الرابع: وهو مختار القاضي وإمام الحرمين لا يجوز تعلقه بمعلومين حيث يجوز انفكاك العلم بهما، أي كل معلومين يتصور العلم بأحدهما مع إمكان عدم العلم بالآخر كالقديم والحديث والسواد والبياض، فإنه لا يجوز أن يتعلق بهما علم واحد، وإلا جاز انفكاك الشيء عن نفسه إذ المفروض جواز الانفكاك بين العلم بهما، فإذا كان ذلك العلم واحداً جاز انفكاكه عن نفسه، قلنا: إنما يلزم ما ذكرتم إذا جاز الانفكاك بين العلم بالسواد والعلم بالبياض مطلقاً، وهو ممنوع إذ لقائل أن يقول: إنهما إذا علما بعلمين جاز الانفكاك بين العلم بهما، وأما إذا علما بعلم واحد فلا يتصور ذلك الانفكاك، وإليه الإشارة بقوله: قد نعلم ما ذكرتموه أعني المعلومين اللذين يجوز الانفكاك بين العلم بهما تارة بعلم واحد فلا يجوز ذلك الانفكاك، وتارة بعلمين فيجوز الانفكاك ولا استحالة في ذلك، لأن جواز حالة في وعدم جوازه في أخرى.
The fourth opinion: which is the choice of al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn: it is not permissible for it (knowledge) to attach to two known things where separation of knowledge from them is possible, i.e., every two known things such that knowledge of one of them can be conceived while non-knowledge of the other is possible, like the eternal and the originated, and black and white. Then a single knowledge is not permissible to attach to them two, otherwise separation of the thing from itself would be permissible, since separation between the two knowledges is presupposed. So if that knowledge is one, separation of it from itself would be permissible. We said: What you mentioned necessarily follows only if separation between knowledge of black and knowledge of white is permissible absolutely, and that is denied. For one may say: When they are known by two knowledges, separation between the knowledges is permissible; but when they are known by one knowledge, that separation is not conceivable. And to this points his saying: 'We may know what you mentioned' i.e., the two known things between which separation of knowledge is possible, sometimes by one knowledge, so that separation is not permissible, and sometimes by two knowledges, so separation is permissible and there is no impossibility in that, because permissibility in one case and impermissibility in another.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage defines and defends the fourth madhab (opinion) on the issue of whether a single knowledge can attach to two known things. Al-Jurjani first states the position: according to al-Qadi (Abd al-Jabbar) and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), a single knowledge cannot attach to two known things if it is possible to have knowledge of one without the other – such as the eternal and the originated, or black and white. The reason given is that if a single knowledge did attach, then the thing (the knowledge) would separate from itself, since separation between the two knowledges is assumed possible. Al-Jurjani then presents the objection in the form of a rebuttal: this consequence only follows if separation is possible absolutely, but it is not possible when the two items are known by a single knowledge. The objector could say that when they are known by two knowledges, separation is possible, but when known by one knowledge, that same separation does not occur. The author supports this by quoting a previous statement: 'We may know what you mentioned sometimes by one knowledge, sometimes by two.' The conclusion is that permissibility in one scenario does not entail permissibility in another, so the objection fails.

Correction: The passage shows that when they are known by a single knowledge, that very knowledge is not separable, so the objection fails.
