Single Knowledge for Inseparable Knowns
Al-Jurjani explains that a single knowledge may attach to knowns that are inseparable in knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a single knowledge can encompass two knowns when they are inseparable in knowledge, such as in reflexive or relational cases.

Core Idea
Some pairs of knowns are so intertwined that one knowledge covers both.

Al-Jurjani continues his discussion of the permissibility of a single knowledge attaching to multiple knowns. After addressing the general case where separation is possible, he now turns to knowns that cannot be separated in knowledge. This category includes reflexive knowledge (knowing that you know) and relational opposites. Understanding this helps clarify how Allah's knowledge can be single yet encompass multiple aspects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two categories Al-Jurjani presents: reflexive knowledge and relational knowledge.
The passage is concise but dense.
2
Identify the examples
The first example is 'knowledge of a thing and knowledge of knowledge of it'. The second is 'knowledge of opposition' – knowing that A is opposite to B implies knowing that B is opposite to A. The passage also mentions difference, likeness, and all relations.
These are cases where the two knowns cannot be separated in knowledge.
3
Understand the proof
The passage concludes with a proof: 'whoever knows a thing knows his knowledge of it'. This shows that reflexive knowledge is a single knowledge covering both the object and the awareness of it.
This proof applies to all inseparable knowns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما لا يجوز انفكاك العلم بهما كالعلم بالشيء، والعلم بالعلم به، وكالعلم بالتضاد، فإن العلم بمضادة شيء لآخر لا يكون إلا مع العلم بمضادة الآخر إياه، وكذا الحال في الاختلاف والتماثل وسائر الإضافات، فقد يتعلق بهما علم واحد أي يجوز تعلقه بهما، إذ من علم شيئاً علم علمه.
And as for what does not permit separation of knowledge from them, like knowledge of a thing and knowledge of knowledge of it, and like knowledge of opposition – for knowledge of something being opposite to another does not exist except with knowledge of the other's being opposite to it – and similarly in the case of difference, likeness, and all relations, then a single knowledge may attach to them, i.e., it is permissible for it to attach to them, because whoever knows a thing knows his knowledge of it.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage addresses the second category: known things that cannot be separated in knowledge. Examples include knowing a thing and knowing that one knows it, and knowing the opposition between two things (since one cannot know that A is opposite to B without knowing that B is opposite to A). Similarly for difference, similarity, and all relational concepts. In such cases, a single knowledge can indeed attach to both knowns simultaneously. The proof given is that if someone knows a thing, he necessarily knows his knowledge of that thing (self-awareness of knowledge). Thus, the knowledge of the thing and the knowledge of the knowledge are two knowns that are inseparable, and a single knowledge encompasses them.

A Single Knowledge Can Relate to Two Known Things: The First Proof
The author defines the claim and gives the first proof using inseparable knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim that a single knowledge can relate to two known things when their knowledge is inseparable, and the first proof using the necessity of knowing that one knows a thing to avoid infinite regress.

Core Idea
A single knowledge can encompass two inseparable known things, preventing an infinite chain of knowledges.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a key argument in the theology of divine attributes. The author explains that a single attribute of knowledge can relate to multiple known objects when those objects are inherently connected in knowledge. He provides a proof based on the necessity that knowing something entails knowing that one knows it. Let's unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim and rejection
Read the opening where the author rejects the idea that a single attribute cannot necessitate two different judgments. He calls it false by necessity and consensus.
This sets up the problem: if a single attribute could give two judgments, it might undermine the need for multiple attributes like power and knowledge.
2
Distinguish separable and inseparable
Notice the author distinguishes between cases where the knowledge of two things can be separated and cases where they cannot. For inseparable cases, a single knowledge may relate to both.
Inseparable examples include knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge, and knowledge of contradiction.
3
Examine the proof
The core proof: if someone knows a thing, they necessarily know that they know it. If these were two separate knowledges, it would lead to an infinite regress. Therefore, a single knowledge must cover both.
The author also uses the example of al-Jafr and al-Jami'a to illustrate the absurdity of denying this.
4
Note the nature of the claim
The author clarifies that the claim is a particular affirmative, so only one example is needed. The proof does not need to cover every type of inseparable knowledge.
This addresses a potential misunderstanding that the proof must be universal.
5
Review objections and answers
The passage includes discussions about the nature of necessitation (customary vs. ontological) and about proper formulation of examples. These refine the argument.
Reflect on how the author defends the proof against these objections.

(And if, meaning one of us, does not know his knowledge of it) meaning what he knows from al-Jafr, the occurrence of relation to them two, and perhaps for the sake of matching its counterpart, I mean his saying: it is not permissible for it to relate to two known things if their knowledge can be separated, but the apparent then is that it is permissible for it to relate with the letter fa' to harmonize the preceding with the following. Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage defines the claim that a single knowledge can relate to two known things and provides the first proof using the example of knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge. The author begins by rejecting the idea that a single attribute (like knowledge) cannot necessitate two heterogeneous judgments (like knowledge and power), noting that it is false by necessity and consensus. He then distinguishes between cases where the knowledge of two things can be separated and cases where they cannot. In the inseparable case, such as knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge, or knowledge of contradiction (where knowing A's opposition to B entails knowing B's opposition to A), he asserts that a single knowledge may relate to both. The proof is that if someone knows a thing, they necessarily know that they know it; if these were two separate knowledges, it would lead to an infinite regress, as indicated by the illustration of al-Jafr and al-Jami'a. The commentary explains that the author uses the word 'qad' to signal that the claim is a particular affirmative (not a universal one), so a single example suffices. The example of knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge is sufficient; the proof does not need to cover every type of inseparable knowledge. The subsequent commentary addresses objections about the interpretation of 'necessitation' (whether it is ontological or customary) and clarifies that the Ahl al-Sunna allow a single attribute to produce multiple effects customarily. The passage also includes a discussion about the proper formulation of the example and a response to potential misunderstandings. The overall argument establishes the possibility of a single knowledge relating to two known things when the two known things are necessarily connected in knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof must be universal for all types of inseparable knowledge.
Correction: The author clarifies that the claim is a particular affirmative, so one example is sufficient. The proof works for the case of knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge, and it does not need to cover every type, such as knowledge of contradiction or similarity.

Checkpoint

A) It is impossible.
B) It is permissible and even necessary in some cases.
C) It is permissible but only for homogeneous judgments.
D) It is necessary only for knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The first proof uses the necessity of knowing that one knows a thing to show that a single knowledge can relate to two inseparable known things, thereby avoiding an infinite regress of knowledges.

?
The proof shows that infinite regress is absurd. In your spiritual life, are you chasing endless justifications—waiting for more knowledge, a better state, or a sign—instead of resting in the single, certain knowledge that Allah is enough? What would it look like to halt the regress and act on what you already know? Choose one doubt you keep revisiting and confront it with the truth you cannot separate from your soul.

Go to the source of your faith today and re-connect knowledge with its awareness; stop pretending to know what you have not internalized.

Why Infinite Regress Does Not Apply to Relations
The author explains why the infinite regress proof does not apply to relations and clarifies the permissibility claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof from infinite regress does not apply to relations like contradiction, and what al-Ghazali and al-Baqillani's claim actually is.

Core Idea
Infinite regress only arises in knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge, not in other relations.

In the previous lesson, we saw how some scholars argued that a single knowledge cannot relate to two known things based on the impossibility of infinite regress. Here, the author clarifies that this argument only works in a narrow case. For relations like contradiction or similarity, the claim is merely permissibility, not necessity. Let's see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
The author states that the proof from infinite regress does not apply to contradiction and all relations. Understand this as the thesis.
This is a direct counter to extending the proof to all cases.
2
Understand the reason
The infinite regress arises because knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge are multiple in every rank, not because the two known things are opposites or related.
The regress is about the chain of knowledge, not the objects.
3
Grasp the answer
The claim of the Imam and Qadi is merely permissibility in relations; no positive proof is needed—only absence of proof of impossibility.
They distinguish between cases where separation is impossible (like adjacency) and those where it is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And this proof does not apply to contradiction and all relations, because there is no basis for saying: if the knowledge related to the two opposites were not one, infinite regress would follow, because knowledge of a thing implies knowledge of the knowledge of it, and so on, as mentioned there. This is because the occurrence of infinite regress in this way arises only from knowledge of a thing and knowledge of the knowledge of it being multiple in every rank, whether the knowledge of the two opposites is one or multiple, and whether the knowledge considered in the first rank is related to the two relatives or not, like knowledge of Zayd and Amr. The answer is that the claim of the Imam (al-Ghazali) and the Qadi (al-Baqillani) in the case of knowledge of relations is merely the permissibility of a single knowledge relating to two known things, based on the fact that the previous impossibility, namely the separation of a thing from itself, does not follow, as indicated by his words in the glosses on al-Tajrid. So there is no need for the inference in his opinion; rather the absence of a proof of impossibility suffices. He only adduced the proof in the case of knowledge of a thing and knowledge of the knowledge of it to indicate a claim of something extra in this specific case, which is possibility in itself, rather the necessity of the one relating to the multiple. He said in Sharh al-Maqasid in reporting their doctrine: 'As for what cannot be separated, like adjacency, similarity, opposition, and so on, it is permissible for a single knowledge to relate to two known things, and perhaps it is even necessary, as in knowledge of a thing with knowledge of it, for there are information to the end of the proof.' And on this there is no dust in the speech. His saying: (they indicate that it is not complete, and such was the matter, for it is reported that al-Ma'mun experienced slight weakness on a festival day, so he sent for Ali ibn Musa inviting him to the prayer ground, intending to establish among the people his deputyship for him. When he headed to the prayer ground, he said: 'We will do as the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, did,' and he walked barefoot, then began the takbir. When he had gone a distance, many people gathered with him and they said the takbir. Al-Ma'mun heard the commotion and feared him, then ordered his return. Ali ibn Musa, may God have mercy on him, said: 'I knew it would be so.' It is said that he poisoned him after this incident, so he died before al-Ma'mun.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage addresses the second part of the discussion: cases involving relations like contradiction, adjacency, and similarity. The author states that the proof from infinite regress (used for knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge) does not apply to these cases. The reason is that the infinite regress arises specifically from the multiplicity of knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge in each rank—it does not depend on whether the knowledge of the two opposites is one or multiple. For example, knowing Zayd and Amr as two separate individuals does not involve such regress. The claim of al-Ghazali and al-Baqillani regarding relations is only that it is permissible for a single knowledge to relate to two known things, not that it is necessary. The necessity (as in the case of knowledge of a thing and knowledge of knowing it) is an extra feature in that specific case. The passage then quotes Sharh al-Maqasid to confirm that for inseparable relations, a single knowledge may relate to two known things, and sometimes it may even be necessary, as in the case of a thing and knowledge of it. Finally, a historical anecdote about Ali ibn Musa (Imam al-Ridha) and al-Ma'mun is related, indicating that the prediction from al-Jafr and al-Jami'a was not complete, and the story illustrates the point about foreknowledge.

The Necessity of Knowledge of Knowledge and the Infinite Regress
The author establishes the necessity of knowledge of knowledge and demonstrates the infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of a thing necessarily entails knowledge of that knowledge, and how this leads to an infinite regress that must be avoided by allowing a single knowledge to relate to multiple knowns.

Core Idea
If you know, you must know that you know, or an absurd regress follows.

This lesson begins by establishing a fundamental principle: knowing something implies knowing that you know it. The author uses an example from Islamic tradition and then shows how this leads to an infinite regress if a single knowledge cannot cover multiple knowns.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the main claim about the necessity of knowledge of knowledge.
Notice the author's use of the example of al-Jafr and al-Jami'a.
2
Understand the absurdity scenario
The author argues that if knowledge of a thing did not entail knowledge of that knowledge, absurdities would follow, such as knowing without knowing one knows.
This is the 'otherwise' clause.
3
Follow the infinite regress
The passage then shows that if knowledge of a thing requires knowledge of that knowledge, and so on, an infinite chain results.
The author uses 'then he knows his knowledge by his knowledge of it, and so on'.
4
Reflect on the solution
To halt the regress, a single knowledge must relate to multiple knowns, which is the thesis of the chapter.

به بالضرورة وإلا، أي وإن لم يصح ما ذكرناه من استلزام العلم بالشيء العلم بذلك العلم، جاز أن يكون أحدنا عالما بالجفر والجامعة، وهما كتابان لعلي رضي الله تعالى عنه، قد ذكر فيهما على طريقة علم الحروف الحوادث التي تحدث إلى انقراض العالم، وكانت الأئمة المعروفون من أولاده يعرفونهما ويحكمون بهما، وفي كتاب قبول العهد الذي كتبه علي بن موسى رضي الله عنهما إلى المأمون: إنّك قد عرفت من حقوقنا ما لم يعرفه آباؤك، فقبلت منك عهدك. إلا أنّ الجفر والجامعة يدلان على يتم، ولمشايخ المغاربة نصيب من علم الحروف ينتسبون فيه إلى أهل البيت ورأيت أنا بالشام نظماً أشير فيه بالرموز إلى أحوال ملوك مصر، وسمعت أنه مستخرج من ذينك الكتابين. وإن كان أي أحدنا لا يعلم علمه به أي بما علمه من الجفر وقوع التعلق بهما ولعله لرعاية المطابقة لنظيره أعني قوله: لا يجوز تعلقه بمعلومين يجوز انفكاك العلم بهما لكن الظاهر حينئذ فيجوز تعلقه بالفاء ليلائم السابق اللاحق. قوله: ثم إنه يعلم علمه بعلمه به وهلم جراً، فثمة معلومات غير متناهية إلخ، وهذا الدليل لا يجري في التضاد وسائر الإضافات إذ لا وجه لأن يقال: لو لم يكن العلم المتعلق بالمتضادين واحداً لزم التسلسل لأن العلم بالشيء يستلزم العلم بالعلم به إلى آخر ما ذكر هناك، وذلك لأنّ لزوم التسلسل بهذا الطريق، إنما ينشأ من كون العلم بالشيء، والعلم بالعلم به متعددا في كل مرتبة سواء كان العلم بالمتضادين واحداً أو متعدداً، وسواء كان العلم المعتبر في أول المراتب متعلقاً بالمضافين أو لا، كالعلم بزيد وعمرو، والجواب أنّ مدعى الإمام والقاضي في صورة العلم بالإضافات مجرد جواز تعلق علم واحد بمعلومين، بناء على أنّه لا يلزم المحال السابق، أعني انفكاك الشيء عن نفسه كما دل عليه كلامه في حواشي التجريد، فلا يحتاج فيه إلى الاستدلال بزعمه، بل يكتفى بانتفاء دليل الامتناع، وإنّما أورد الدليل في صورة العلم بالشيء، والعلم بالعلم به إشارة إلى ادعاء أمر زائد في هذه الصورة بخصوصها وهو الإمكان بحسب نفس الأمر، بل وجوب تعلق الواحد بالمتعدد. قال في شرح المقاصد في تقرير مذهبهما، وأما فيما لا يجوز الانفكاك كالمجاورة والمماثلة والمضادة وغير ذلك، فيجوز أن يتعلق علم واحد بمعلومين بل ربما يجب كما في العلم بالشيء مع العلم به فإنّ هناك معلومات إلى آخر الدليل وعلى هذا لا غبار في الكلام.
The second type - the second objective: The single knowledge and its relation to two known things. By necessity, otherwise, i.e., if what we mentioned – that knowledge of a thing entails knowledge of that knowledge – is not correct, then it would be possible for one of us to be a knower of al-Jafr and al-Jami'a, which are two books of Ali (may God be pleased with him), in which are mentioned, according to the method of the science of letters, the events that occur until the extinction of the world. The well-known imams among his descendants used to know them and judge by them. And in the book of acceptance of the covenant that Ali ibn Musa (may God be pleased with them both) wrote to al-Ma'mun: 'You have recognized of our rights what your fathers did not recognize, so I accepted your covenant.' However, al-Jafr and al-Jami'a indicate incompleteness. And the scholars of the Maghreb have a portion of the science of letters by which they trace their lineage to the People of the House. And I saw in Syria a poem in which reference is made by symbols to the conditions of the kings of Egypt, and I heard that it is extracted from those two books. Although (one of us) does not know his knowledge of it, i.e., of what he knows from al-Jafr, the occurrence of relation to them, and perhaps for the sake of conformity with its counterpart, namely his statement: 'It is not permissible for it to relate to two known things if the knowledge of them can be separated,' but the apparent meaning then is that it is permissible for it to relate by the letter fa to harmonize the preceding with the following. His statement: 'Then he knows his knowledge by his knowledge of it, and so on, so there are infinite pieces of information, etc.' This proof does not apply to opposition and other relations, for there is no reason to say: 'If the knowledge related to the two opposites were not single, then an infinite regress would follow because knowledge of a thing entails knowledge of the knowledge of it, up to what was mentioned there.' That is because the occurrence of infinite regress in this way arises from the multiplicity of knowledge of the thing and knowledge of the knowledge of it in each rank, whether the knowledge of the two opposites is single or multiple, and whether the knowledge considered in the first rank is related to the two relata or not, like knowledge of Zayd and Amr. The answer is that the claim of al-Imam and al-Qadi in the case of knowledge of relations is merely the permissibility of a single knowledge relating to two known things, based on the fact that the previous impossible consequence – namely, the separation of a thing from itself – does not follow, as indicated by his words in the glosses of al-Tajrid. Thus there is no need for proof in his opinion; rather, it suffices with the absence of a proof of impossibility. Indeed, he adduced the proof in the case of knowledge of the thing and knowledge of the knowledge of it to indicate an additional claim in this particular case, namely possibility according to the matter itself, but rather necessity of the single relating to the multiple. He said in Sharh al-Maqasid in presenting their doctrine: 'As for what cannot be separated, like adjacency, similarity, opposition, and others, it is permissible for a single knowledge to relate to two known things, and perhaps it is necessary, as in the case of knowledge of the thing along with knowledge of it, for there are pieces of information up to the end of the proof.' And on this, there is no dust in the speech.

Commentary
This passage is a complex argumentative passage that defines the problem of a single knowledge relating to multiple knowns. The author begins by stating the necessity of the implication: knowledge of a thing necessarily entails knowledge of that knowledge; otherwise, one could know the books al-Jafr and al-Jami'a without knowing that one knows them, which is absurd. He then references a historical letter from Ali ibn Musa to al-Ma'mun to illustrate that this knowledge is possible. The argument then moves to the infinite regress: if knowledge of a thing implies knowledge of the knowledge, and that implies further knowledge, then an infinite number of knowledges would result. To avoid this, one must allow a single knowledge to cover multiple knowns, thereby stopping the regress. However, the author notes that this argument does not apply in cases of opposites and other relations, because the regress arises from the multiplicity of knowledges regardless of whether the original knowledge is single or multiple. The answer of al-Imam and al-Qadi is that in the case of relations, the claim is only that a single knowledge can relate to two knowns, since the impossibility of a thing separating from itself does not arise. The passage concludes by quoting Sharh al-Maqasid, which states that for inseparable relations like adjacency and opposition, a single knowledge can and sometimes must relate to multiple knowns, as in the case of knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of a thing necessarily includes knowledge of that knowledge, leading to an infinite regress that must be resolved by allowing a single knowledge to cover multiple knowns.

Scope of the Argument: Opposition and Other Relations
The author excludes opposition and relations from the regress argument and gives the ruling of al-Imam and al-Qadi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the infinite regress argument does not apply to relations like opposition, and how al-Imam and al-Qadi argue for the permissibility of a single knowledge relating to two knowns in such cases.

Core Idea
The regress argument fails for relations; only the absence of impossibility matters.

In this lesson, we examine a critical clarification: the infinite regress argument from the previous lesson does not apply to cases of opposition and other relations. The author explains why and presents the position of al-Imam and al-Qadi that a single knowledge can relate to two knowns simply because it is not impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage to see the exclusion
Read the Arabic and translation to understand why the regress argument does not apply to opposites and relations.
The author states 'This proof does not apply to opposition and other relations'.
2
Understand the reason for the exclusion
The regress would arise regardless of whether the knowledge is single or multiple, so the argument is not decisive for relations.
The author explains that the regress arises from the multiplicity of knowledges in each rank.
3
Learn the answer of al-Imam and al-Qadi
They claim mere permissibility: a single knowledge can relate to two knowns because the impossible consequence of a thing separating from itself does not follow.
The answer is based on the absence of a proof of impossibility.
4
Reflect on the quote from Sharh al-Maqasid
For inseparable relations like adjacency and opposition, a single knowledge can and sometimes must relate to multiple knowns.
This solidifies their position.

النوع الثاني - المقصد الثاني : العلم الواحد وتعلقه بمعلومين
به بالضرورة وإلا، أي وإن لم يصح ما ذكرناه من استلزام العلم بالشيء العلم بذلك العلم، جاز أن يكون أحدنا عالما بالجفر والجامعة، وهما كتابان لعلي رضي الله تعالى عنه، قد ذكر فيهما على طريقة علم الحروف الحوادث التي تحدث إلى انقراض العالم، وكانت الأئمة المعروفون من أولاده يعرفونهما ويحكمون بهما، وفي كتاب قبول العهد الذي كتبه علي بن موسى رضي الله عنهما إلى المأمون: إنّك قد عرفت من حقوقنا ما لم يعرفه آباؤك، فقبلت منك عهدك. إلا أنّ الجفر والجامعة يدلان على يتم، ولمشايخ المغاربة نصيب من علم الحروف ينتسبون فيه إلى أهل البيت ورأيت أنا بالشام نظماً أشير فيه بالرموز إلى أحوال ملوك مصر، وسمعت أنه مستخرج من ذينك الكتابين. وإن كان أي أحدنا لا يعلم علمه به أي بما علمه من الجفر وقوع التعلق بهما ولعله لرعاية المطابقة لنظيره أعني قوله: لا يجوز تعلقه بمعلومين يجوز انفكاك العلم بهما لكن الظاهر حينئذ فيجوز تعلقه بالفاء ليلائم السابق اللاحق. قوله: ثم إنه يعلم علمه بعلمه به وهلم جراً، فثمة معلومات غير متناهية إلخ، وهذا الدليل لا يجري في التضاد وسائر الإضافات إذ لا وجه لأن يقال: لو لم يكن العلم المتعلق بالمتضادين واحداً لزم التسلسل لأن العلم بالشيء يستلزم العلم بالعلم به إلى آخر ما ذكر هناك، وذلك لأنّ لزوم التسلسل بهذا الطريق، إنما ينشأ من كون العلم بالشيء، والعلم بالعلم به متعددا في كل مرتبة سواء كان العلم بالمتضادين واحداً أو متعدداً، وسواء كان العلم المعتبر في أول المراتب متعلقاً بالمضافين أو لا، كالعلم بزيد وعمرو، والجواب أنّ مدعى الإمام والقاضي في صورة العلم بالإضافات مجرد جواز تعلق علم واحد بمعلومين، بناء على أنّه لا يلزم المحال السابق، أعني انفكاك الشيء عن نفسه كما دل عليه كلامه في حواشي التجريد، فلا يحتاج فيه إلى الاستدلال بزعمه، بل يكتفى بانتفاء دليل الامتناع، وإنّما أورد الدليل في صورة العلم بالشيء، والعلم بالعلم به إشارة إلى ادعاء أمر زائد في هذه الصورة بخصوصها وهو الإمكان بحسب نفس الأمر، بل وجوب تعلق الواحد بالمتعدد. قال في شرح المقاصد في تقرير مذهبهما، وأما فيما لا يجوز الانفكاك كالمجاورة والمماثلة والمضادة وغير ذلك، فيجوز أن يتعلق علم واحد بمعلومين بل ربما يجب كما في العلم بالشيء مع العلم به فإنّ هناك معلومات إلى آخر الدليل وعلى هذا لا غبار في الكلام.
The second type - the second objective: The single knowledge and its relation to two known things. By necessity, otherwise, i.e., if what we mentioned – that knowledge of a thing entails knowledge of that knowledge – is not correct, then it would be possible for one of us to be a knower of al-Jafr and al-Jami'a, which are two books of Ali (may God be pleased with him), in which are mentioned, according to the method of the science of letters, the events that occur until the extinction of the world. The well-known imams among his descendants used to know them and judge by them. And in the book of acceptance of the covenant that Ali ibn Musa (may God be pleased with them both) wrote to al-Ma'mun: 'You have recognized of our rights what your fathers did not recognize, so I accepted your covenant.' However, al-Jafr and al-Jami'a indicate incompleteness. And the scholars of the Maghreb have a portion of the science of letters by which they trace their lineage to the People of the House. And I saw in Syria a poem in which reference is made by symbols to the conditions of the kings of Egypt, and I heard that it is extracted from those two books. Although (one of us) does not know his knowledge of it, i.e., of what he knows from al-Jafr, the occurrence of relation to them, and perhaps for the sake of conformity with its counterpart, namely his statement: 'It is not permissible for it to relate to two known things if the knowledge of them can be separated,' but the apparent meaning then is that it is permissible for it to relate by the letter fa to harmonize the preceding with the following. His statement: 'Then he knows his knowledge by his knowledge of it, and so on, so there are infinite pieces of information, etc.' This proof does not apply to opposition and other relations, for there is no reason to say: 'If the knowledge related to the two opposites were not single, then an infinite regress would follow because knowledge of a thing entails knowledge of the knowledge of it, up to what was mentioned there.' That is because the occurrence of infinite regress in this way arises from the multiplicity of knowledge of the thing and knowledge of the knowledge of it in each rank, whether the knowledge of the two opposites is single or multiple, and whether the knowledge considered in the first rank is related to the two relata or not, like knowledge of Zayd and Amr. The answer is that the claim of al-Imam and al-Qadi in the case of knowledge of relations is merely the permissibility of a single knowledge relating to two known things, based on the fact that the previous impossible consequence – namely, the separation of a thing from itself – does not follow, as indicated by his words in the glosses of al-Tajrid. Thus there is no need for proof in his opinion; rather, it suffices with the absence of a proof of impossibility. Indeed, he adduced the proof in the case of knowledge of the thing and knowledge of the knowledge of it to indicate an additional claim in this particular case, namely possibility according to the matter itself, but rather necessity of the single relating to the multiple. He said in Sharh al-Maqasid in presenting their doctrine: 'As for what cannot be separated, like adjacency, similarity, opposition, and others, it is permissible for a single knowledge to relate to two known things, and perhaps it is necessary, as in the case of knowledge of the thing along with knowledge of it, for there are pieces of information up to the end of the proof.' And on this, there is no dust in the speech.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a complex argumentative passage that defines the problem of a single knowledge relating to multiple knowns. The author begins by stating the necessity of the implication: knowledge of a thing necessarily entails knowledge of that knowledge; otherwise, one could know the books al-Jafr and al-Jami'a without knowing that one knows them, which is absurd. He then references a historical letter from Ali ibn Musa to al-Ma'mun to illustrate that this knowledge is possible. The argument then moves to the infinite regress: if knowledge of a thing implies knowledge of the knowledge, and that implies further knowledge, then an infinite number of knowledges would result. To avoid this, one must allow a single knowledge to cover multiple knowns, thereby stopping the regress. However, the author notes that this argument does not apply in cases of opposites and other relations, because the regress arises from the multiplicity of knowledges regardless of whether the original knowledge is single or multiple. The answer of al-Imam and al-Qadi is that in the case of relations, the claim is only that a single knowledge can relate to two knowns, since the impossibility of a thing separating from itself does not arise. The passage concludes by quoting Sharh al-Maqasid, which states that for inseparable relations like adjacency and opposition, a single knowledge can and sometimes must relate to multiple knowns, as in the case of knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of al-Imam and al-Qadi regarding a single knowledge relating to two knowns in cases of relations?
A) It is impossible.
B) It is necessary.
C) It is permissible.
D) It is prohibited.

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress argument is specific to knowledge of knowledge; for relations like opposition, a single knowledge can relate to two knowns simply because no impossibility prevents it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you explain your faith to others, do you assume that one argument fits all situations, or do you carefully consider where each proof applies? This passage shows that even a strong proof like the regress has its proper domain. Are you willing to check your reasoning against the actual teachings, even if it means admitting you were wrong?
?
Think of a moment when you were certain that a simple logical rule solved a complex problem about Allah or His attributes. How does this lesson's distinction between knowledge of a thing and knowledge of relations challenge your confidence? Will you now look more carefully at whether the arguments you use actually apply to the case at hand?

The Infinite Regress Problem and the Role of Attention
Regress argument and attention-based response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the infinite regress of knowledges is considered impossible and how the mind's attention can stop the regress.

Core Idea
Knowledge does not automatically entail knowledge of itself unless the mind attends to it.

The opponents argue that knowing something leads to an infinite chain of knowledges, which is impossible. But is it necessary to always be aware of your knowledge? The author argues that we can know things without being aware of that knowledge unless we turn our attention to it. This attention is not continuous, so the regress is avoided.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation to understand the regress argument and the response.
Notice the structure: first the problem, then the answer.
2
Identify the regress argument
The passage begins by stating that knowing something entails knowing that knowledge, and so on, leading to infinite knowledges. This is presented as an impossibility.
The arguer claims this is a problem for any theory that allows multiple knowledges.
3
Understand the response: attention
The author denies that knowledge of a thing always entails knowledge of that knowledge. He says we can know without knowing we know, unless the mind attends to the knowledge. This attention can cease, cutting off the regress.
The key is that attention is not constantly present.
4
Reflect on why this solves the regress
If attention is not continuous, then the chain of knowledges does not extend infinitely in actuality. The regress is only potential, not actual, so it is not a problem.

فظهر أنّ من علم شيئاً علم علمه به ( ثم ) ( يعلم ) أيضاً ( علمه بعلمه به لما ذكرناه من استلزام العلم بالعلم به ( وهلم جراً، فثمة معلومات غير متناهية فلو لم يجز أن تكون عدة من هذه المعلومات معلومة بعلم واحد، بل استدعى كل معلوم ) منها (علماً ) على حدة، (لزم أن يكون لأحدنا) إذا علم شيئاً واحداً ) علوم غير متناهية بالفعل وأنّه محال والوجدان يحققه) أي يشهد بكونه محالاً ) والجواب : إِنّا لا نسلم أنّ العلم بالشيء يستلزم العلم بذلك العلم، إذ ( قد نعلم الشيء ولا نعلم العلم به إلا إذا التفت الذهن إليه )، لما مر من أن الموجود في الذهن لا يمكن أن يحكم عليه من حيث هو موجود فيه إلا بأن يتصور مرة ثانية، ويلتفت إليه من حيث أنه في الذهن ( و ) هذا الالتفات لا يمكن أن يستمر حتى يلزم علوم غير متناهية بل ( ينقطع بانقطاع الاعتبار، ولا فرق في ذلك بين معلوم واحد ومعلومات جمة، إذ يجوز الغفلة عن العلم في الكل، ولكن لما كان الالتفات إلى العلم قريباً من الحصول غير محتاج إلى تكلف ظن أنه حاصل بالفعل عليه ما بني. (وأما قول من قال : ) يعني به الآمدي فإنه قال في الجواب : الكلام إنما هو في جواز تعلق العلم الواحد بمعلومين ) والعلم لا يتعلق بنفسه لأن النسبة ) التي هي التعلق لا تتصور إلا بين شيئين متغايرين ولا مغايرة بين الشيء ونفسه وقول القائل : ذات الشيء ونفسه يوهم بظاهره نسبة الشيء إلى نفسه إلا أنه مجاز لا حقيقة له ومعنى كون الواحد منا عالما بعلمه لا يزيد على قيام علمه بنفسه ( فظاهر البطلان لأنّ تعلق العلم بالعلم ليس من قبيل تعلق الشيء بنفسه، بل من قبيل تعلق وبنى قوله : ( يوهم بظاهره) إنّما قال ذلك لأنّه في الحقيقة من إضافة العام إلى الخاص . قوله : ( إنّه مجاز لا حقيقة له فإنَّ المراد منهما لا غير الشيء كما في ما يقوم بنفسه أي لا يقوم بغيره . قوله : ( فثمة معلومات غير متناهية هي معلومات علمية، فلا بد أن يجوز تعلق علم منها باثنين حتى ينقطع التسلسل في درجة قيل على تقدير جواز تعلق علم واحد بمتعدد يلزم علوم غير متناهية ،أيضاً لأنّ العلم المتعلق بأشياء متعددة يتعلق به علم آخر على تقدير لزوم العلم بالعلم وهلم جراً. والجواب أولاً : جواز أن يتعلق العلم بنفسه حينئذ إذ يكفي فيه تغاير اعتباري كما صرح به في أواخر بحث العلم من الإلهيات . وثانياً جواز أن يتعلق العلم المعلوم بعلمه . قوله: (وأما قول من قال إلخ حاصل كلام الآمدي منع أن طريق معلومية العلم تعلقه بنفسه بل العلم به علم حضوري فلا يلزم تسلسل وإن لم يجوز تعلق علم واحد بمتعدد، وقد يدفع دليله بكفاية التغاير الاعتباري أيضاً كما أشرنا إليه.
So it appears that whoever knows a thing knows his knowledge of it, then he also knows his knowledge by his knowledge of it, because of what we mentioned of the entailment of knowledge of the knowledge of it, and so on. So there are infinite pieces of information. If it were not permissible for several of these pieces of information to be known by a single knowledge, but rather each piece of information required a separate knowledge, then it would be necessary for each one of us, if he knows a single thing, to have infinite actual knowledges, which is impossible, and intuition confirms it, i.e., it witnesses that it is impossible. The answer: We do not grant that knowledge of a thing entails knowledge of that knowledge, for we may know a thing and not know the knowledge of it unless the mind attends to it, because of what has passed: that what exists in the mind cannot be judged insofar as it exists in it except by being imagined a second time and attending to it insofar as it is in the mind. And this attention cannot continue such that infinite knowledges result, but rather it is cut off by the cutting off of consideration. There is no difference in this between a single known and many knowns, for it is permissible to be heedless of knowledge in all cases. But because attention to knowledge is close to attainment and does not require effort, it was thought that it is actually attained, upon which [the argument] is built. As for the statement of one who said – meaning al-Amidi – he said in response: The discussion is only about the permissibility of a single knowledge relating to two known things, and knowledge does not relate to itself because the relation, which is the attachment, is only conceivable between two distinct things, and there is no distinction between a thing and itself. The saying of one who says 'the essence of the thing and itself' apparently suggests a relation of the thing to itself, but it is a metaphor, not a reality. The meaning of one of us being knowledgeable of his knowledge is nothing more than the subsistence of his knowledge in itself. So it is evidently false, because the relation of knowledge to knowledge is not of the type of relation of a thing to itself, but rather of the type of relation... And he built his statement: 'It apparently suggests' – he said that only because in reality it is an attribution of the general to the specific. His statement: 'It is a metaphor, not a reality' – for what is meant by the two is nothing other than the thing, as in what subsists by itself, i.e., it does not subsist by another. His statement: 'So there are infinite pieces of information that are scientific pieces of information, so it must be permissible that a knowledge of them relates to two so that the regress is cut off at a certain degree.' It is said: on the assumption that a single knowledge can relate to multiple, then infinite knowledges would also result, because the knowledge related to multiple things is related to another knowledge on the assumption of the entailment of knowledge of knowledge, and so on. The answer: First, it is permissible for knowledge to relate to itself in that case, for conceptual distinction suffices, as stated at the end of the discussion of knowledge in metaphysics. Second, it is permissible for the known knowledge to relate to its knowledge. His statement: 'As for the statement of one who said, etc.' – the gist of al-Amidi's speech is to deny that the way of the knowability of knowledge is its relation to itself; rather, knowledge of it is presential knowledge (ilm huduri), so no regress follows even if a single knowledge relating to multiple is not allowed. And his proof can be repelled by the sufficiency of conceptual distinction, as we indicated.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part response to the infinite regress argument. It begins by restating the problem: if knowing a thing entails knowing the knowledge of it, and so on, then infinite knowledges result, which is impossible. The first response denies the entailment: we often know a thing without knowing our knowledge of it unless the mind turns its attention to the knowledge. Since attention can cease, the regress is cut off. This applies whether the known is one or many. The passage then presents al-Amidi's objection: the discussion is about a single knowledge relating to two knowns, but knowledge cannot relate to itself because relation requires two distinct things. The apparent relation of knowledge to itself is metaphorical. The author then counters by introducing the concept of conceptual distinction (taghayur i'tibari), which allows knowledge to relate to itself. A further objection is raised: even if one knowledge relates to multiple, the regress continues because that knowledge itself would require another knowledge. The answer is twofold: either knowledge can relate to itself by conceptual distinction, or the known knowledge can relate to its own knowledge. Finally, the author summarizes that al-Amidi's denial of the regress is based on the idea that knowledge of knowledge is presential (huduri), not relational, but this can be refuted by the sufficiency of conceptual distinction.

According to the author, why does the infinite regress not actually occur?
A) Because knowledge of knowledge is always present but not noticed.
B) Because the mind must attend to the knowledge, and attention can cease.
C) Because infinite regress is logically impossible from the start.
D) Because knowledge cannot relate to itself at all.

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress of knowledges is avoided because knowledge of a thing does not automatically give knowledge of that knowledge; the mind must actively attend, and that attention can be discontinued.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume you are aware of your own knowledge when in fact you are heedless? Identify one moment today where you knew something but were not consciously attending to that knowledge. What would change if you deliberately turned your attention to it?
?
The author says attention to knowledge is close to attainment and does not require effort, yet we often think it is always present. In your own life, when has heedlessness about your own beliefs led you to act wrongly? Commit to one specific practice of turning attention to what you claim to know, especially in matters of faith.
?
If the infinite regress were real, you could never know anything — but the author says attention cuts it off. What does this reveal about your responsibility to actively attend to your knowledge of Allah? Are you attending, or are you content with mere knowing? What will you do today to turn your mind toward what you already know about your Lord?

Al-Amidi's Objection and the Solution of Conceptual Distinction
Al-Amidi's objection and conceptual distinction response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's objection that knowledge cannot relate to itself and how the concept of 'conceptual distinction' (taghayur i'tibari) provides a solution.

Core Idea
Even for self-relation, conceptual distinction suffices to avoid requiring two really distinct things.

Al-Amidi raises a powerful objection: if knowledge were to relate to itself, that would require a real distinction between the knower and the known, which is impossible for a single thing. The author responds by introducing 'conceptual distinction' (taghayur i'tibari): even without real separation, the mind can distinguish aspects, allowing knowledge to 'relate' to itself. This preserves the possibility of self-knowledge without regress.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Amidi's objection
The passage presents al-Amidi's claim that the discussion is about a single knowledge relating to two knowns, and that knowledge cannot relate to itself because relation requires two distinct things. The saying 'the essence of the thing and itself' is merely metaphorical.
Al-Amidi thinks self-relation is impossible on a real distinction basis.
2
Understand the author's response: conceptual distinction
The author counters that conceptual distinction (taghayur i'tibari) is sufficient for a relation. Even without real difference, the mind can consider the same thing under two aspects, allowing a relation.
This is a key concept in Islamic philosophy and kalam.
3
Consider the further objection and double answer
A further objection is raised: even if one knowledge relates to multiple, the regress continues because that knowledge itself would need another knowledge. The author gives two answers: (1) knowledge can relate to itself via conceptual distinction, or (2) the known knowledge can relate to its knowledge.
Both answers rely on conceptual distinction.
4
Reflect on al-Amidi's alternative and its refutation
Al-Amidi suggests that knowledge of knowledge is presential (huduri), not relational, but the author argues that even presential knowledge can be analyzed with conceptual distinction, thus the objection is not decisive.

فظهر أنّ من علم شيئاً علم علمه به ( ثم ) ( يعلم ) أيضاً ( علمه بعلمه به لما ذكرناه من استلزام العلم بالعلم به ( وهلم جراً، فثمة معلومات غير متناهية فلو لم يجز أن تكون عدة من هذه المعلومات معلومة بعلم واحد، بل استدعى كل معلوم ) منها (علماً ) على حدة، (لزم أن يكون لأحدنا) إذا علم شيئاً واحداً ) علوم غير متناهية بالفعل وأنّه محال والوجدان يحققه) أي يشهد بكونه محالاً ) والجواب : إِنّا لا نسلم أنّ العلم بالشيء يستلزم العلم بذلك العلم، إذ ( قد نعلم الشيء ولا نعلم العلم به إلا إذا التفت الذهن إليه )، لما مر من أن الموجود في الذهن لا يمكن أن يحكم عليه من حيث هو موجود فيه إلا بأن يتصور مرة ثانية، ويلتفت إليه من حيث أنه في الذهن ( و ) هذا الالتفات لا يمكن أن يستمر حتى يلزم علوم غير متناهية بل ( ينقطع بانقطاع الاعتبار، ولا فرق في ذلك بين معلوم واحد ومعلومات جمة، إذ يجوز الغفلة عن العلم في الكل، ولكن لما كان الالتفات إلى العلم قريباً من الحصول غير محتاج إلى تكلف ظن أنه حاصل بالفعل عليه ما بني. (وأما قول من قال : ) يعني به الآمدي فإنه قال في الجواب : الكلام إنما هو في جواز تعلق العلم الواحد بمعلومين ) والعلم لا يتعلق بنفسه لأن النسبة ) التي هي التعلق لا تتصور إلا بين شيئين متغايرين ولا مغايرة بين الشيء ونفسه وقول القائل : ذات الشيء ونفسه يوهم بظاهره نسبة الشيء إلى نفسه إلا أنه مجاز لا حقيقة له ومعنى كون الواحد منا عالما بعلمه لا يزيد على قيام علمه بنفسه ( فظاهر البطلان لأنّ تعلق العلم بالعلم ليس من قبيل تعلق الشيء بنفسه، بل من قبيل تعلق وبنى قوله : ( يوهم بظاهره) إنّما قال ذلك لأنّه في الحقيقة من إضافة العام إلى الخاص . قوله : ( إنّه مجاز لا حقيقة له فإنَّ المراد منهما لا غير الشيء كما في ما يقوم بنفسه أي لا يقوم بغيره . قوله : ( فثمة معلومات غير متناهية هي معلومات علمية، فلا بد أن يجوز تعلق علم منها باثنين حتى ينقطع التسلسل في درجة قيل على تقدير جواز تعلق علم واحد بمتعدد يلزم علوم غير متناهية ،أيضاً لأنّ العلم المتعلق بأشياء متعددة يتعلق به علم آخر على تقدير لزوم العلم بالعلم وهلم جراً. والجواب أولاً : جواز أن يتعلق العلم بنفسه حينئذ إذ يكفي فيه تغاير اعتباري كما صرح به في أواخر بحث العلم من الإلهيات . وثانياً جواز أن يتعلق العلم المعلوم بعلمه . قوله: (وأما قول من قال إلخ حاصل كلام الآمدي منع أن طريق معلومية العلم تعلقه بنفسه بل العلم به علم حضوري فلا يلزم تسلسل وإن لم يجوز تعلق علم واحد بمتعدد، وقد يدفع دليله بكفاية التغاير الاعتباري أيضاً كما أشرنا إليه.
So it appears that whoever knows a thing knows his knowledge of it, then he also knows his knowledge by his knowledge of it, because of what we mentioned of the entailment of knowledge of the knowledge of it, and so on. So there are infinite pieces of information. If it were not permissible for several of these pieces of information to be known by a single knowledge, but rather each piece of information required a separate knowledge, then it would be necessary for each one of us, if he knows a single thing, to have infinite actual knowledges, which is impossible, and intuition confirms it, i.e., it witnesses that it is impossible. The answer: We do not grant that knowledge of a thing entails knowledge of that knowledge, for we may know a thing and not know the knowledge of it unless the mind attends to it, because of what has passed: that what exists in the mind cannot be judged insofar as it exists in it except by being imagined a second time and attending to it insofar as it is in the mind. And this attention cannot continue such that infinite knowledges result, but rather it is cut off by the cutting off of consideration. There is no difference in this between a single known and many knowns, for it is permissible to be heedless of knowledge in all cases. But because attention to knowledge is close to attainment and does not require effort, it was thought that it is actually attained, upon which [the argument] is built. As for the statement of one who said – meaning al-Amidi – he said in response: The discussion is only about the permissibility of a single knowledge relating to two known things, and knowledge does not relate to itself because the relation, which is the attachment, is only conceivable between two distinct things, and there is no distinction between a thing and itself. The saying of one who says 'the essence of the thing and itself' apparently suggests a relation of the thing to itself, but it is a metaphor, not a reality. The meaning of one of us being knowledgeable of his knowledge is nothing more than the subsistence of his knowledge in itself. So it is evidently false, because the relation of knowledge to knowledge is not of the type of relation of a thing to itself, but rather of the type of relation... And he built his statement: 'It apparently suggests' – he said that only because in reality it is an attribution of the general to the specific. His statement: 'It is a metaphor, not a reality' – for what is meant by the two is nothing other than the thing, as in what subsists by itself, i.e., it does not subsist by another. His statement: 'So there are infinite pieces of information that are scientific pieces of information, so it must be permissible that a knowledge of them relates to two so that the regress is cut off at a certain degree.' It is said: on the assumption that a single knowledge can relate to multiple, then infinite knowledges would also result, because the knowledge related to multiple things is related to another knowledge on the assumption of the entailment of knowledge of knowledge, and so on. The answer: First, it is permissible for knowledge to relate to itself in that case, for conceptual distinction suffices, as stated at the end of the discussion of knowledge in metaphysics. Second, it is permissible for the known knowledge to relate to its knowledge. His statement: 'As for the statement of one who said, etc.' – the gist of al-Amidi's speech is to deny that the way of the knowability of knowledge is its relation to itself; rather, knowledge of it is presential knowledge (ilm huduri), so no regress follows even if a single knowledge relating to multiple is not allowed. And his proof can be repelled by the sufficiency of conceptual distinction, as we indicated.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part response to the infinite regress argument. It begins by restating the problem: if knowing a thing entails knowing the knowledge of it, and so on, then infinite knowledges result, which is impossible. The first response denies the entailment: we often know a thing without knowing our knowledge of it unless the mind turns its attention to the knowledge. Since attention can cease, the regress is cut off. This applies whether the known is one or many. The passage then presents al-Amidi's objection: the discussion is about a single knowledge relating to two knowns, but knowledge cannot relate to itself because relation requires two distinct things. The apparent relation of knowledge to itself is metaphorical. The author then counters by introducing the concept of conceptual distinction (taghayur i'tibari), which allows knowledge to relate to itself. A further objection is raised: even if one knowledge relates to multiple, the regress continues because that knowledge itself would require another knowledge. The answer is twofold: either knowledge can relate to itself by conceptual distinction, or the known knowledge can relate to its own knowledge. Finally, the author summarizes that al-Amidi's denial of the regress is based on the idea that knowledge of knowledge is presential (huduri), not relational, but this can be refuted by the sufficiency of conceptual distinction.

Key Terms
تعلیق
تغاير اعتباري
علم حضوري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that conceptual distinction is merely a sophistical trick with no reality.
Correction: Conceptual distinction (taghayur i'tibari) is a recognized logical tool in Islamic thought, allowing valid distinctions without real separation.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to al-Amidi's claim that knowledge cannot relate to itself?
A) He denies that relation requires two distinct things.
B) He argues that knowledge is presential, not relational.
C) He introduces conceptual distinction as sufficient for self-relation.
D) He accepts that self-relation is impossible and offers an alternative.

Key Takeaway
Conceptual distinction (taghayur i'tibari) is a powerful tool that allows self-relation in knowledge, thereby answering al-Amidi's objection and solving the regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own self-awareness, do you ever sense a split between the 'knower' and the 'known' within you? This lesson shows that such a split may be merely conceptual, not real. How does that change your understanding of your own identity?
?
If your knowledge of yourself is ultimately a relation of the same thing to itself, what does that imply about the stability of your self-knowledge? Are you willing to accept that you cannot truly distance yourself from yourself?
?
The argument here relies on conceptual distinction to solve a logical puzzle. But in your own life, do you ever conflate conceptual distinctions with real separations, leading to unnecessary doubts or conflicts? How might this lesson help you distinguish between the two?

Can One Knowledge Know Multiple Objects? The Foundational Distinction
Al-Razi and al-Iji discuss whether one knowledge can relate to multiple knowns, distinguishing definitions and possibility types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of knowledge (as relation or attribute) determines whether one knowledge can relate to multiple objects, and the crucial distinction between mental and external possibility.

Core Idea
The unity of knowledge depends on its definition.

One of the most debated questions in Islamic epistemology is whether a single act of knowledge can encompass multiple distinct objects. This debate hinges on the very definition of knowledge. In this passage, al-Razi and al-Iji lay out the key positions and distinctions that clarify the issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow of the argument. Do not try to analyze yet.
Notice the structure: al-Razi's opinion, then al-Iji's clarification, then a proof, then an objection and answer.
2
Identify al-Razi's conditional
Al-Razi states two definitions of knowledge. Compare the implications: if knowledge is the relation itself, one knowledge cannot relate to multiple; if it is an attribute with relation, it can. Identify the key phrase 'نفس التعلق' vs 'صفة ذات تعلق'.
3
Grasp al-Iji's distinction
Al-Iji distinguishes mental possibility (the intellect does not judge impossibility) from external possibility (actual possibility). Understand why mental possibility is undisputed but external possibility is not.
This is a crucial nuance in kalam methodology.
4
Analyze the proof from opposition
Read the proof that knowledge of specific opposition requires knowledge of both relata. See how it argues against a single knowledge relating to multiple objects in detail.
The proof assumes detailed knowledge of each particular.
5
Understand the objection and answer
The objection from Naqd al-Muhassal uses the example of knowledge of a whole. The answer clarifies that the dispute is about detailed knowledge (الخصوصيات). See how the answer preserves the original position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام الرازي : والمختار عندي أن الخلاف متفرع على تفسير العلم فإن قلنا : إنّه نفس التعلق، فلا شك أن التعلق بهذا غير التعلق بذاك، فلا يتعلق علم واحد بمعلومين وإن قلنا : إنّه صفة ذات تعلق جاز أن يكون العلم صفة واحدة يتعدد تعلقاته وكثرة التعلقات الخارجة عن حقيقة الصفة لا تجعل الصفة متكثرة في ذاتها قال المصنف : واعلم أن الجواز الذهني لا نزاع فيه و الجواز الخارجي مما يناقش فيه يعني أنا إذا نظرنا إلى أن العلم صفة ذات تعلق جوز العقل أن تكون هذه الصفة واحدة شخصية متعلقة بأمور أن العقل بمجرد هذه الملاحظة لا يحكم بامتناع تعلق علم واحد بمعلومين وهذا هو المسمى بالإمكان الذهني وليس يلزم منه الإمكان بحسب نفس الأمر لجواز أن يكون ممتنعاً في نفسه لكن العقل لم يطلع على وجه استحالته والاستدلال على إمكانه في نفسه بأن العلم المتعلق بكون السواد مضاداً للبياض إن لم يكن هو بعينه متعلقاً بهما لم يمكن متعلقاً بالمضادة التي بينهما، بل بمطلق المضادة وكلامنا في المضادة المخصوصة مفهوم متعلق بهما والعلم بها موقوف على العلم بهما معاً، فليس هناك علم واحد علم به معلومان وفي نقد المحصل أن العلم إذا فسر بالتعلق جاز تعدد المعلوم مع وحدة العلم، كما إذا علم مجموع من حيث هو هو، فإن الأجزاء داخلة فيه والجواب ما مر من أن الخلاف في تعلق العلم الواحد بمتعدد على سبيل التفصيل بأن يكون متعلقاً بخصوصية هذا وخصوصية ذاك معاً، فإنه جوزه جماعة كثيرة وليس العلم المتعلق بالمجموع من هذا القبيل.
Imam al-Razi said: The chosen opinion with me is that the disagreement branches on the interpretation of knowledge. For if we say that it is the relation itself, then there is no doubt that the relation to this is other than the relation to that, so one knowledge cannot be related to two knowns. And if we say that it is an attribute having relation, then it is permissible that knowledge be one attribute with multiple relations, and the multiplicity of relations external to the essence of the attribute does not make the attribute multiple in its essence. The author said: Know that mental possibility is not disputed, but external possibility is debated. Meaning, when we consider that knowledge is an attribute having relation, the intellect permits that this attribute be one individual related to things; the intellect, by mere observation, does not judge the impossibility of one knowledge being related to two knowns. This is called mental possibility. It does not entail possibility in reality, for it may be impossible in itself but the intellect has not discovered the aspect of its impossibility. The proof of its possibility in itself is that if the knowledge related to blackness being opposite to whiteness is not itself related to both, then it cannot be related to the opposition between them, but only to absolute opposition. Our discussion is about specific opposition, a concept related to both, and knowledge of it depends on knowledge of both together. So there is not one knowledge relating to two knowns. In Naqd al-Muhassal: If knowledge is interpreted as relation, then multiplicity of the known with unity of knowledge is permissible, as when one knows a whole as such, for the parts are included in it. The answer is as previously stated: the disagreement concerns one knowledge relating to multiple things in detail, such that it relates to the particularity of this and the particularity of that together. A large group permitted that, and knowledge of a whole is not of this type.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents the discussion on whether a single knowledge (ʿilm) can relate to multiple known objects (maʿlūmāt). The argument is a definition-based proof. It begins by citing Imam al-Razi, who states that the disagreement depends on the definition of knowledge. If knowledge is purely the relation itself (nafs al-taʿalluq), then each relation is distinct, so one knowledge cannot relate to multiple objects. If knowledge is an attribute that has relation (ṣifa dhāt taʿalluq), then it is possible for one attribute to have multiple relations without multiplying the attribute's essence. The author (al-Iji) then introduces a distinction between mental possibility (al-jawāz al-dhihnī) and external possibility (al-jawāz al-khārijī). Mental possibility is undisputed: the intellect does not see an immediate impossibility in one knowledge relating to multiple objects. However, this does not imply actual possibility, because the thing may be inherently impossible though unrecognized. The passage then gives a specific proof: knowledge of the opposition between blackness and whiteness requires knowledge of both relata. If that knowledge is not itself related to both, it would only be knowledge of absolute opposition, not specific opposition. Thus, there is no single knowledge relating to two distinct objects. The passage references Naqd al-Muhassal, where an objection is raised: if knowledge is defined as relation, knowing a whole (e.g., a set) would involve one knowledge relating to multiple parts. The answer is that the disagreement concerns detailed knowledge (ʿalā sabīl al-tafṣīl) where the knowledge relates to the particularities of each object individually. Knowledge of a whole as a whole does not involve such detailed relation, so it is not a counterexample.

Key Terms
علم
تعلق
معلوم
جواز ذهني
جواز خارجي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental possibility (jawāz dhihnī) implies actual possibility (jawāz khārijī).
Correction: The text explicitly states that mental possibility does not entail possibility in reality; something may be impossible in itself even if the intellect has not identified the reason.

Checkpoint
What is the key factor that determines whether one knowledge can relate to multiple knowns, according to the passage?
A) The number of known objects
B) The definition of knowledge as relation or attribute
C) The classification of knowledge as necessary or acquired
D) The distinction between mental and external possibility

Key Takeaway
The possibility of one knowledge relating to multiple objects depends on the definition of knowledge and involves a distinction between mental and external possibility; the dispute concerns detailed knowledge of each particular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever confidently asserted something is true simply because you could not see a contradiction in it? What does this lesson's distinction between mental and external possibility teach you about intellectual humility before theological claims?
?
Imam al-Razi shows that the disagreement hinges on the definition of knowledge. In your own understanding of your faith, what core concepts (like knowledge, belief, or existence) have you assumed you properly define? Could your mistakes in these definitions lead you to wrong conclusions about Allah or His attributes?
?
The passage reveals that a single knowledge may relate to multiple objects only if it relates to them as a whole, not in their detailed particularity. How do you approach holding multiple ideas about the same subject? Do you ever oversimplify or conflate them, and how might that affect your worship of Allah who knows all things in their full detail?

The Identity of Knowledge and Compound Ignorance
Al-Jurjani states the identity thesis and responds to the first objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core argument that knowledge and compound ignorance are identical in essence and differ only by accidents, and how al-Amidi responds to the objection based on most specific attributes.

Core Idea
Unity in essence allows accidental differences without contradiction.

Al-Jurjani begins by presenting the central claim: knowledge and compound ignorance share the same essence and differ only by accidental attributes. This sets up a key debate in Islamic epistemology. The companions raise an objection using the notion of most specific attributes, which al-Amidi answers by distinguishing between essential and accidental attributes. This lesson unpacks that foundational exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the original argument
Start by reading the passage where al-Jurjani states that compound ignorance is an accident with unity of essence, and that similarity allows transformation because of accidental differences.
Note the contrast with opposites and those differing in reality.
2
Identify the objection
Find where 'the companions' argue that correspondence and non-correspondence are the most specific attributes, so difference in them implies difference in essence.
This objection challenges the idea that accidental difference is sufficient.
3
Study al-Amidi's response
Al-Amidi argues that sharing the most specific attribute of the soul is what matters for similarity, and knowledge has an attribute that compound ignorance lacks: attainment by correct reasoning.
He uses the example of the imitator's belief to illustrate similarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني - المقصد الثالث : الجهل المركب عارض مع اتحاد الذات، والحقيقة في ذينك الشيئين فيكونان متماثلين انقلب أحدهما إلى الآخر بسبب اختلاف العوارض ولا استحالة فيه بخلاف المتضادين والمتخالفين في الحقيقة فإن الانقلاب بينهما يفضي إلى انقلاب الحقائق وهو محال، وأيضاً قد ثبت في المثال المذكور اتحاد العلم والجهل المركب في الذات، فلا يكون الاختلاف إلا بالعوارض ( وقال الأصحاب : ( في جوابهم بطريق المعارضة ( المطابقة واللامطابقة أخص صفاتهما ) ، أي صفات العلم والجهل المركب ( فيلزم من الاختلاف فيه، أي في أخص الصفات الاختلاف في الذات) لما مَرَّ المتماثلين ما يشتركان في أخص صفات النفس، وأجاب الآمدي بعبارة أخرى وهي : إنّ الاشتراك في الأخص المعتبر في التماثل يستلزم الاشتراك في الأعم، ومن صفات العلم حصوله بالنظر الصحيح، وذلك غير متصور في الجهل المركب بالاتفاق، فلا يكون مثلاً للعلم، قال : واتفق الكل على أن اعتقاد المقلد للشيء على ما هو عليه مثل للعلم.

أنَّ
قوله : ( وهو محال فيه أنَّ الانقلاب المحال : انقلاب كل من الواجب والممكن والممتنع إلى آخر لا انقلاب حقيقة ممكنة إلى أخرى فإن العناصر ينقلب بعضها إلى بعض . قوله: (وأيضاً إلخ) فحينئذ لا حاجة إلى قوله والانقلاب لا يتصور إلا في أمر عارض مع اتحاد الذات .
قوله : (أخص صفاتهما) إذ تينك الصفتين امتازا عن جميع أنواع الإدراك .
قوله : ( ومن صفات العلم إلخ) فيه أنَّ الحصول بالنظر الصحيح ليس صفة للعلم مطلقاً، بل للعلم النظري، فلا يلزم من التماثل بين العلم الجهل المركب اشتراكهما فيما هو صفة للعلم النظري .
قوله : ( واتفق إلخ) مع حصول العلم بالنظر الصحيح دون اعتقاد المقلد .
قوله : ( وأيضاً قد ثبت إلخ) يعني : إِنَّ اتحاد الذات في الصورة المفروضة قد ثبت بالضرورة الوجدانية وإن قطعنا النظر عن لزوم الانقلاب على تقدير عدمه. قوله : (إلا بالعوارض وتلك العارضة ليست إلا المطابقة فالوجه الثاني عائد إلى الأول وجزئي من جزئياته .
قوله : ( في جوابهم بطريق المعارضة وأما جوابهم بطريق حل دليلهم المبني على امتناع انقلاب الحقائق فهو : إِنَّ انقلاب الحقائق الممتنع عند المحققين هو الثاني واحد من الواجب والممكن والممتنع إلى الآخر صرح به في التلويح ومن البين أنه لم يلزم هذا ذكره على تقدير اختلاف العلم والجهل المركب في الماهية.
قوله: (أخص صفاتهما قد يمنع ذلك بتجويز كونهما من الصفات المعنوية ولا يخفى بعده . قوله : ( ومن صفات العلم حصوله بالنظر الصحيح) أي من صفات العلم النظري ويحتمل
The second type - third objective: compound ignorance. An accident with the unity of essence, and the reality in those two things, so they become similar; one of them turns into the other due to difference of accidents, and there is no impossibility in it, unlike the opposites and those differing in reality, for transformation between them leads to transformation of realities, which is impossible. And also it has been established in the mentioned example the unity of knowledge and compound ignorance in essence, so difference is only by accidents. And the companions said, in their answer by way of opposition: correspondence and non-correspondence are the most specific attributes of the two, i.e., the attributes of knowledge and compound ignorance, so from the difference in them, i.e., in the most specific attributes, follows difference in essence, because similar things share in the most specific attributes of the soul. And al-Amidi answered with another expression, which is: sharing in the most specific considered in similarity necessitates sharing in the more general. And from the attributes of knowledge is its attainment by correct reasoning, and that is not conceivable in compound ignorance by agreement, so it is not a likeness of knowledge. He said: And all agreed that the belief of the imitator of a thing as it is is a likeness of knowledge. From that: His saying: 'And it is impossible' includes that the impossible transformation is the transformation of each of the necessary, possible, and impossible into another, not the transformation of a possible reality into another, for elements transform into each other. His saying: 'And also etc.' So then there is no need for his saying: 'And transformation is only conceivable in an accident with the unity of essence.' His saying: 'The most specific attributes of the two' since those two attributes are distinguished from all types of perception. His saying: 'And from the attributes of knowledge etc.' includes that attainment by correct reasoning is not an attribute of knowledge absolutely, but of theoretical knowledge, so similarity between knowledge and compound ignorance does not necessitate their sharing in what is an attribute of theoretical knowledge. His saying: 'And agreed etc.' with the attainment of knowledge by correct reasoning without the belief of the imitator. His saying: 'And also it has been established etc.' means: indeed the unity of essence in the supposed case has been established by immediate necessity even if we disregard the necessity of transformation on the assumption of its absence. His saying: 'Only by accidents' and those accidents are only correspondence, so the second aspect returns to the first and is a part of its parts. His saying: 'In their answer by way of opposition' and as for their answer by way of solving their argument based on the impossibility of transformation of realities, it is: indeed the impossible transformation of realities according to the verifiers is the second, one of the necessary, possible, and impossible into another, as stated in al-Talwih, and it is clear that this did not occur on the assumption of difference of knowledge and compound ignorance in quiddity. His saying: 'The most specific attributes of the two' may be prevented by allowing them to be among the intellectual attributes, and its remoteness is not hidden. His saying: 'And from the attributes of knowledge its attainment by correct reasoning' i.e., from the attributes of theoretical knowledge, and it is probable...
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the proof that knowledge and compound ignorance are identical in essence. The author first states the original argument: they share the same subject (essence) and differ only by accidents (attributes), so transformation between them is possible and not impossible as in the case of opposites. Then an objection is raised by 'the companions' via opposition: the most specific attributes (correspondence and non-correspondence) are different, so the essences must differ. Al-Amidi responds that sharing in the most specific attributes is not always required; similarity requires sharing in the most specific of the soul's attributes, but knowledge has the attribute of 'attainment by correct reasoning', which compound ignorance lacks, so they are not similar. The author then addresses further points: the impossibility of transformation applies only to essential changes (necessary to possible etc.), not to accidental changes like elements. He clarifies that the unity of essence is established by immediate necessity, and the accidents in question are only correspondence or non-correspondence, so the second aspect (difference in attributes) reduces to the first (difference in accidents). Finally, he notes that the opponents' argument about impossibility of transformation of realities is answered because the impossible transformation is only from one category to another, not from one specific reality to another within the same category. The passage ends with a comment that the claim that the most specific attributes are intellectual may be prevented, but it is unlikely.

النظر الصحيح
التماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any difference in attributes means difference in essence.
Correction: Accidental differences (like correspondence) do not affect essential identity; only essential attributes define similarity.

Checkpoint
Why do the companions argue that knowledge and compound ignorance must differ in essence?
A) Because they have different names
B) Because their most specific attributes (correspondence/non-correspondence) are different
C) Because one is knowledge and the other is ignorance
D) Because al-Amidi said so

Key Takeaway
Knowledge and compound ignorance are identical in essence and differ only by accidental attributes; objections based on most specific attributes are answered by al-Amidi's distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If knowledge and compound ignorance are essentially the same, what does that say about your own certainties that might be based on imitation rather than proof? What specific belief of yours are you afraid to test with correct reasoning? Commit to examining one such belief this week.
?
Al-Amidi says the imitator's belief is a 'likeness of knowledge' because it represents truth. But is that enough for God's acceptance? Look at your own heart: Are you content with a mere representation of faith, or do you hunger for the certainty that comes from reasoned conviction? What step will you take today to move from imitation to verification?
?
The passage emphasizes that transformation from ignorance to knowledge is possible because they share the same essence. What deep-seated ignorance in your life have you been avoiding transforming? What would it cost you to seek the truth, and are you willing to pay that price?

Clarifying Transformation and Immediate Necessity
Al-Jurjani clarifies the meaning of impossible transformation and asserts unity by immediate necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the clarification about impossibility of transformation and the argument that the unity of essence is established by immediate necessity.

Core Idea
Impossible transformation is only between categories, not within a single essence.

After the initial exchange, al-Jurjani addresses further objections regarding the possibility of transformation and the basis for unity. He distinguishes between impossible transformation (changing from necessary to possible, etc.) and permissible transformation within the same essence. He also asserts that the unity of essence is known by immediate necessity, not merely by argument. This lesson clarifies these subtle points.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of transformation
Read the passage where al-Jurjani explains that the impossible transformation is one of essential categories (e.g., necessary to possible), not the transformation of one possible reality into another (like elements).
This addresses the objection that transformation would lead to impossibility.
2
Understand the role of immediate necessity
Notice that al-Jurjani says the unity of essence is established by immediate necessity, not just by reasoning about transformation.
This strengthens the argument by grounding it in direct intuition.
3
Examine the reduction of the second aspect
The passage concludes that difference in accidents (correspondence) is the only difference, so the second aspect (difference in most specific attributes) reduces to the first.
This unifies the two objections into one.
