The Second Stage of Intellect: Definition and Conditions
Al-Jurjani defines the second stage and explains conditions for necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the second stage of intellect (al-ʿaql bi al-malaka) and the conditions required for necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge can be absent due to missing conditions of sense or conception.

In this passage, al-Jurjani defines the second stage of intellect, known as the intellect in habitu. He clarifies that 'necessary sciences' do not mean all necessities; some may be absent due to missing conditions for conception or assent. This lesson unpacks those conditions and explains why even axioms can be lost if their conditions are not met.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the second stage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note that 'necessary sciences' refers to universal forms and assenting judgments flowing from the Principle.
The author immediately qualifies that not all necessities are necessarily known.
2
Identify the two categories of conditions
The passage lists conditions for conception (sense and inner sense) and for assent (sense and inner experience). Pay attention to the examples: a blind person cannot conceive color, an impotent person cannot conceive the pleasure of copulation.
3
Focus on the condition for axioms
For axioms (al-badīhiyyāt), the condition is conceiving the two extremes and the relation. Understand why this is the strongest and least conditional type of necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرتبة الثانية: العقل بالملكة وهو أن يفيض عليها من المبدأ صور كلية وأحكام تصديقية فيما بينها، فهذه علوم ضرورية ) ولا نريد بذلك أي العلم بالضروريات العلم) بجميع الضروريات فإن الضروريات قد تفقد ) إما ( لفقد شرط للتصور كحس ووجدان كالأكمه ) الفاقد للعين في أصل الخلقة ( والعنين) الفاقد لقوة المجامعة ( لا يتصور أنَّ ماهية اللون ) التي يتوصل إلى إدراكها بأبصار جزئياتها . ( و ) ماهية ( لذة الجماع ) التي يتوصل إلى إدراكها بوجدان جزئياتها ( أو ) لفقد شرط ( للتصديق كأحدهما ) أي الحس والوجدان ( في القضايا الحسية) فإن فاقد حس من الحواس فاقد للقضايا المستندة إلى ذلك الحس، (أو) القضايا الوجدانية) فإن فاقد الوجدان فاقد لها قطعاً (وكتصور ( الطرفين هذا عطف على قوله : كأحدهما . فإن تصور الطرفين ( والنسبة ) شرط (في ) البديهيات) أي الأوليات التي هي أقوى الضروريات وأقلها شرطاً، فإذا فقد هذا الشرط فقدت القضايا البديهية، فضلا عما عداها من الضروريات المتوقفة على شروط أخر أيضاً . المرتبة الثالثة: العقل بالفعل وهو ملكة استنباط النظريات من الضروريات ( أي صيرورة الشخص ) بحيث متى شاء استحضر الضروريات ) ولاحظها ) واستنتج منها النظريات ولا شك أن هذه الحالة إنما تحصل له إذا صار طريقة الاستنباط ملكة راسخة فيه ( وقيل : ( ليس العقل بالفعل ما ذكر (بل) هو ما اشتهر من أنه ( حصول النظريات) وصيرورتها بعد استنتاجها من الضروريات بحيث يستحضرها متى شاء بلا (روية وتجشم كسب جديد وذلك إنما يحصل إذا لاحظ النظريات الحاصلة مرة بعد أخرى حتى يحصل له ملكة نفسانية يقوى بها على استحضارها متى أراد من غير حاجة إلى فكر . المرتبة الرابعة : العقل المستفاد وهو أن يحضر عنده النظريات ) التي أدركها ( بحيث لا تغيب عنه وهل يمكن ذلك ؟ ) أي حضورها بأسرها مشاهدة للقوة العاقلة الإنسانية ) والإنسان في جلباب من بدنه أم لا يمكن؟ فيه (تردد) إذ يجوز عند العقل أن يتجرد بعض النفوس الكاملة عن العلائق البدنية تجرداً تاماً بحيث تشاهد معقولاتها دفعة واحدة، كأنها لمعة برق، ثم تترقى عن هذه الحالة إلى مشاهدة بعد مشاهدة، وهكذا حتى تصير المشاهدة ملكة
قوله : ( ملكة راسخة بخلاف المرتبة الثانية، فإن فيها ملكة تستعد بها للاستنباط . قوله : ( يقوى بها على استحضارها إلخ) فالمرتبة الثالثة ملكة استحضار النظريات الحاصلة بلا تجشم كسب جديد .
قوله : ( وهل يمكن ذلك قد مر في أول الكتاب ما فيه سؤلاً وجواباً فلينظر فيه .
قوله : ( في الدار الآخرة أي بعد المفارقة عن البدن كما هو مصطلح الحكماء. قوله : ( بالقياس الى كل نظري ) فيجوز اجتماع المراتب الأربع في شخص واحد . قوله : ( ولا شبهة في وقوعه إنما الشبهة في بقائه لأن الاشتغال بتدبير البدن يعوقها لامتناع توجه النفس إلى أمرين في هذه النشأة .
The second stage: the intellect in habitu (al-ʿaql bi al-malaka), which is that universal forms and assenting judgments flow upon it from the Principle; these are necessary sciences. But we do not mean by that the knowledge of all necessities, for necessities may be lacking, either due to loss of a condition for conception, like sense and inner sense, like the blind (who lacks the eye in original creation) and the impotent (who lacks the power of copulation) cannot conceive the quiddity of color (which is reached by seeing its particulars) and the quiddity of the pleasure of copulation (which is reached by experiencing its particulars), or due to loss of a condition for assent, like one of them (i.e., sense and inner sense) in sensory propositions, for one lacking a sense lacks propositions based on that sense, or inner experiential propositions, for one lacking inner experience certainly lacks them. And like the conception of the two extremes—this is an apposition to His saying: 'like one of them'—for the conception of the two extremes and the relation is a condition in axioms (al-badīhiyyāt), i.e., the primary axioms which are the strongest of necessities and least conditional; if this condition is lost, the axiomatic propositions are lost, let alone other necessities that depend on additional conditions. The third stage: the actual intellect (al-ʿaql bi al-fiʿl), which is the faculty of deducing theoretical matters from necessities—that is, the person becomes such that whenever he wishes, he brings to mind the necessities, observes them, and deduces theoretical matters from them. There is no doubt that this state occurs only if the method of deduction becomes a firmly rooted faculty in him. And it is said: the actual intellect is not what was mentioned, but rather what is well-known: that it is the acquisition of theoretical matters and their becoming, after deduction from necessities, such that he brings them to mind whenever he wishes without deliberation and the effort of new acquisition. That only occurs if he observes the theoretical matters obtained repeatedly until he acquires a psychic faculty by which he is able to bring them to mind whenever he wishes without need for reflection. The fourth stage: the acquired intellect (al-ʿaql al-mustafād), which is that the theoretical matters he has perceived are present with him such that they are not absent from him. And is that possible?—i.e., their entire presence as an intuition for the human rational faculty while man is in the garment of his body—or is it not possible? There is hesitation. For it is rationally permissible that some perfect souls detach completely from bodily attachments such that they intuit their intelligibles all at once, like a flash of lightning, then ascend from this state to intuition after intuition, and so on until intuition becomes a faculty.
His saying: 'a firmly rooted faculty'—unlike the second stage, for in it there is a faculty by which one prepares for deduction. His saying: 'by which he is able to bring them to mind, etc.'—so the third stage is a faculty of bringing to mind the theoretical matters obtained without the effort of new acquisition.
His saying: 'And is that possible?'—It has already passed at the beginning of the book what contains a question and answer, so let it be examined there.
His saying: 'in the afterlife'—that is, after separation from the body, as is the terminology of the philosophers. His saying: 'in relation to each theoretical matter'—so it is permissible for the four stages to coexist in one person. His saying: 'And there is no doubt about its occurrence'—doubt is only about its persistence, because preoccupation with managing the body hinders it, due to the impossibility of the soul attending to two matters in this worldly existence.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage defines the second, third, and fourth stages of intellect according to the philosophers, and discusses the possibility of the fourth. The author begins with the second stage (al-ʿaql bi al-malaka), describing it as the intellect having universal forms and assenting judgments that flow from the First Principle. He clarifies that 'necessary sciences' here does not mean knowledge of all necessities, because necessities can be absent due to missing conditions. For conception, conditions like sense and inner sense are needed; for example, a blind person cannot conceive color, and an impotent person cannot conceive the pleasure of copulation. For assent, conditions like the presence of sense or inner experience are needed. Even for axioms (the most basic necessities), one needs to conceive the two extremes and the relation. If these are lacking, the axioms are lost. Then he moves to the third stage, the actual intellect, which is the faculty of deducing theoretical matters from necessities. He presents two interpretations: one is that it is a firmly rooted habit of deduction, and the other (more famous) is that it is the acquired theoretical matters being present in mind without need for new effort, after repeated observation. The fourth stage is the acquired intellect, where theoretical matters are constantly present. He questions whether this is possible while the soul is still in the body, and says there is hesitation, as some perfect souls may temporarily detach and intuit all intelligibles at once, like a flash of lightning. The marginal notes further explain: the third stage is a faculty for bringing to mind already acquired knowledge without new effort; the fourth stage's possibility in this life is debated, but its persistence is unlikely due to bodily distractions. The note also mentions that the four stages can coexist in the same person with respect to different theoretical matters.

Key Terms
العقل بالملكة
ضروريات
تصور
تصديق
بديهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary sciences are known by all rational beings at all times
Correction: The passage explains that necessities can be absent due to missing conditions such as lack of sense, inner sense, or conception of terms.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for knowing axioms (al-badīhiyyāt) according to this passage?
A) Possession of the intellect in habitu
B) Conception of the two extremes and the relation
C) Having all five senses
D) Prior acquisition of theoretical matters

Key Takeaway
The second stage is the possession of universal forms and assenting judgments, but not all necessities are known unless the necessary conditions for conception and assent are met.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you assumed that your knowledge of certain religious truths is complete? How does Al-Jurjani's reminder that necessary knowledge can be absent due to missing conditions challenge your certainty?
?
What condition (sense, inner experience, or conception) might you be neglecting in your pursuit of understanding certain aspects of faith? How will you actively seek to fulfill that condition?
?
The passage teaches that even axioms require conception of the two extremes and relation. When you think about fundamental beliefs like the existence of God, have you truly conceived the terms and their relation, or have you merely accepted them without genuine understanding? What will you do to ensure your belief is built on proper conception?

The Third Stage: Two Interpretations of Actual Intellect
Al-Jurjani presents two definitions of the third stage (actual intellect) and distinguishes it from the second stage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of the actual intellect (al-ʿaql bi al-fiʿl) and how it differs from the second stage.

Core Idea
Actual intellect is either a faculty for deduction or the habit of having theoretical knowledge ready without effort.

This passage presents two interpretations of the third stage, the actual intellect. The first defines it as the faculty of deducing theoretical matters from necessities, while the second (more famous) defines it as the acquisition of theoretical matters such that they can be recalled without renewed effort. The commentary clarifies the distinction between the second and third stages.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the third stage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the author first gives one definition, then presents an alternative ('and it is said').
The first definition focuses on the process of deduction; the second on the result.
2
Compare the two interpretations
Identify the key difference: the first emphasizes the habit of deduction, while the second emphasizes the ability to recall already deduced theoretical matters without new effort.
The marginal note clarifies that the third stage is a faculty for bringing to mind already acquired theoretical knowledge.
3
Distinguish from the second stage
The second stage is the possession of necessary sciences, while the third stage adds the ability to derive theoretical knowledge. The margin explicitly contrasts the two stages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرتبة الثانية: العقل بالملكة وهو أن يفيض عليها من المبدأ صور كلية وأحكام تصديقية فيما بينها، فهذه علوم ضرورية ) ولا نريد بذلك أي العلم بالضروريات العلم) بجميع الضروريات فإن الضروريات قد تفقد ) إما ( لفقد شرط للتصور كحس ووجدان كالأكمه ) الفاقد للعين في أصل الخلقة ( والعنين) الفاقد لقوة المجامعة ( لا يتصور أنَّ ماهية اللون ) التي يتوصل إلى إدراكها بأبصار جزئياتها . ( و ) ماهية ( لذة الجماع ) التي يتوصل إلى إدراكها بوجدان جزئياتها ( أو ) لفقد شرط ( للتصديق كأحدهما ) أي الحس والوجدان ( في القضايا الحسية) فإن فاقد حس من الحواس فاقد للقضايا المستندة إلى ذلك الحس، (أو) القضايا الوجدانية) فإن فاقد الوجدان فاقد لها قطعاً (وكتصور ( الطرفين هذا عطف على قوله : كأحدهما . فإن تصور الطرفين ( والنسبة ) شرط (في ) البديهيات) أي الأوليات التي هي أقوى الضروريات وأقلها شرطاً، فإذا فقد هذا الشرط فقدت القضايا البديهية، فضلا عما عداها من الضروريات المتوقفة على شروط أخر أيضاً . المرتبة الثالثة: العقل بالفعل وهو ملكة استنباط النظريات من الضروريات ( أي صيرورة الشخص ) بحيث متى شاء استحضر الضروريات ) ولاحظها ) واستنتج منها النظريات ولا شك أن هذه الحالة إنما تحصل له إذا صار طريقة الاستنباط ملكة راسخة فيه ( وقيل : ( ليس العقل بالفعل ما ذكر (بل) هو ما اشتهر من أنه ( حصول النظريات) وصيرورتها بعد استنتاجها من الضروريات بحيث يستحضرها متى شاء بلا (روية وتجشم كسب جديد وذلك إنما يحصل إذا لاحظ النظريات الحاصلة مرة بعد أخرى حتى يحصل له ملكة نفسانية يقوى بها على استحضارها متى أراد من غير حاجة إلى فكر . المرتبة الرابعة : العقل المستفاد وهو أن يحضر عنده النظريات ) التي أدركها ( بحيث لا تغيب عنه وهل يمكن ذلك ؟ ) أي حضورها بأسرها مشاهدة للقوة العاقلة الإنسانية ) والإنسان في جلباب من بدنه أم لا يمكن؟ فيه (تردد) إذ يجوز عند العقل أن يتجرد بعض النفوس الكاملة عن العلائق البدنية تجرداً تاماً بحيث تشاهد معقولاتها دفعة واحدة، كأنها لمعة برق، ثم تترقى عن هذه الحالة إلى مشاهدة بعد مشاهدة، وهكذا حتى تصير المشاهدة ملكة
قوله : ( ملكة راسخة بخلاف المرتبة الثانية، فإن فيها ملكة تستعد بها للاستنباط . قوله : ( يقوى بها على استحضارها إلخ) فالمرتبة الثالثة ملكة استحضار النظريات الحاصلة بلا تجشم كسب جديد .
قوله : ( وهل يمكن ذلك قد مر في أول الكتاب ما فيه سؤلاً وجواباً فلينظر فيه .
قوله : ( في الدار الآخرة أي بعد المفارقة عن البدن كما هو مصطلح الحكماء. قوله : ( بالقياس الى كل نظري ) فيجوز اجتماع المراتب الأربع في شخص واحد . قوله : ( ولا شبهة في وقوعه إنما الشبهة في بقائه لأن الاشتغال بتدبير البدن يعوقها لامتناع توجه النفس إلى أمرين في هذه النشأة .
The second stage: the intellect in habitu (al-ʿaql bi al-malaka), which is that universal forms and assenting judgments flow upon it from the Principle; these are necessary sciences. But we do not mean by that the knowledge of all necessities, for necessities may be lacking, either due to loss of a condition for conception, like sense and inner sense, like the blind (who lacks the eye in original creation) and the impotent (who lacks the power of copulation) cannot conceive the quiddity of color (which is reached by seeing its particulars) and the quiddity of the pleasure of copulation (which is reached by experiencing its particulars), or due to loss of a condition for assent, like one of them (i.e., sense and inner sense) in sensory propositions, for one lacking a sense lacks propositions based on that sense, or inner experiential propositions, for one lacking inner experience certainly lacks them. And like the conception of the two extremes—this is an apposition to His saying: 'like one of them'—for the conception of the two extremes and the relation is a condition in axioms (al-badīhiyyāt), i.e., the primary axioms which are the strongest of necessities and least conditional; if this condition is lost, the axiomatic propositions are lost, let alone other necessities that depend on additional conditions. The third stage: the actual intellect (al-ʿaql bi al-fiʿl), which is the faculty of deducing theoretical matters from necessities—that is, the person becomes such that whenever he wishes, he brings to mind the necessities, observes them, and deduces theoretical matters from them. There is no doubt that this state occurs only if the method of deduction becomes a firmly rooted faculty in him. And it is said: the actual intellect is not what was mentioned, but rather what is well-known: that it is the acquisition of theoretical matters and their becoming, after deduction from necessities, such that he brings them to mind whenever he wishes without deliberation and the effort of new acquisition. That only occurs if he observes the theoretical matters obtained repeatedly until he acquires a psychic faculty by which he is able to bring them to mind whenever he wishes without need for reflection. The fourth stage: the acquired intellect (al-ʿaql al-mustafād), which is that the theoretical matters he has perceived are present with him such that they are not absent from him. And is that possible?—i.e., their entire presence as an intuition for the human rational faculty while man is in the garment of his body—or is it not possible? There is hesitation. For it is rationally permissible that some perfect souls detach completely from bodily attachments such that they intuit their intelligibles all at once, like a flash of lightning, then ascend from this state to intuition after intuition, and so on until intuition becomes a faculty.
His saying: 'a firmly rooted faculty'—unlike the second stage, for in it there is a faculty by which one prepares for deduction. His saying: 'by which he is able to bring them to mind, etc.'—so the third stage is a faculty of bringing to mind the theoretical matters obtained without the effort of new acquisition.
His saying: 'And is that possible?'—It has already passed at the beginning of the book what contains a question and answer, so let it be examined there.
His saying: 'in the afterlife'—that is, after separation from the body, as is the terminology of the philosophers. His saying: 'in relation to each theoretical matter'—so it is permissible for the four stages to coexist in one person. His saying: 'And there is no doubt about its occurrence'—doubt is only about its persistence, because preoccupation with managing the body hinders it, due to the impossibility of the soul attending to two matters in this worldly existence.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage defines the second, third, and fourth stages of intellect according to the philosophers, and discusses the possibility of the fourth. The author begins with the second stage (al-ʿaql bi al-malaka), describing it as the intellect having universal forms and assenting judgments that flow from the First Principle. He clarifies that 'necessary sciences' here does not mean knowledge of all necessities, because necessities can be absent due to missing conditions. For conception, conditions like sense and inner sense are needed; for example, a blind person cannot conceive color, and an impotent person cannot conceive the pleasure of copulation. For assent, conditions like the presence of sense or inner experience are needed. Even for axioms (the most basic necessities), one needs to conceive the two extremes and the relation. If these are lacking, the axioms are lost. Then he moves to the third stage, the actual intellect, which is the faculty of deducing theoretical matters from necessities. He presents two interpretations: one is that it is a firmly rooted habit of deduction, and the other (more famous) is that it is the acquired theoretical matters being present in mind without need for new effort, after repeated observation. The fourth stage is the acquired intellect, where theoretical matters are constantly present. He questions whether this is possible while the soul is still in the body, and says there is hesitation, as some perfect souls may temporarily detach and intuit all intelligibles at once, like a flash of lightning. The marginal notes further explain: the third stage is a faculty for bringing to mind already acquired knowledge without new effort; the fourth stage's possibility in this life is debated, but its persistence is unlikely due to bodily distractions. The note also mentions that the four stages can coexist in the same person with respect to different theoretical matters.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The actual intellect is simply the possession of theoretical knowledge
Correction: The passage distinguishes between having the faculty of deduction (first interpretation) and having the theoretical knowledge readily recallable without effort (second interpretation). Simply knowing the theoretical matters is not sufficient; one must have the ability to bring them to mind easily.

Key Takeaway
The actual intellect is either a deeply rooted habit of deduction or a psychic faculty that allows immediate recall of theoretical matters without effort.

The Fourth Stage: The Acquired Intellect and Its Possibility
Al-Jurjani defines the fourth stage and discusses its possibility in this life and the afterlife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the fourth stage (al-ʿaql al-mustafād) and the debate about whether it can occur while the soul is still in the body.

Core Idea
The acquired intellect is constant presence of theoretical truths, but its full realization may only be possible after death.

In this passage, al-Jurjani defines the fourth stage, the acquired intellect, as the state where theoretical matters are constantly present and never absent. He then raises the question of whether this is possible while the soul is still in the body, noting hesitation. The marginal notes clarify that some perfect souls may experience temporary flashes, but lasting presence is usually for the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the fourth stage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the acquired intellect is described as the perpetual presence of theoretical intelligibles.
The definition does not specify conditions yet; it is a goal state.
2
Examine the question of possibility
The author asks whether this constant presence is possible while the soul is in the body. He says there is hesitation (taraddud).
The margin references an earlier discussion in the book for more detail.
3
Consider the examples of perfect souls
The passage mentions that some perfect souls may temporarily detach from the body and intuit all intelligibles at once, like a flash of lightning.
This suggests that partial realization is possible in this life, but full permanence is not.
4
Review the marginal notes on afterlife and coexistence
The margins clarify that it is possible in the afterlife, and that the four stages can coexist in one person regarding different theoretical matters.
Also, the doubt is about persistence, not occurrence.

The second stage: the intellect in habitu (al-ʿaql bi al-malaka), which is that universal forms and assenting judgments flow upon it from the Principle; these are necessary sciences. But we do not mean by that the knowledge of all necessities, for necessities may be lacking, either due to loss of a condition for conception, like sense and inner sense, like the blind (who lacks the eye in original creation) and the impotent (who lacks the power of copulation) cannot conceive the quiddity of color (which is reached by seeing its particulars) and the quiddity of the pleasure of copulation (which is reached by experiencing its particulars), or due to loss of a condition for assent, like one of them (i.e., sense and inner sense) in sensory propositions, for one lacking a sense lacks propositions based on that sense, or inner experiential propositions, for one lacking inner experience certainly lacks them. And like the conception of the two extremes—this is an apposition to His saying: 'like one of them'—for the conception of the two extremes and the relation is a condition in axioms (al-badīhiyyāt), i.e., the primary axioms which are the strongest of necessities and least conditional; if this condition is lost, the axiomatic propositions are lost, let alone other necessities that depend on additional conditions. The third stage: the actual intellect (al-ʿaql bi al-fiʿl), which is the faculty of deducing theoretical matters from necessities—that is, the person becomes such that whenever he wishes, he brings to mind the necessities, observes them, and deduces theoretical matters from them. There is no doubt that this state occurs only if the method of deduction becomes a firmly rooted faculty in him. And it is said: the actual intellect is not what was mentioned, but rather what is well-known: that it is the acquisition of theoretical matters and their becoming, after deduction from necessities, such that he brings them to mind whenever he wishes without deliberation and the effort of new acquisition. That only occurs if he observes the theoretical matters obtained repeatedly until he acquires a psychic faculty by which he is able to bring them to mind whenever he wishes without need for reflection. The fourth stage: the acquired intellect (al-ʿaql al-mustafād), which is that the theoretical matters he has perceived are present with him such that they are not absent from him. And is that possible?—i.e., their entire presence as an intuition for the human rational faculty while man is in the garment of his body—or is it not possible? There is hesitation. For it is rationally permissible that some perfect souls detach completely from bodily attachments such that they intuit their intelligibles all at once, like a flash of lightning, then ascend from this state to intuition after intuition, and so on until intuition becomes a faculty.

His saying: 'by which he is able to bring them to mind, etc.'—so the third stage is a faculty of bringing to mind the theoretical matters obtained without the effort of new acquisition.

His saying: 'in the afterlife'—that is, after separation from the body, as is the terminology of the philosophers. His saying: 'And there is no doubt about its occurrence'—doubt is only about its persistence, because preoccupation with managing the body hinders it, due to the impossibility of the soul attending to two matters in this worldly existence.

Proof that 'Aql is 'Ilm and Refutation of Objection
Proof that 'aql is 'ilm with objection and answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that the intellect ('aql) is identical to knowledge ('ilm) and the objection that distinct things can be inseparable.

Core Idea
The intellect is not all knowledge, only necessary knowledge, and distinct things can be inseparable.

In this passage, the author presents a rigorous proof that the intellect is not a separate faculty from knowledge, but is identical to knowledge of necessary truths. He then addresses a powerful objection that distinct things can coexist inseparably, using the example of a substance and its location. Imam al-Razi's view is also cited.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof that 'aql is 'ilm
Notice the conditional argument: if 'aql were other than 'ilm, separation would be possible. But it's impossible for a rational being to lack knowledge, and for a knower to lack intellect. Therefore, they are identical.
2
Understand why 'aql is not theoretical knowledge
Theoretical knowledge requires perfection of 'aql, which itself depends on 'aql, causing a two-step posteriority.
3
Learn that 'aql is knowledge of necessary truths (but not all)
Some necessary knowledge may be absent due to missing conditions, so 'aql is knowledge of some necessary truths.
4
Examine the objection and its answer
The objector says distinct things can be inseparable, citing substance and location. The author refutes the objection by showing the argument's flaw.
5
Consider al-Razi's alternative view
Al-Razi suggests 'aql is an innate disposition that precedes knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما ذكره ( بأنه ( أي العقل ( ليس غير العلم وإلا جاز تصور (انفكاكها ) إما من الجانبين أو من أحدهما ) وهو محال إذ يمتنع عاقل لا علم له أصلاً أو عالم لا عقل له أصلاً فثبت أن العقل هو العلم (وليس) العقل ( العلم بالنظريات (لأنه أي العلم بالنظريات مشروط بكمال العقل وكمال العقل مشروط بالعقل ( فيكون) العلم بالنظريات ) متأخراً عن العقل بمرتبتين فلا يكون نفسه، فهو ) أي العقل هو ( العلم بالضروريات وليس ) العقل ( علماً بكلها ) أي بكل الضروريات ) فإن العاقل قد يفقد بعضها كما ذكرنا في المقصد العاشر من أن الضروريات قد تفقد لفقد شرط من شرائطها ، فهو ) العلم ببعضها وهو المطلوب، وجوابه: إنا لا نسلم أنه لو كان العقل ( غير العلم جاز الانفكاك ( بينهما (لجواز تلازمهما فإن المتغايرين قد يتلازمان بحيث يمتنع الانفكاك بينهما مطلقاً كالجوهر والحصول في الحيز فإنهما متغايران ولا محال للانفكاك بينهما قال الإمام الرازي : والظاهر أنه ( أي العقل ( غريزة يتبعها العلم
What he mentioned: that it (i.e., the intellect) is nothing other than knowledge; otherwise, conceptual separation of them would be possible, either from both sides or from one side. But this is impossible, for it is impossible that there be a rational being with no knowledge at all, or a knower with no intellect at all. So it is established that the intellect is knowledge. And the intellect is not knowledge of theoretical matters, because knowledge of theoretical matters is conditional upon perfection of the intellect, and perfection of the intellect is conditional upon the intellect. Thus knowledge of theoretical matters would be posterior to the intellect by two degrees, so it cannot be identical to it. Therefore, the intellect is knowledge of necessary things. And the intellect is not knowledge of all of them (i.e., all necessary things), for the rational person may lack some of them, as we mentioned in the tenth topic, that necessary things may be lost due to loss of a condition. So it is knowledge of some of them, and that is what is sought. And its answer: we do not grant that if the intellect were other than knowledge, separation between them would be possible, because they may be necessarily coexistent. For two distinct things may be such that separation between them is absolutely impossible, like substance and being in space, for they are distinct and separation between them is not impossible. Imam al-Razi said: The apparent is that it (the intellect) is an innate disposition followed by knowledge.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the intellect ('aql) is not something other than knowledge ('ilm), and then responds to an objection. The proof proceeds as follows: If 'aql were not identical to 'ilm, then it would be conceivable that they could exist separately—either both existing independently (separation from both sides) or one without the other (separation from one side). This is impossible because (1) a rational being with no knowledge at all cannot exist (a rational being necessarily has some knowledge), and (2) a knower with no intellect at all cannot exist (knowledge requires intellect). Therefore, 'aql is 'ilm. The proof then argues that 'aql is not knowledge of theoretical matters (nazariyyat), because theoretical knowledge depends on the perfection of 'aql, and that perfection depends on 'aql itself, creating a circularity; theoretical knowledge would thus be two degrees removed from 'aql, so it cannot be identical to 'aql. Hence, 'aql is knowledge of necessary truths (daruriyyat), but not all of them, since a person may lack some necessary knowledge due to missing a condition. Thus 'aql is knowledge of some necessary truths—the sought conclusion. The objection is then presented: The opponent says that even if 'aql were other than knowledge, separation is not necessarily possible, because two distinct things can be inseparably coexistent, like a substance and its occupation of space; they are distinct yet cannot be separated. Al-Razi's opinion is then cited: 'aql is an innate disposition (ghariza) that is followed by knowledge.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things are distinct, they can always exist separately.
Correction: They may be necessarily coexistent, like a substance and its location.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why is 'aql not knowledge of theoretical matters?
A) Because theoretical knowledge is not known by everyone
B) Because theoretical knowledge depends on perfection of 'aql which depends on 'aql, making it posterior by two degrees
C) Because theoretical knowledge is not necessary
D) Because theoretical knowledge is not part of 'aql's definition

Key Takeaway
The intellect is identified with knowledge of necessary truths, not all knowledge, and distinct things can be inseparable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your intellect is only knowledge of necessary truths, how much of your daily thinking is built on necessary truths that you have neglected to reflect upon? What are the foundational beliefs that you hold without proof, and have you ever examined them to see if they are truly necessary?
?
The passage argues that a rational being cannot exist without knowledge. Have you ever considered that your very rationality depends on the knowledge of certain truths? What are the necessary truths that you must hold to be rational? And are you living in accordance with them?
?
Al-Razi suggests that the intellect is an innate disposition followed by knowledge. When was the last time you acted purely on your innate disposition without overthinking? Are there areas of your life where you have allowed theoretical reasoning to overshadow the simple necessary truths that should guide you?

The Proof that Intellect is Knowledge
Al-Jurjānī argues that intellect is essentially knowledge of necessary truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that intellect is identical to knowledge and why it must be defined as knowledge of some necessary truths rather than theoretical knowledge.

Core Idea
Intellect is not other than knowledge, otherwise separation would be impossible.

Al-Jurjānī begins by stating that intellect is the basis of obligation by consensus. He then provides a proof that intellect is nothing other than knowledge, arguing that if they were separate, we could conceive of a rational being without knowledge, which is impossible. He further refines this to say intellect is knowledge of necessary truths, not theoretical knowledge, to avoid circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the proof
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the initial consensus statement and the proof that intellect must be identical to knowledge because separation is inconceivable.
2
Understand the impossibility argument
The proof says: if intellect were other than knowledge, we could imagine a rational being with no knowledge or a knower with no intellect. Both are impossible, so intellect must be knowledge.
This is a 'reductio ad absurdum' argument.
3
Examine the refinement regarding theoretical knowledge
The passage then argues that intellect cannot be knowledge of theoretical matters because that would create a circular dependency (theoretical knowledge depends on perfection of intellect, which depends on intellect).
Thus, intellect must be knowledge of necessary truths.
4
Note the final definition
The conclusion is that intellect is knowledge of some necessary truths (not all), because a rational being may lack some necessary truths due to missing conditions.
This matches what was mentioned in the tenth purpose.

النوع الثاني - المقصد الحادي عشر : العقل مناط التكليف إجماعاً
ما ذكره ( بأنه ( أي العقل ( ليس غير العلم وإلا جاز تصور (انفكاكها ) إما من الجانبين أو من أحدهما ) وهو محال إذ يمتنع عاقل لا علم له أصلاً أو عالم لا عقل له أصلاً فثبت أن العقل هو العلم (وليس) العقل ( العلم بالنظريات (لأنه أي العلم بالنظريات مشروط بكمال العقل وكمال العقل مشروط بالعقل ( فيكون) العلم بالنظريات ) متأخراً عن العقل بمرتبتين فلا يكون نفسه، فهو ) أي العقل هو ( العلم بالضروريات وليس ) العقل ( علماً بكلها ) أي بكل الضروريات ) فإن العاقل قد يفقد بعضها كما ذكرنا في المقصد العاشر من أن الضروريات قد تفقد لفقد شرط من شرائطها ، فهو ) العلم ببعضها وهو المطلوب، وجوابه: إنا لا نسلم أنه لو كان العقل ( غير العلم جاز الانفكاك ( بينهما (لجواز تلازمهما فإن المتغايرين قد يتلازمان بحيث يمتنع الانفكاك بينهما مطلقاً كالجوهر والحصول في الحيز فإنهما متغايران ولا محال للانفكاك بينهما قال الإمام الرازي : والظاهر أنه ( أي العقل ( غريزة يتبعها العلم
قوله : ( أو عالم لا عقل له أصلاً وما قيل من أن المجنون له علم ولا عقل له، فيدفعه قوله : أصلاً فإن المجنون المطلق لا علم له ولا عقل والمجنون في الجملة له عقل وعلم في الجملة. قوله: (بمرتبتين) لكون الواسطة واحدة وهي كمال العقل، ولو قيل: إن العلم بالضروريات مشروط بالنظر المشروط بكمال العقل المشروط بالعقل كان التأخير بمراتب . قوله : ( وجوابه إِلخ ) هذا الجواب غير مطابق للاستدلال فإنه استدل بجواز تصور الانفكاك لا بجواز الانفكاك، وجواز تصور الانفكاك لا ينافي التلازم وما قيل أن مراد المستدل بحسن بعض الحسن وبقبح بعض القبيح لا كله لأن المعدود عندهم من البديهيات بعضها، كما صرح به في الإلهيات . قوله : ( وإلا جاز تصور (انفكاكهما في العبارة مسامحة أي انفكاكهما المتصور كما يدل عليه بطلان التالي والجواب الآتي أيضاً وهو الموافق لما في سائر الكتب . قوله : (أو عالم لا عقل له قيل : المجانين والصبيان ليسوا عقلاء مع أن لهم علماً سيما على رأي الشيخ أن الإحساس نوع من العلم، فلا بد من تخصيص العلم المذكور. قوله : ( والنظر مشروط بكمال العقل هذه الزيادة وقعت في بعض النسخ، فعلى هذا يكون العلم بالنظريات متأخراً عن العقل بمراتب، والتأخر بمرتبتين إنما يتفرع على ما ذكره، إذا كانت العبارة هكذا ، لأنه مشروط بالنظر والنظر مشروط بالعقل .
قوله : ( وكمال العقل مشروط بالعقل أي العقل الذي نحن بصدده وهو ما يخرج وقوع التكليف عن كونه بما لا يطاق، كما أشرنا إليه، فليتأمل. قوله : ( يتبعها العلم بالضروريات المراد بالعلم بالضروريات العلم بالبديهيات الكلية على وجه يتمكن به من الاكتساب، فمعنى الغريزة القوة الخلقية الغير الاختيارية بالذات لا بالواسطة لا القوة الجبلية اللازمة للنفس حتى لزم أن يوجد للصبيان .
بالضروريات عند سلامة الآلات والنائم لم يزل عقله وإن لم يكن عالماً في حالة النوم بشيء من الضروريات لاختلال وقع في الآلات وكذا الحال في اليقظان الذي لا يستحضر عن العلم بالضروريات لا كلها ولا بعضها، ولا شك أنَّ العاقل إذا كان سالماً الآفات المتعلقة بالآلات كان مدركاً لبعض الضروريات قطعاً فالعقل صفة غريزية تتبعها تلك العلوم، وقد اتضح بما ذكر من حال النائم أن العلم قد ينفك عن العقل، فلا يتم نفي التالي في دليل الشيخ كما لم تتم الملازمة أيضاً .
The second type - the eleventh purpose: The intellect is the basis of obligation by consensus.
What he mentioned (that the intellect is not other than knowledge, otherwise the separation of the two would be conceivable, either from both sides or from one of them, and that is impossible because there cannot be an intellectual with no knowledge at all nor a knower with no intellect at all, so it is established that the intellect is knowledge) and (the intellect is not) knowledge of theoretical matters (because knowledge of theoretical matters is conditional upon the perfection of the intellect, and the perfection of the intellect is conditional upon the intellect, so knowledge of theoretical matters is posterior to the intellect by two degrees, thus it is not itself), so the intellect is knowledge of necessary truths, and the intellect is not knowledge of all of them (i.e., all necessary truths), for the intellectual may lack some of them as we mentioned in the tenth purpose that necessary truths may be lacking due to the lack of one of their conditions, so it is knowledge of some of them, and that is the intended conclusion. And its answer: We do not accept that if the intellect were other than knowledge, separation would necessarily follow, because they may be necessarily concomitant, for two distinct things may be so concomitant that separation between them is absolutely impossible, like substance and occupying space, for they are distinct and yet separation between them is not possible. Imam al-Razi said: The apparent is that the intellect is a disposition followed by knowledge.
His statement: (or a knower with no intellect at all) and what is said that the insane has knowledge but no intellect is refuted by his statement 'at all', for the absolutely insane has neither knowledge nor intellect, and the partially insane has intellect and knowledge partially. His statement: (by two degrees) because the intermediate is one, namely the perfection of the intellect. And if it were said that knowledge of necessary truths is conditional upon reasoning, which is conditional upon the perfection of the intellect, which is conditional upon the intellect, then the posteriority would be by degrees. His statement: (and its answer etc.) This answer does not correspond to the proof, for he argued for the conceivability of separation, not actual separation, and conceivability of separation does not negate concomitance. And it was said that the arguer intended by 'good' some good and by 'evil' some evil, not all, because what they count among the self-evident truths is only some of them, as made clear in the Divine Matters. His statement: (otherwise the conceived separation would be permissible) in the expression there is a license, meaning their conceived separation, as indicated by the falsity of the consequent and the upcoming answer, and it is in agreement with what is in other books. His statement: (or a knower with no intellect) It was said: The insane and children are not rational although they have knowledge, especially according to the view of the Shaykh that sensation is a type of knowledge, so one must restrict the mentioned knowledge. His statement: (and reasoning is conditional upon the perfection of the intellect) This addition occurred in some copies; accordingly, knowledge of theoretical matters is posterior to the intellect by degrees, and the posteriority by two degrees is only derived from what he mentioned if the expression is such, because it is conditional upon reasoning and reasoning is conditional upon intellect.
His statement: (and the perfection of the intellect is conditional upon the intellect) i.e., the intellect we are dealing with, which excludes the occurrence of obligation being impossible to fulfill, as we indicated, so consider. His statement: (followed by knowledge of necessary truths) By knowledge of necessary truths is meant knowledge of universal self-evident truths in a way that enables acquisition; so the meaning of 'disposition' is the innate non-voluntary power essentially, not indirectly, not the inherent power necessary for the soul such that it would necessarily exist in children.
With necessary truths when the instruments are sound, and the sleeper's intellect does not cease although he is not conscious of any necessary truths during sleep due to a disruption in the instruments. Similarly for the wakeful person who does not recall about necessary truths, neither all nor some. And there is no doubt that if the rational person is free from defects related to the instruments, he necessarily perceives some necessary truths. So the intellect is an innate disposition that is followed by those sciences. And it has become clear from what was mentioned about the sleeper that knowledge may separate from the intellect, so the negation of the consequent in the Shaykh's proof is not established, nor is the implication established either.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a complex argumentative section on the nature of intellect (`aql`) and its relationship to knowledge (`ilm`). It begins by stating the eleventh purpose: the intellect is the basis of obligation by consensus. The author then presents a proof that the intellect is nothing other than knowledge: if it were other, separation would be conceivable, but it is impossible to have an intellectual with no knowledge or a knower with no intellect, so they are identical. Next, he refines the definition: intellect is not knowledge of theoretical matters because that would be circular (knowledge of theoretical matters depends on perfection of intellect, which depends on intellect), so it is knowledge of necessary truths, but not all of them, since one may lack some due to missing conditions. Then comes a response to the initial proof: we do not accept the necessity of separation because two distinct things can be inseparable by necessary concomitance, like substance and occupying space. Imam al-Razi's view is quoted: intellect is a disposition (gharīza) followed by knowledge. The commentary then elaborates on various points: the example of the insane (the absolutely insane has neither intellect nor knowledge), the meaning of 'by two degrees' (the intermediate is the perfection of intellect), and a critique of the response (the proof was about conceivability, not actual separation). Also discussed are the restriction of 'knowledge' in the case of children and the insane, the addition of 'reasoning is conditional upon perfection of intellect' in some copies, and the clarification that 'disposition' means an innate non-voluntary power. The passage continues with a discussion on the sleeper: his intellect remains even without conscious knowledge, showing that knowledge can separate from intellect, thus the proof's consequent is not negated. This section demonstrates the subtle dialectic between definitions and counterarguments in kalam.

Key Terms
العقل
العلم
الضروريات
النظريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that intellect and knowledge are separate faculties that can exist independently.
Correction: The proof shows they are necessarily identical; a rational being cannot exist without knowledge, and a knower cannot exist without intellect.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main argument that intellect is identical to knowledge?
A) Because knowledge is a condition for obligation.
B) Because otherwise separation would be conceivable, but it is impossible to have an intellectual with no knowledge or a knower with no intellect.
C) Because the intellect is a disposition followed by knowledge.
D) Because knowledge of theoretical matters is posterior to the intellect.

Key Takeaway
Intellect is essentially knowledge of some necessary truths, not theoretical knowledge, proven by the impossibility of their separation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If intellect is essentially knowledge of necessary truths, then every time you fail to reflect on the necessary truths about Allah—His existence, unity, and attributes—are you not dulling your own intellect? What specific truth will you meditate on today to sharpen your intellect?
?
The author shows that theoretical knowledge depends on intellect, not the other way around. Are you more concerned with amassing theoretical knowledge (e.g., complex fiqh issues, philosophical arguments) while neglecting the immediate necessary truths that should transform your heart? What necessary truth have you been avoiding that demands a change in your life?
?
The passage acknowledges that a person may lack some necessary truths due to missing conditions. What conditions in your life—such as arrogance, distraction, or sin—might be blocking you from seeing clear truths about yourself and your Creator? What concrete step will you take to remove that barrier today?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
5 min
The Counterargument: Necessary Concomitance and Al-Rāzī's View
Al-Jurjānī presents a response and al-Rāzī's alternative definition of intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the counterargument to the proof that intellect is knowledge—namely, that two distinct things can be inseparable by necessary concomitance—and al-Rāzī's alternative definition of intellect as a disposition.

Core Idea
Distinct things can be inseparable; intellect may be a disposition that entails knowledge.

After presenting the proof that intellect is identical to knowledge, the author records a response: we do not accept that distinctness implies separability because two distinct things (like substance and occupying space) can be necessarily concomitant. Then Imam al-Rāzī offers an alternative view: intellect is a disposition (gharīza) that is followed by knowledge. This lesson examines that critique and the alternative definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the counterargument
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'And its answer' and the translation. Notice that the response denies the necessity of separation between distinct things.
2
Understand the example of concomitance
The example of substance and occupying space shows that two distinct entities can be inseparable. This challenges the proof's assumption that distinctness implies possible separation.
Substance always occupies space, yet they are different.
3
Examine al-Rāzī's alternative definition
Al-Rāzī states that the apparent view is that intellect is a disposition (gharīza) followed by knowledge. This avoids identifying intellect with knowledge while preserving their close relationship.
Disposition here means an innate non-voluntary power.

قوله : ( وكمال العقل مشروط بالعقل أي العقل الذي نحن بصدده وهو ما يخرج وقوع التكليف عن كونه بما لا يطاق، كما أشرنا إليه، فليتأمل. قوله : ( يتبعها العلم بالضروريات المراد بالعلم بالضروريات العلم بالبديهيات الكلية على وجه يتمكن به من الاكتساب، فمعنى الغريزة القوة الخلقية الغير الاختيارية بالذات لا بالواسطة لا القوة الجبلية اللازمة للنفس حتى لزم أن يوجد للصبيان .
بالضروريات عند سلامة الآلات والنائم لم يزل عقله وإن لم يكن عالماً في حالة النوم بشيء من الضروريات لاختلال وقع في الآلات وكذا الحال في اليقظان الذي لا يستحضر عن العلم بالضروريات لا كلها ولا بعضها، ولا شك أنَّ العاقل إذا كان سالماً الآفات المتعلقة بالآلات كان مدركاً لبعض الضروريات قطعاً فالعقل صفة غريزية تتبعها تلك العلوم، وقد اتضح بما ذكر من حال النائم أن العلم قد ينفك عن العقل، فلا يتم نفي التالي في دليل الشيخ كما لم تتم الملازمة أيضاً .
The second type - the eleventh purpose: The intellect is the basis of obligation by consensus.
What he mentioned (that the intellect is not other than knowledge, otherwise the separation of the two would be conceivable, either from both sides or from one of them, and that is impossible because there cannot be an intellectual with no knowledge at all nor a knower with no intellect at all, so it is established that the intellect is knowledge) and (the intellect is not) knowledge of theoretical matters (because knowledge of theoretical matters is conditional upon the perfection of the intellect, and the perfection of the intellect is conditional upon the intellect, so knowledge of theoretical matters is posterior to the intellect by two degrees, thus it is not itself), so the intellect is knowledge of necessary truths, and the intellect is not knowledge of all of them (i.e., all necessary truths), for the intellectual may lack some of them as we mentioned in the tenth purpose that necessary truths may be lacking due to the lack of one of their conditions, so it is knowledge of some of them, and that is the intended conclusion. And its answer: We do not accept that if the intellect were other than knowledge, separation would necessarily follow, because they may be necessarily concomitant, for two distinct things may be so concomitant that separation between them is absolutely impossible, like substance and occupying space, for they are distinct and yet separation between them is not possible. Imam al-Razi said: The apparent is that the intellect is a disposition followed by knowledge.
His statement: (or a knower with no intellect at all) and what is said that the insane has knowledge but no intellect is refuted by his statement 'at all', for the absolutely insane has neither knowledge nor intellect, and the partially insane has intellect and knowledge partially. His statement: (by two degrees) because the intermediate is one, namely the perfection of the intellect. And if it were said that knowledge of necessary truths is conditional upon reasoning, which is conditional upon the perfection of the intellect, which is conditional upon the intellect, then the posteriority would be by degrees. His statement: (and its answer etc.) This answer does not correspond to the proof, for he argued for the conceivability of separation, not actual separation, and conceivability of separation does not negate concomitance. And it was said that the arguer intended by 'good' some good and by 'evil' some evil, not all, because what they count among the self-evident truths is only some of them, as made clear in the Divine Matters. His statement: (otherwise the conceived separation would be permissible) in the expression there is a license, meaning their conceived separation, as indicated by the falsity of the consequent and the upcoming answer, and it is in agreement with what is in other books. His statement: (or a knower with no intellect) It was said: The insane and children are not rational although they have knowledge, especially according to the view of the Shaykh that sensation is a type of knowledge, so one must restrict the mentioned knowledge. His statement: (and reasoning is conditional upon the perfection of the intellect) This addition occurred in some copies; accordingly, knowledge of theoretical matters is posterior to the intellect by degrees, and the posteriority by two degrees is only derived from what he mentioned if the expression is such, because it is conditional upon reasoning and reasoning is conditional upon intellect.
His statement: (and the perfection of the intellect is conditional upon the intellect) i.e., the intellect we are dealing with, which excludes the occurrence of obligation being impossible to fulfill, as we indicated, so consider. His statement: (followed by knowledge of necessary truths) By knowledge of necessary truths is meant knowledge of universal self-evident truths in a way that enables acquisition; so the meaning of 'disposition' is the innate non-voluntary power essentially, not indirectly, not the inherent power necessary for the soul such that it would necessarily exist in children.
With necessary truths when the instruments are sound, and the sleeper's intellect does not cease although he is not conscious of any necessary truths during sleep due to a disruption in the instruments. Similarly for the wakeful person who does not recall about necessary truths, neither all nor some. And there is no doubt that if the rational person is free from defects related to the instruments, he necessarily perceives some necessary truths. So the intellect is an innate disposition that is followed by those sciences. And it has become clear from what was mentioned about the sleeper that knowledge may separate from the intellect, so the negation of the consequent in the Shaykh's proof is not established, nor is the implication established either.

Commentary
This passage is a complex argumentative section on the nature of intellect (`aql`) and its relationship to knowledge (`ilm`). It begins by stating the eleventh purpose: the intellect is the basis of obligation by consensus. The author then presents a proof that the intellect is nothing other than knowledge: if it were other, separation would be conceivable, but it is impossible to have an intellectual with no knowledge or a knower with no intellect, so they are identical. Next, he refines the definition: intellect is not knowledge of theoretical matters because that would be circular (knowledge of theoretical matters depends on perfection of intellect, which depends on intellect), so it is knowledge of necessary truths, but not all of them, since one may lack some due to missing conditions. Then comes a response to the initial proof: we do not accept the necessity of separation because two distinct things can be inseparable by necessary concomitance, like substance and occupying space. Imam al-Razi's view is quoted: intellect is a disposition (gharīza) followed by knowledge. The commentary then elaborates on various points: the example of the insane (the absolutely insane has neither intellect nor knowledge), the meaning of 'by two degrees' (the intermediate is the perfection of intellect), and a critique of the response (the proof was about conceivability, not actual separation). Also discussed are the restriction of 'knowledge' in the case of children and the insane, the addition of 'reasoning is conditional upon perfection of intellect' in some copies, and the clarification that 'disposition' means an innate non-voluntary power. The passage continues with a discussion on the sleeper: his intellect remains even without conscious knowledge, showing that knowledge can separate from intellect, thus the proof's consequent is not negated. This section demonstrates the subtle dialectic between definitions and counterarguments in kalam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if two things are distinct, they must be separable.
Correction: They can be necessarily concomitant, like substance and space, so distinctness does not imply separability.

Checkpoint
What example is given to show that two distinct things can be inseparable?
A) Intellect and knowledge
B) Substance and occupying space
C) Knowledge and theoretical matters
D) Intellect and obligation

Key Takeaway
Distinct things can be inseparable due to necessary concomitance, and intellect may be a disposition that yields knowledge, not knowledge itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You consider yourself intelligent, but does your intellect actually produce knowledge that changes how you live? Or is it merely a dormant disposition, like a seed that never sprouts?
?
This lesson shows that distinct things can be inseparable, like faith and action. In your life, are your beliefs and actions distinct yet inseparable, or do you allow a gap between what you claim to believe and what you do?
?
Al-Razi says intellect is a disposition that is followed by knowledge. What new knowledge has your intellect led you to today? If none, what is holding you back from using your God-given intellect to seek truth?

The Critique of the Proof and the Case of the Sleeper
Al-Jurjānī critiques the response and uses the sleeper to show knowledge can separate from intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique that the initial proof was about conceivability, not actual separation, and the sleeper example showing that knowledge can separate from intellect, undermining the proof.

Core Idea
The proof fails because knowledge can actually separate from intellect, as seen in sleep.

In this final segment, the author clarifies that the initial argument was about the conceivability of separation, not actual separation, and points out that the response did not fully address the proof. Then, using the example of a sleeper, it is shown that knowledge can indeed separate from intellect (the sleeper has intellect but no actual knowledge), thus the proof's negation of the consequent is not established.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique of the response
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'قوله : ( وجوابه إِلخ )' and the translation. Notice the distinction between conceivability and actual separation.
2
Understand the sleeper example
The sleeper has intellect but no conscious knowledge of necessary truths, showing that knowledge can separate from intellect.
This contradicts the earlier claim that separation is impossible.
3
See the conclusion
Since knowledge can separate, the proof that intellect must be identical to knowledge fails; the negation of the consequent is not established.

The second type - the eleventh purpose: The intellect is the basis of obligation by consensus.
What he mentioned (that the intellect is not other than knowledge, otherwise the separation of the two would be conceivable, either from both sides or from one of them, and that is impossible because there cannot be an intellectual with no knowledge at all nor a knower with no intellect at all, so it is established that the intellect is knowledge) and (the intellect is not) knowledge of theoretical matters (because knowledge of theoretical matters is conditional upon the perfection of the intellect, and the perfection of the intellect is conditional upon the intellect, so knowledge of theoretical matters is posterior to the intellect by two degrees, thus it is not itself), so the intellect is knowledge of necessary truths, and the intellect is not knowledge of all of them (i.e., all necessary truths), for the intellectual may lack some of them as we mentioned in the tenth purpose that necessary truths may be lacking due to the lack of one of their conditions, so it is knowledge of some of them, and that is the intended conclusion. And its answer: We do not accept that if the intellect were other than knowledge, separation would necessarily follow, because they may be necessarily concomitant, for two distinct things may be so concomitant that separation between them is absolutely impossible, like substance and occupying space, for they are distinct and yet separation between them is not possible. Imam al-Razi said: The apparent is that the intellect is a disposition followed by knowledge.
His statement: (or a knower with no intellect at all) and what is said that the insane has knowledge but no intellect is refuted by his statement 'at all', for the absolutely insane has neither knowledge nor intellect, and the partially insane has intellect and knowledge partially. His statement: (by two degrees) because the intermediate is one, namely the perfection of the intellect. And if it were said that knowledge of necessary truths is conditional upon reasoning, which is conditional upon the perfection of the intellect, which is conditional upon the intellect, then the posteriority would be by degrees. His statement: (and its answer etc.) This answer does not correspond to the proof, for he argued for the conceivability of separation, not actual separation, and conceivability of separation does not negate concomitance. And it was said that the arguer intended by 'good' some good and by 'evil' some evil, not all, because what they count among the self-evident truths is only some of them, as made clear in the Divine Matters. His statement: (otherwise the conceived separation would be permissible) in the expression there is a license, meaning their conceived separation, as indicated by the falsity of the consequent and the upcoming answer, and it is in agreement with what is in other books. His statement: (or a knower with no intellect) It was said: The insane and children are not rational although they have knowledge, especially according to the view of the Shaykh that sensation is a type of knowledge, so one must restrict the mentioned knowledge. His statement: (and reasoning is conditional upon the perfection of the intellect) This addition occurred in some copies; accordingly, knowledge of theoretical matters is posterior to the intellect by degrees, and the posteriority by two degrees is only derived from what he mentioned if the expression is such, because it is conditional upon reasoning and reasoning is conditional upon intellect.
His statement: (and the perfection of the intellect is conditional upon the intellect) i.e., the intellect we are dealing with, which excludes the occurrence of obligation being impossible to fulfill, as we indicated, so consider. His statement: (followed by knowledge of necessary truths) By knowledge of necessary truths is meant knowledge of universal self-evident truths in a way that enables acquisition; so the meaning of 'disposition' is the innate non-voluntary power essentially, not indirectly, not the inherent power necessary for the soul such that it would necessarily exist in children.
With necessary truths when the instruments are sound, and the sleeper's intellect does not cease although he is not conscious of any necessary truths during sleep due to a disruption in the instruments. Similarly for the wakeful person who does not recall about necessary truths, neither all nor some. And there is no doubt that if the rational person is free from defects related to the instruments, he necessarily perceives some necessary truths. So the intellect is an innate disposition that is followed by those sciences. And it has become clear from what was mentioned about the sleeper that knowledge may separate from the intellect, so the negation of the consequent in the Shaykh's proof is not established, nor is the implication established either.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a complex argumentative section on the nature of intellect (`aql`) and its relationship to knowledge (`ilm`). It begins by stating the eleventh purpose: the intellect is the basis of obligation by consensus. The author then presents a proof that the intellect is nothing other than knowledge: if it were other, separation would be conceivable, but it is impossible to have an intellectual with no knowledge or a knower with no intellect, so they are identical. Next, he refines the definition: intellect is not knowledge of theoretical matters because that would be circular (knowledge of theoretical matters depends on perfection of intellect, which depends on intellect), so it is knowledge of necessary truths, but not all of them, since one may lack some due to missing conditions. Then comes a response to the initial proof: we do not accept the necessity of separation because two distinct things can be inseparable by necessary concomitance, like substance and occupying space. Imam al-Razi's view is quoted: intellect is a disposition (gharīza) followed by knowledge. The commentary then elaborates on various points: the example of the insane (the absolutely insane has neither intellect nor knowledge), the meaning of 'by two degrees' (the intermediate is the perfection of intellect), and a critique of the response (the proof was about conceivability, not actual separation). Also discussed are the restriction of 'knowledge' in the case of children and the insane, the addition of 'reasoning is conditional upon perfection of intellect' in some copies, and the clarification that 'disposition' means an innate non-voluntary power. The passage continues with a discussion on the sleeper: his intellect remains even without conscious knowledge, showing that knowledge can separate from intellect, thus the proof's consequent is not negated. This section demonstrates the subtle dialectic between definitions and counterarguments in kalam.

Misconception: Some might think that if the proof is about conceivability, then the answer about necessary concomitance is sufficient.
Correction: The answer fails to address the conceivability argument; the sleeper example shows actual separation, which further undermines the proof.

Two Knowledges of Two Knowns Are Distinct
Al-Jurjānī states the thesis that two knowledges of two knowns are distinct, with an objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two knowledges about two different knowns are always distinct, even when the knowns are identical in kind, according to the companions (al-aṣḥāb) and how the particularity of the object ensures their difference.

Core Idea
Knowledges are distinguished by their objects, not by the similarity of those objects.

In this lesson, we explore a fundamental epistemological thesis: every two knowledges that pertain to two different knowns are themselves different. This holds true whether the knowns are identical in type (like two whitenesses) or different (like whiteness and blackness). Al-Jurjānī presents this as the twelfth purpose and contrasts the majority view with a minority position held by the father of Imam al-Razi. A key objection—that if correspondence is the most specific attribute of knowledge, then two knowledges sharing that attribute would be similar—is addressed by highlighting the role of the particular object in defining each knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the thesis and note the exception
Read the first part of the passage: 'Every two knowledges that pertain to two knowns, they are different according to the companions, except the father of Imam al-Razi'. Understand that this is the default position.
The exception holds that knowledge follows the known in similarity/difference.
2
Understand the objection about correspondence
The passage presents a potential problem: if correspondence (muṭābaqah) is the most specific attribute of knowledge, then any two knowledges sharing this attribute would be similar. Why would that be a problem? Because our thesis says they are different.
The objection is based on the idea that sharing the most specific attribute entails similarity.
3
See the resolution: particularity of the object
The answer explains that correspondence is always tied to a particular object. For example, the knowledge of Zayd's standing corresponds to that specific standing, while the knowledge of 'Amr's sitting corresponds to that specific sitting. Thus, the correspondence in each knowledge is different, preventing similarity.
This is why the companions consider the knowledges different, not similar.
4
Review the examples and summarize
Consider the examples of two whitenesses or whiteness and blackness. Even if the qualities are identical, the knowledges are distinct because each is a correspondence to a specific instance. Write a one-sentence summary of the principle.

لمطابقته إياه .
قوله : (سواء تماثلا وتماثل المعلومين لا يقتضي تماثل العلمين، إنما ذلك إذا كان قوله : ( إن العلم قد ينفك عن العقل أي العقل الذي نحن بصدده وهو ما يخرج وقوع التكليف عن كونه بما لا يطاق كما أشرنا إليه، فتأمل. قوله : (مختلفان عند الأصحاب ) فإن قلت : قد سبق أن المطابقة أخص صفات العلم فيلزم من اشتراك ذينك العلمين في تلك المطابقة تماثلهما؟ قلت : يعتبر في كونها أخص صفات النفس للعلم تعلقه بمعلوم واحد وبالجملة خصوصية المتعلق معتبرة في المطابقة المذكورة فأخص صفات العلم المتعلق بقيام زيد كون علم قيام زيد مطابقاً للواقع، وأخص صفات العلم المتعلق بقعود عمرو كون علم قعوده مطابقاً له، ولهذا جعلوا العلمين المتعلقين بمعلومين مختلفين لا متماثلين.
The twelfth purpose: Every two knowledges that pertain to two knowns.
Every two knowledges that pertain to two knowns, they (i.e., those two knowledges) are different according to the companions (al-aṣḥāb), except the father of Imam al-Razi, whether the two knowns are similar (like two whitenesses) or different (like whiteness and blackness). Otherwise (i.e., if the two knowledges are not different but similar), the conceived separation would not be permissible. And since it is an interpretation of his statement that is not satisfactory, it necessitates including the qualifier of conception. His statement: (disposition) meaning a created thing, either an accident or a substance.
His statement: (although he is not knower in the state of sleep of any necessary things) rather they are stored with him; as for vision, it is false imagination, as will come. His statement: (the disruption etc.) In it that the mentioned disruption only affects the acquisition of sciences initially, not the persistence of acquired sciences, so the apparent is that the sleeper is a knower, even if he does not have knowledge of his knowledge; thus it has become clear that the proof of the Shaykh is complete.
His statement: (except the father of Imam al-Razi, for he said that knowledge follows the known in similarity and difference due to its correspondence to it).
His statement: (whether they are similar) and the similarity of the two knowns does not necessitate the similarity of the two knowledges; that is only when... His statement: (knowledge may separate from the intellect) i.e., the intellect we are dealing with, which excludes the occurrence of obligation being impossible to fulfill, as we indicated, so consider. His statement: (different according to the companions) For if you say: It has preceded that correspondence is the most specific attribute of knowledge, so the sharing of those two knowledges in that correspondence would necessitate their similarity? I say: The consideration of it being the most specific attribute of the soul for knowledge is its relation to a single known; in short, the particularity of the object is considered in that correspondence. So the most specific attribute of knowledge pertaining to Zayd's standing is that the knowledge of Zayd's standing corresponds to reality, and the most specific attribute of knowledge pertaining to 'Amr's sitting is the knowledge of his sitting corresponds to it. For this reason, they made the two knowledges pertaining to two knowns different, not similar.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents the twelfth purpose: the thesis that every two knowledges relating to two different knowns are themselves distinct, regardless of whether the knowns are similar or different. The exception is the father of Imam al-Razi, who held that knowledge follows the known in similarity and difference. The commentary clarifies that even if the knowns are similar (e.g., two whitenesses), the knowledges are still considered different because correspondence is tied to a specific object. A potential objection is raised: if correspondence is the most specific attribute of knowledge, then two knowledges sharing that attribute would be similar. The answer is that correspondence is specified by the object: the correspondence of knowledge of Zayd's standing is different from that of 'Amr's sitting, so the knowledges are distinct. The passage also includes side comments on the previous topic, such as the sleeper's knowledge being stored, and the nature of disposition as either an accident or substance. However, the core argument of this lesson is that the particularity of the object makes each knowledge unique, even if the knowns are similar in kind.

المعلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two knowns are similar (e.g., both whiteness), then the knowledges of them are also similar.
Correction: The knowledges are distinct because each knowledge corresponds to a specific instance of whiteness; the object's particularity prevents the knowledges from being identical.

Checkpoint

A) They are always similar if the knowns are similar.
B) They are always different, regardless of whether the knowns are similar or different.
C) They are different only if the knowns are different.
D) They are similar only if the knowns are similar.

Key Takeaway
Two knowledges of two different knowns are always distinct because each knowledge corresponds to its specific object, making the attribute of correspondence unique to each.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own knowledge of different things — like your knowledge of your own faith and your knowledge of a worldly fact — do you treat them as fundamentally distinct or as similar? How does this lesson's claim challenge your assumption?
?
The passage argues that two knowledges are distinguished by their objects, not by their similarity. In your daily life, do you sometimes conflate different types of knowledge because they feel the same? How can you cultivate a more precise awareness of the particularity of each piece of knowledge you hold?
?
This lesson corrects the idea that knowledge of similar things is the same. Think of a time you assumed someone understood something the same way you did because the object was similar. How might that assumption have led to miscommunication or error? What would it mean for you to honor the uniqueness of each person's knowledge of even similar topics?

The Rule of Two Knowledges: Sameness with Same Known and Time
Al-Amidi qualifies the rule of similarity by introducing time as part of the object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basic rule that two knowledges related to one known are similars, and al-Amidi's qualification that this holds only when both the known and its time are identical.

Core Idea
Time can change the object of knowledge, making two knowledges different even if the essence is one.

The author begins by stating a key principle of Islamic epistemology: two knowledges related to two different knowns cannot coexist, like opposites. But what about two knowledges related to the same known? The Ash‛ari masters hold they are similars. Al-Amidi agrees but adds an important condition: the known and its time must also be one. This lesson unpacks that condition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial rule
Read the first few lines of the Arabic passage where the rule is stated: every two knowledges related to two knowns are like similars that cannot coexist. Then note the statement about two knowledges related to one known being similars.
This is the baseline position.
2
Al-Amidi's qualification
Read al-Amidi's response: he affirms the rule only if the known and its time are both one. Notice his argument: if time differs, the object of knowledge effectively becomes different, so the two knowledges are not related to one known.
Time is not just a container but part of the object.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why does time matter? According to al-Amidi, a thing at time T1 is different from the same thing at time T2 when considered as an object of knowledge. This means the two knowledges are not about the same known, so the original rule does not apply.
This is a subtle point about how objects are individuated.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
