مسد الآخر ( قال الآمدي : هذا الذي ذكروه من تماثل العلمين حق بلا اشتباه ( إن اتحد المعلوم ووقته أيضاً، فإن كلاً من العلمين حينئذ متعلق بعين ما تعلق به الآخر فكل منهما يقوم مقام صاحبه ولا يجامعه ( وأما إذا اختلف ( الوقت وحده مع اتحاد ذات المعلوم ( فقد يقال : ( العلمان المتعلقان به في ذينك الوقتين ( مثلان إذ اختلاف الوقت لا يؤثر في اختلاف العلمين ) كما ) لا يؤثر اختلاف الوقت (في) اختلاف (الجوهر)، فإن الجوهر لا يختلف بسبب كونه في وقتين مختلفين قال الآمدي : ( والفرق ظاهر فإن الوقت هاهنا ) أي فيما نحن فيه ( داخل في متعلق (العلم ) إذ الكلام فيما إذا تعلق العلم بشيء معين من حيث أنه في وقت وتعلق به أيضاً من حيث أنه في وقت آخر، ولا شك أن ذلك الشيء مأخوذاً مع أحد الوقتين مغاير له مأخوذاً مع الآخر، وإذا تعدد المعلومان، فقد بان أنه يلزم منه اختلاف العلمين (و) الوقت (ثمة) أي فيما ذكروه من النظير ( عارض للجوهر) الحاصل في الوقتين فلا يقتضي تعدداً في ذاته ( وإنما نظير ذلك ) الذي ذكروه من حال الجوهر هو ( العلم ( الواحد الثابت ( في وقتين ) فإنه كالجوهر لا يختلف بسبب حصوله في الوقتين ( لا العلم بمعلوم) واحد (مقيد بوقتين مختلفين فإن ذلك التقييد يقتضي تعدد المعلوم المستلزم لاختلاف العلمين كما ،قررناه وأنت خبير بأنه لما اقتضى تعدد المعلوم لم يكن العلمان متعلقين بمعلوم ،واحد كما هو المبحث إلا أن الأصحاب لما قالوا كل علمين متعلقين بمعلوم واحد ، فهما، مثلان اتحد الوقت أو اختلف نبه الآمدي على أن اعتبار العلم عبارة عن صورة مساوية للمعلوم في تمام الماهية، والأصحاب لا يقولون بالصورة فضلاً عن الصورة المساوية على أنه يجوز أن تكون العوارض الخارجة عن ماهية المعلوم داخلة في العلم، وكونه مطابقاً للمعلوم معناه أن يكون حكاية عنه ومرآة لمشاهدته ولا يلزم اتحاده به، كيف والعلم تعلق أو صفة ذات تعلق والمعلوم لا يجب أن يكون كذلك. قوله : ( وأما العلمان إلخ حاصله : أن العلمين الحاصلين في محلين المتعلقين بمعلوم واحد بالذات والاعتبار إذا قيسا إلى محل واحد فهما مثلان لامتناع اجتماعهما فيه للزوم ،الاتحاد، وإذا قيسا إلى محلين فإن كان كل من العلمين يقتضي الاختصاص بمحله لذاته كعلمنا بوجدانياتنا فهما ،متخالفان وإلا فهما متماثلان هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم هذا المقال، ولا يلتفت إلى قيل وقال . قوله : ( إن اتحد المعلوم إلخ) أي ذاتاً واعتباراً وإلا فلا حاجة إليه بعد اعتبار وحدة المعلوم فهو قيد احتياطي . الوقت يمكن على وجهين: أحدهما أن يكون ظرفاً للعلم فلا يوجد تعدده تعدده تعدداً فيه فضلاً الاختلاف والتماثل وإذا فرض تعدده فيهما كانا متماثلين والثاني: أن عن يكون قيداً للمعلوم فيتعدد العلم ويكون مختلفاً، وهذا الذي ذكرناه من حال العلمين المتعلقين بمعلوم واحد إنما هو على تقدير اتحاد محل العلم أي العالم ( وأما إذا اختلف محل العلم بمعلوم واحد ( كزيد وعمرو) العالمين بشيء واحد ( فإن قلنا : كل من العلمين القائمين بهما يقتضي الاختصاص بمحله لذاته أي يقتضي ذاته أن يكون حالاً في ذلك المحل دون غيره ( فهما مختلفان ) لأن المثلين لا يتفاوتان في الاقتضاء المستند إلى الذات ( وإلا فمثلان كما هو الظاهر إذ لا طريق إلى الاختلاف واقتضائه سوى ما ذكر والفرض أنّه منتف ) وسيأتي لذلك زيادة بيان ) هذا وعد بلا ،وفاء والسبب فيه أن الآمدي أورد هذا المبحث في أوائل أبكار الأفكار، وقال بعد قوله وإلا فهما ،مثلان وسيأتي تحقيق ذلك فيما بعد، وأشار به إلى ما سيأتي في أواسط كتابه من تحقيق معنى التماثل والمثلين وإثبات ذلك على منكريه فالمصنف تابعه في هذه الحوالة وغفل عن تقديمه مباحث التماثل والمثلين في مرصد الوحدة، والكثرة من الأمور العامة . قوله : ( وإذا فرض تعدده فيهما إلخ) بأن قلنا بتجدد الاعراض. قوله: (فهما مثلان اتحد الوقت أو اختلف أورد عليه أنه إذا اتحد الوقت والمفروض أن العالم أيضاً متحد كما يدل عليه قوله، وأما إذا اختلف محل العلم لم يتصور تعدد العلم فكيف يقال : إنهما مثلان؟ وأجيب : بأن عدم التعدد لكونهما مثلين والأظهر أن يقال : الحكم بالمثلية فرضي مبتن على فرض التعدد، والمعنى لو وجد علمان كذا وكذا لكانا ،مثلين فليتأمل قوله : ( فلا يوجب تعدده تعدداً فيه سواء كان العلم عبارة عن التعلق أو عن الصورة الحاصلة في النفس لجواز أن يستمر التعلق أو الصورة زمانين فقد تعدد الوقت دون العلم وهذا ظاهر إذا جوز بقاء العرض، فليتأمل. قوله : ( يقتضي الاختصاص بمحله لذاته قد سبق أن المطابقة أخص صفات النفس للعلم
The second type - the twelfth objective: Every two knowledges that relate to two known things come together because similars do not come together like opposites. As for two knowledges related to one known, they are similars according to the companions, and therefore their conjunction is impossible, and one suffices for the other. Al-Amidi said: What they have mentioned about the similarity of the two knowledges is true without doubt if the known and its time are also one, for then each of the two knowledges is related to exactly what the other is related to, so each takes the place of its companion and does not co-exist with it. But if only time differs while the essence of the known is one, then it may be said that the two knowledges related to it in those two times are similars, since difference of time does not affect the difference of the two knowledges, just as difference of time does not affect the difference of substance, for substance does not differ due to being in two different times. Al-Amidi said: The difference is apparent, for time here—i.e., in what we are discussing—is included in the object of knowledge, since the discussion concerns when knowledge relates to a specific thing inasmuch as it is in a time, and it also relates to it inasmuch as it is in another time. There is no doubt that that thing taken with one of the two times is different from it taken with the other. If the knowns become multiple, then it is clear that difference of the two knowledges follows. And time there—i.e., in the analogy they mentioned—is an accident to the substance occurring in the two times, so it does not necessitate multiplicity in its essence. Rather, the analogy of that which they mentioned concerning the state of substance is: one knowledge that is constant in two times, for it, like substance, does not differ due to its occurrence in two times, not knowledge of a single thing restricted by two different times, for that restriction necessitates multiplicity of the known, which entails difference of the two knowledges, as we have established. And you are well aware that when it necessitated multiplicity of the known, the two knowledges were not related to one known as is the subject of inquiry. But the companions said: Every two knowledges related to one known—they are similars whether time is one or different. Al-Amidi noted that considering knowledge as a form equal to the known in the entirety of essence—the companions do not posit a form, let alone an equal form—and it is possible that accidents external to the essence of the known are included in knowledge. Its being conformable to the known means that it is a report of it and a mirror for witnessing it, and its unity with it is not necessary. How [could it be], for knowledge is a relation or an attribute having a relation, and the known does not have to be like that. His statement: 'And as for the two knowledges etc.' Its summary: That the two knowledges occurring in two locations, related to one known in essence and consideration, if compared to one location, they are similars due to the impossibility of their conjunction in it because of necessitating unity; and if compared to two locations, then if each of the two knowledges necessitates specificity to its location by its essence, like our knowledge of our inner experiences, they are different; otherwise they are similars. This is how this statement should be understood, and one should not pay attention to hearsay. His statement: 'If the known is one etc.' means in essence and consideration, otherwise there is no need for it after considering the unity of the known, so it is a precautionary condition. Time can be in two ways: one is that it is a container for knowledge, so its multiplicity does not cause multiplicity in it [knowledge], let alone difference and similarity; if its multiplicity is assumed in them [two knowledges], they are similars. The second: that it is a restriction for the known, so knowledge becomes multiple and different. And this which we have mentioned concerning the state of two knowledges related to one known is only on the assumption of the unity of the locus of knowledge, i.e., the knower. And if the locus of knowledge of one known differs (like Zayd and 'Amr) who know one thing: if we say that each of the two knowledges subsisting in them necessitates specificity to its location by its essence, i.e., its essence necessitates that it be in that location and not in another, then they are different, because similars do not differ in the necessitation based on essence; otherwise they are similars, as is apparent, since there is no path to difference and its necessitation except what was mentioned, and the assumption is that it is absent. And there will come further clarification of this. This is a promise without fulfillment, and the reason is that al-Amidi raised this discussion in the beginning of Abkar al-Afkar, and said after his statement 'otherwise they are similars' 'and there will come an investigation of that later'. He pointed to what will come in the middle of his book concerning the investigation of the meaning of similarity and similars and establishing it against its deniers. The author followed him in this referral and neglected to present the investigations of similarity and similars in the observation post of unity and multiplicity from the general matters. His statement: 'And if its multiplicity is assumed in them etc.' — it is because we say by the renewal of accidents. His statement: 'They are similars whether time is one or different' — an objection was raised: if time is one and the assumption is that the knower is also one, as his statement indicates, and if the locus of knowledge differs, then multiplicity of knowledge is inconceivable, so how is it said that they are similars? And it was answered: that the lack of multiplicity is because they are similars, and the more apparent is to say: the judgment of similarity is hypothetical based on the assumption of multiplicity, and the meaning is: if two knowledges such-and-such existed, they would be similars; so let one reflect. His statement: 'So its multiplicity does not cause multiplicity in it' — whether knowledge is the relation or the form obtained in the soul, because it is possible for the relation or the form to continue for two time-periods, so time multiplies without knowledge, and this is apparent if one allows the survival of the accident; so let one reflect. His statement: 'It necessitates specificity to its location by its essence' — it has preceded that conformity is the most specific attribute of the soul for knowledge.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a complex discussion about the nature of two knowledges and whether they are similar or different based on their relation to the known, time, and the locus of knowledge. The author begins by stating the rule that two knowledges related to two different knowns are like similars (mithlan) that cannot coexist, analogous to opposites. Then he addresses the case where both knowledges relate to a single known, at which the Ash‘ari masters (al-ashab) rule that they are similars. Al-Amidi is then cited as affirming this rule on condition that both the known and its time are identical. If only the time differs while the known remains the same, al-Amidi notes that some might still call them similars because time does not affect the essence of knowledge, just as it does not affect a substance. However, al-Amidi argues that time is part of the object of knowledge: if knowledge is of a thing at a specific time, then the same thing at a different time is a different object, so the two knowledges become different. The author then explains that this makes the two knowledges relate to two different knowns, so they are not both related to one known—contradicting the original question. The author then summarizes the position: if two knowledges in two loci are compared to one locus, they are similars because they cannot coexist; if compared to two loci, they may be different if each necessitates its own locus (like knowledge of one's own mental states), otherwise they are similars. A note follows criticizing al-Amidi for referring to a later investigation that never materialized and criticizing the author for repeating the referral. Then a series of glosses clarify that the multiplicity of time does not affect the knowledge itself unless time is a restriction, that the judgment of similarity is hypothetical, and that knowledge can endure over time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That two knowledges of the same thing at different times are always similars because the thing remains the same.
Correction: Al-Amidi argues that time is part of the object of knowledge, so the thing at different times constitutes different knowns, making the knowledges different.

Checkpoint
According to al-Amidi, when are two knowledges related to one known truly similars?
A) When the known is the same in essence regardless of time.
B) When both the known and its time are one.
C) When the knower is the same.
D) When the time is different but the essence is one.

Key Takeaway
Time can individuate objects of knowledge, so two knowledges of what seems the same thing at different times are not necessarily similars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a past failure or sin that still weighs on you. Do you see yourself as the same person now? How does this lesson’s insight—that time is part of the object of knowledge—urge you to let go of a fixed identity and embrace change? What specific step will you take today to treat your present self as a new object of Allah’s mercy?
?
Consider a person you have judged and set in your mind as 'that kind of person'. How might the fact that time makes each moment a new known challenge that judgment? Who do you need to ‘re-know’ by letting go of your old knowledge of them? Will you do that today?
?
When you pray, do you rely on past spiritual states as if they guarantee present connection? How can you apply Al-Amidi’s point to treat each prayer as a new knowledge of Allah, not a repetition? What will you do in your next prayer to make it fresh?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
7 min
Two Knowledges in Different Loci: When Are They Similars?
Author analyzes the effect of different loci (knowers) on similarity of knowledges.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the locus of knowledge (the knower) affects whether two knowledges are similars or different, especially in cases of inner experience versus external objects.

Core Idea
If each knowledge inherently requires its own locus, they are different; otherwise, they are similars.

After settling the role of time, the author turns to another factor: the locus of knowledge (mahall al-ilm), i.e., the knower. Two knowledges of the same thing existing in two different knowers: are they similars? The answer depends on whether each knowledge intrinsically necessitates being in its particular locus. This lesson explores that nuance and the hypothetical nature of similarity judgments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the new scenario
Focus on the part where the author says: 'And if the locus of knowledge of one known differs (like Zayd and 'Amr knowing one thing)'. This introduces two knowers knowing the same thing.
The passage shifts from time to locus.
2
The two subcases
Read the condition: if each knowledge necessitates its own locus (like knowledge of one's own inner states), they are different. Otherwise, they are similars.
Inner experiences are unique to the individual, so Zayd's pain-knowledge is different from 'Amr's.
3
Reflect on the hypothetical nature
Notice the gloss: the judgment of similarity is hypothetical. If two knowledges could exist in different loci, they would be similars unless they have essential specificity.
The discussion is theoretical, assuming multiplicity is possible.

فالمصنف تابعه في هذه الحوالة وغفل عن تقديمه مباحث التماثل والمثلين في مرصد الوحدة، والكثرة من الأمور العامة . قوله : ( وإذا فرض تعدده فيهما إلخ) بأن قلنا بتجدد الاعراض. قوله: (فهما مثلان اتحد الوقت أو اختلف أورد عليه أنه إذا اتحد الوقت والمفروض أن العالم أيضاً متحد كما يدل عليه قوله، وأما إذا اختلف محل العلم لم يتصور تعدد العلم فكيف يقال : إنهما مثلان؟ وأجيب : بأن عدم التعدد لكونهما مثلين والأظهر أن يقال : الحكم بالمثلية فرضي مبتن على فرض التعدد، والمعنى لو وجد علمان كذا وكذا لكانا ،مثلين فليتأمل قوله : ( فلا يوجب تعدده تعدداً فيه سواء كان العلم عبارة عن التعلق أو عن الصورة الحاصلة في النفس لجواز أن يستمر التعلق أو الصورة زمانين فقد تعدد الوقت دون العلم وهذا ظاهر إذا جوز بقاء العرض، فليتأمل. قوله : ( يقتضي الاختصاص بمحله لذاته قد سبق أن المطابقة أخص صفات النفس للعلم
The second type - the twelfth objective: Every two knowledges that relate to two known things come together because similars do not come together like opposites. As for two knowledges related to one known, they are similars according to the companions, and therefore their conjunction is impossible, and one suffices for the other. Al-Amidi said: What they have mentioned about the similarity of the two knowledges is true without doubt if the known and its time are also one, for then each of the two knowledges is related to exactly what the other is related to, so each takes the place of its companion and does not co-exist with it. But if only time differs while the essence of the known is one, then it may be said that the two knowledges related to it in those two times are similars, since difference of time does not affect the difference of the two knowledges, just as difference of time does not affect the difference of substance, for substance does not differ due to being in two different times. Al-Amidi said: The difference is apparent, for time here—i.e., in what we are discussing—is included in the object of knowledge, since the discussion concerns when knowledge relates to a specific thing inasmuch as it is in a time, and it also relates to it inasmuch as it is in another time. There is no doubt that that thing taken with one of the two times is different from it taken with the other. If the knowns become multiple, then it is clear that difference of the two knowledges follows. And time there—i.e., in the analogy they mentioned—is an accident to the substance occurring in the two times, so it does not necessitate multiplicity in its essence. Rather, the analogy of that which they mentioned concerning the state of substance is: one knowledge that is constant in two times, for it, like substance, does not differ due to its occurrence in two times, not knowledge of a single thing restricted by two different times, for that restriction necessitates multiplicity of the known, which entails difference of the two knowledges, as we have established. And you are well aware that when it necessitated multiplicity of the known, the two knowledges were not related to one known as is the subject of inquiry. But the companions said: Every two knowledges related to one known—they are similars whether time is one or different. Al-Amidi noted that considering knowledge as a form equal to the known in the entirety of essence—the companions do not posit a form, let alone an equal form—and it is possible that accidents external to the essence of the known are included in knowledge. Its being conformable to the known means that it is a report of it and a mirror for witnessing it, and its unity with it is not necessary. How [could it be], for knowledge is a relation or an attribute having a relation, and the known does not have to be like that. His statement: 'And as for the two knowledges etc.' Its summary: That the two knowledges occurring in two locations, related to one known in essence and consideration, if compared to one location, they are similars due to the impossibility of their conjunction in it because of necessitating unity; and if compared to two locations, then if each of the two knowledges necessitates specificity to its location by its essence, like our knowledge of our inner experiences, they are different; otherwise they are similars. This is how this statement should be understood, and one should not pay attention to hearsay. His statement: 'If the known is one etc.' means in essence and consideration, otherwise there is no need for it after considering the unity of the known, so it is a precautionary condition. Time can be in two ways: one is that it is a container for knowledge, so its multiplicity does not cause multiplicity in it [knowledge], let alone difference and similarity; if its multiplicity is assumed in them [two knowledges], they are similars. The second: that it is a restriction for the known, so knowledge becomes multiple and different. And this which we have mentioned concerning the state of two knowledges related to one known is only on the assumption of the unity of the locus of knowledge, i.e., the knower. And if the locus of knowledge of one known differs (like Zayd and 'Amr) who know one thing: if we say that each of the two knowledges subsisting in them necessitates specificity to its location by its essence, i.e., its essence necessitates that it be in that location and not in another, then they are different, because similars do not differ in the necessitation based on essence; otherwise they are similars, as is apparent, since there is no path to difference and its necessitation except what was mentioned, and the assumption is that it is absent. And there will come further clarification of this. This is a promise without fulfillment, and the reason is that al-Amidi raised this discussion in the beginning of Abkar al-Afkar, and said after his statement 'otherwise they are similars' 'and there will come an investigation of that later'. He pointed to what will come in the middle of his book concerning the investigation of the meaning of similarity and similars and establishing it against its deniers. The author followed him in this referral and neglected to present the investigations of similarity and similars in the observation post of unity and multiplicity from the general matters. His statement: 'And if its multiplicity is assumed in them etc.' — it is because we say by the renewal of accidents. His statement: 'They are similars whether time is one or different' — an objection was raised: if time is one and the assumption is that the knower is also one, as his statement indicates, and if the locus of knowledge differs, then multiplicity of knowledge is inconceivable, so how is it said that they are similars? And it was answered: that the lack of multiplicity is because they are similars, and the more apparent is to say: the judgment of similarity is hypothetical based on the assumption of multiplicity, and the meaning is: if two knowledges such-and-such existed, they would be similars; so let one reflect. His statement: 'So its multiplicity does not cause multiplicity in it' — whether knowledge is the relation or the form obtained in the soul, because it is possible for the relation or the form to continue for two time-periods, so time multiplies without knowledge, and this is apparent if one allows the survival of the accident; so let one reflect. His statement: 'It necessitates specificity to its location by its essence' — it has preceded that conformity is the most specific attribute of the soul for knowledge.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a complex discussion about the nature of two knowledges and whether they are similar or different based on their relation to the known, time, and the locus of knowledge. The author begins by stating the rule that two knowledges related to two different knowns are like similars (mithlan) that cannot coexist, analogous to opposites. Then he addresses the case where both knowledges relate to a single known, at which the Ash‘ari masters (al-ashab) rule that they are similars. Al-Amidi is then cited as affirming this rule on condition that both the known and its time are identical. If only the time differs while the known remains the same, al-Amidi notes that some might still call them similars because time does not affect the essence of knowledge, just as it does not affect a substance. However, al-Amidi argues that time is part of the object of knowledge: if knowledge is of a thing at a specific time, then the same thing at a different time is a different object, so the two knowledges become different. The author then explains that this makes the two knowledges relate to two different knowns, so they are not both related to one known—contradicting the original question. The author then summarizes the position: if two knowledges in two loci are compared to one locus, they are similars because they cannot coexist; if compared to two loci, they may be different if each necessitates its own locus (like knowledge of one's own mental states), otherwise they are similars. A note follows criticizing al-Amidi for referring to a later investigation that never materialized and criticizing the author for repeating the referral. Then a series of glosses clarify that the multiplicity of time does not affect the knowledge itself unless time is a restriction, that the judgment of similarity is hypothetical, and that knowledge can endure over time.

Key Terms
محل العلم
اقتضاء الاختصاص
علمان
مثلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That two knowledges of the same object in different minds are always the same.
Correction: They are only similars if they do not necessitate their specific locus. Knowledge of one's own mental states is a case where they are different.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when are two knowledges of the same thing in different knowers considered different?
A) When the known is the same in essence.
B) When each knowledge necessitates its own locus by essence.
C) When the times are different.
D) When the knowers are ignorant.

Key Takeaway
Knowledges in different loci are similars unless they essentially require being in a specific locus, as with inner experiences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Iman' in a truth about Allah, is that knowledge truly yours alone, or do you share it identically with every believer? Does your heart's certainty transplant to another soul without loss?
?
Consider a sin you have committed: your knowledge of its evil is tied to your guilt and repentance. Can another person—even a scholar—know that evil in the same way you do? What does that say about individual accountability before Allah?
?
This lesson distinguishes knowledges that are essentially tied to their locus from those that are not. Is your love for Allah a knowledge that requires your unique self? If so, would any other‘s love be different—and what does that demand of you in sincerity?

The Two Ways Time Relates to Knowledge
Explains two ways time relates to knowledge and implications for sameness/difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways time can relate to knowledge and how they affect the sameness or difference of knowledge across instances.

Core Idea
Time can be either a container for knowledge or a condition for the known.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explores a subtle but crucial point: how does time relate to knowledge? The author presents two possibilities—time as a mere container, or time as a condition that shapes the known object. Understanding this distinction helps clarify when multiple instances of knowledge are considered the same or different.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the two explicit categories introduced by 'أحدهما' and 'الثاني'.
2
Identify the two ways time is considered
Locate where the text discusses time as a container (ظرف) and time as a condition (قيد). Understand how each affects the multiplicity of knowledge.
As a container, time does not cause multiplicity; as a condition, it does.
3
Analyze the different knower scenario
Read the part about two knowers (Zayd and Amr) knowing the same thing. See how the author argues for difference or similarity based on essential specificity.
The conclusion hinges on whether each knowledge necessitates being in its own locus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني - المقصد الثالث عشر : انقلاب العلم الضروري نظرياً الوقت يمكن على وجهين: أحدهما أن يكون ظرفاً للعلم فلا يوجب تعدده تعدداً فيه فضلاً الاختلاف والتماثل وإذا فرض تعدده فيهما كانا متماثلين والثاني: أن يكون قيداً للمعلوم فيتعدد العلم ويكون مختلفاً، وهذا الذي ذكرناه من حال العلمين المتعلقين بمعلوم واحد إنما هو على تقدير اتحاد محل العلم أي العالم ( وأما إذا اختلف محل العلم بمعلوم واحد ( كزيد وعمرو) العالمين بشيء واحد ( فإن قلنا : كل من العلمين القائمين بهما يقتضي الاختصاص بمحله لذاته أي يقتضي ذاته أن يكون حالاً في ذلك المحل دون غيره ( فهما مختلفان ) لأن المثلين لا يتفاوتان في الاقتضاء المستند إلى الذات ( وإلا فمثلان كما هو الظاهر إذ لا طريق إلى الاختلاف واقتضائه سوى ما ذكر والفرض أنّه منتف ) وسيأتي لذلك زيادة بيان ) هذا وعد بلا ،وفاء والسبب فيه أن الآمدي أورد هذا المبحث في أوائل أبكار الأفكار، وقال بعد قوله وإلا فهما ،مثلان وسيأتي تحقيق ذلك فيما بعد، وأشار به إلى ما سيأتي في أواسط كتابه من تحقيق معنى التماثل والمثلين وإثبات ذلك على منكريه فالمصنف تابعه في هذه الحوالة وغفل عن تقديمه مباحث التماثل والمثلين في مرصد الوحدة، والكثرة من الأمور العامة .
Section Two - The Thirteenth Objective: The Transformation of Necessary Knowledge into Theoretical Knowledge. Time can be in two ways: one is that it is a container for knowledge, so its multiplicity does not necessitate multiplicity in it, let alone difference and similarity, and if its multiplicity is assumed in them, they would be similar. The second is that it is a condition for the known, so the knowledge multiplies and becomes different. This which we have mentioned about the state of two knowledges related to a single object is only on the assumption of the unity of the locus of knowledge, i.e., the knower. But if the locus of knowledge differs regarding a single object (like Zayd and Amr, two knowers of one thing), then if we say: each of the two knowledges subsisting in them necessitates specificity to its locus by itself, i.e., its essence necessitates that it be an attribute in that locus to the exclusion of another, then they are different, because similars do not differ in the necessitation based on essence. Otherwise, they would be similars, as is apparent, since there is no way to difference and its necessitation except what was mentioned, and the assumption is that it is absent. A fuller explanation will come later. This is a promise without fulfillment, and the reason is that al-Amidi introduced this discussion at the beginning of Abkar al-Afkar, and said after his statement 'otherwise they are similars' that its verification will come later, and he indicated thereby what will come in the middle of his book regarding the verification of the meaning of similarity and similars and proving it against its deniers. So the author (al-Iji) followed him in this reference and neglected to precede the discussions of similarity and similars in the observatory of unity and multiplicity among general matters.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a previous statement about two knowledges of the same object. It first explains that time can be considered in two ways: either as a container for knowledge (so that the passage of time does not necessitate multiplicity in the knowledge itself), or as a condition for the known (so that knowledge changes with time). Then it discusses a scenario where the knower differs but the known is the same. If each knowledge has an essential specificity to its locus, then the two knowledges are different; otherwise they would be similar. The commentator then notes that al-Amidi made a reference to a later verification of similarity, but the author (al-Iji) followed him without keeping his promise, as he should have discussed similarity earlier in the book.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time always causes multiplicity in knowledge.
Correction: Time can be a container (no multiplicity) or a condition (multiplicity). The passage shows both possibilities.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two ways time can relate to knowledge?
A) As a container for the known, or as a condition for the knower.
B) As a container for knowledge, or as a condition for the known.
C) As a cause of multiplicity, or as a cause of unity.
D) As a condition for similarity, or as a condition for difference.

Key Takeaway
The relationship of time to knowledge can be either as a neutral container or as a conditioning factor, and this affects whether multiple instances of knowledge are considered similar or different.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own knowledge of Allah, do you treat it as a 'container' that stays constant regardless of time, or as a 'condition' that must adapt to new circumstances? Which approach makes you more accountable for seeking deeper understanding?
?
Think of a disagreement you had with another Muslim about a core belief. According to this passage, are your knowledges essentially different because they are in different minds, or are they similar? How does that affect your attitude toward their view?
?
Al-Amidi promised to clarify similarity later but failed. Where in your faith journey have you relied on vague promises—from scholars, books, or yourself—instead of seeking clear understanding? What one step will you take today to replace a vague reference with clear knowledge?

The First Position: Transformation is Absolutely Possible
Al-Qadi's argument for absolute possibility of transformation and al-Amidi's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasoning behind al-Qadi's claim that necessary knowledge can become theoretical, and al-Amidi's objection based on the heterogeneity of sciences.

Core Idea
Homogeneity of all sciences is the key premise — but is it true?

The debate on whether necessary knowledge can transform into theoretical knowledge hinges on whether all sciences share a common genus. Al-Qadi argues they do, so if some sciences are theoretical, all can be. Al-Amidi challenges this, arguing that the concepts applied to sciences may be accidental, not essential. This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from the statement of al-Qadi to al-Amidi's objection.
The passage includes a clarification that transformation is not re-acquisition or transfer.
2
Identify al-Qadi's argument premise
Find the premise that all sciences are homogeneous in genus (knowledge). Understand how this supports the conclusion that if some sciences are theoretical, all can be.
Homogeneity is the core concept.
3
Understand al-Amidi's objection
Al-Amidi denies homogeneity. He argues that 'knowledge', 'perception', etc., may be accidental concepts, not essential genera. Thus sciences may differ in type and individual.
He allows type difference as possible, which blocks al-Qadi's argument.
4
Clarify what transformation is not
The passage explicitly says transformation does not mean re-acquiring the same knowledge through another method, nor transferring knowledge from one person to another.
This prevents misunderstanding.
5
Reflect on the import
Consider why this argument matters in kalam: the nature of knowledge and its categorization affects how we understand acquired vs. necessary truths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الثالث عشر : انقلاب العلم الضروري نظرياً ] (هل ينقلب العلم الضروري) نظرياً (و) العلم (النظري) ضرورياً أو لا أما انقلاب الضروري نظرياً ففيه (مذاهب ثلاثة (الأول: قول القاضي وبعض المتكلمين: يجوز مطلقاً لأن العلوم) بأسرها (متجانسة) متشاركة في جنسها الذي هو العلم ( فيصح على كل منها ما صح على الآخر وقد صح على بعض العلوم أن يكون نظرياً فكذا الباقي . قال الآمدي : إن سلم التجانس وأشار به إلى أنه يمكن منع التجانس لجواز أن يكون العلم والإدراك والإحاطة وغيرها مفهومات عارضة للعلوم فلا تكون متشاركة فيما يكون جنسا لها بل فيما هو عرض عام بالقياس إليها فلا شك في الاختلاف بالنوع والشخص أما الاختلاف الشخصي فلا ريبة فيه، وأما الاختلاف النوعي فهو جائز، وذلك يكفيه فيما هو بصدده (فلعل التنوع والتشخص بدونه وليس المراد أن العلم الذي حصل بلا نظر يصير حاصلا بالنظر فإنه محال لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل، ولأنه ليس بانقلاب بل حصول العلم بطريقين ولا أن العلم الذي حصل لأحد بلا نظر حاصلاً لآخر ينظر فإنه لا انقلاب أيضاً ولا خفاء في وقوعه . قوله : (ما صح على الآخر ) أي النظر إلى كونه علماً . قوله : ( وقد صح على بعض العلوم) أي النظر إلى كونه علماً فإن قسمة العلم إلى كونه ضروياً ونظرياً من حيث ذاته لا باعتبار خصوصية العالم أو المعلوم . قوله : ( أما الاختلاف الشخصي إلخ إشارة إلى تعميم المتن أي لا شك في الاختلاف جوازاً ووقوعاً، أي لا شك في الاختلاف بالنوع جوازاً، وفي الاختلاف بالشخص وقوعاً، وإنما لم يحمل على وقوعه كما هو المتبادر مع تحقق نوعي العلم من التصور والتصديق، لأن نوعيتها غير متحقق عند الأصحاب، فإن قسمة العلم إليها باعتبار إيجابه الحكم وعدمه، بناء على أن العلم صفة توجب تمييزاً لا يحتمل النقيض. قوله : ( وذلك يكفيه إلخ) لأنه مانع . قوله : ( فلعل التنوع إلخ) وما ذكره المستدل أنه قد صح على بعض العلوم كونه نظرياً من حيث أنه علم ممنوع وقد ذهب الإمام إلى أن التصورات كلها ضرورية. فلا يقتضي الاختصاص بمحله وإلا لكان أخص الصفات هذا اللهم إلا أن يقال : المطابقة أخص صفات مطلق العلم ، فلا ينافي كون الاختصاص بمحله أخص صفات فرد منه، فتأمل. قوله : (الأول) قول القاضي محصل هذا القول هو الإيجاب الكلي ومحصل القول الثاني هو السلب الكلي ومحصل الثالث هو السلب الجزئي . قوله : ( لأن العلوم بأسرها متجانسة هذا يدل على أن ليس مراد القاضي بالتجانس التماثل إذ قد سبق أن العلمين المتعلقين بمعلومين مختلفان لا متماثلان إلا عند والد الإمام الرازي . قوله: (وأما الاختلاف النوعي فجائز ظاهر كلام المصنف هو الجزم بالاختلاف النوعي مبني على أن التوقف على الكسب فصل مقوم معتبر في ماهية العلم النظري وعدمه فصل مقوم معتبر في ماهية العلم الضروري فما ذكره الشارح من جواز الاختلاف النوعي، وإن كان كافياً في المقصود التمسك بالمنع بالسند في الحقيقة، لكنه ليس مؤدى كلام المصنف، إلا أن يحمل فكانه
[The Thirteenth Objective: The Transformation of Necessary Knowledge into Theoretical Knowledge] (Can necessary knowledge be transformed) into theoretical (and) theoretical knowledge into necessary or not? As for the transformation of necessary into theoretical, there are (three doctrines). (The first: the statement of al-Qadi and some theologians: it is absolutely permissible, because all sciences) are homogeneous, sharing in their genus which is knowledge. So what is valid for one is valid for the other, and it has been valid for some sciences to be theoretical, so likewise for the rest. Al-Amidi said: If homogeneity is granted, and he indicated thereby that it is possible to deny homogeneity because knowledge, perception, comprehension, and others may be accidental concepts that attach to sciences, so they do not share in something that is a genus for them, but rather in something that is a universal accident relative to them. So there is no doubt about difference in type and in individual. As for individual difference, there is no uncertainty; as for type difference, it is possible, and that suffices him for his purpose. (Perhaps diversity and individuation without it). And the intended meaning is not that knowledge which was obtained without reflection becomes obtained through reflection, for that is impossible due to the impossibility of attaining what is already attained, and because it is not a transformation but rather obtaining knowledge through two ways. Nor that knowledge which was obtained by someone without reflection becomes obtained by another through reflection, for that is also not a transformation, and its occurrence is not hidden. His statement: (what is valid for the other) meaning considering it as knowledge. His statement: (it has been valid for some sciences) meaning considering it as knowledge, for the division of knowledge into necessary and theoretical is with respect to its essence, not by consideration of the particularity of the knower or the known. His statement: (as for individual difference etc.) is an indication of universalizing the matn: i.e., there is no doubt about the difference in possibility and occurrence, i.e., there is no doubt about difference in type as possible, and difference in individual as actual. And it was not taken as actual as is the immediate understanding given the actuality of the two types of knowledge—conception and assent—because their being actual types is not established with the scholars, for the division of knowledge into them is based on whether it necessitates judgment or not, on the basis that knowledge is an attribute that necessitates a distinction that does not admit contradiction. His statement: (and that suffices him etc.) because it is a preventer. His statement: (perhaps diversity etc.) And what the proponent mentioned—that it has been valid for some sciences to be theoretical by virtue of being knowledge—is denied. And the Imam held that all conceptions are necessary. So it does not necessitate specificity to its locus, otherwise it would be the most specific of attributes, except that it might be said: correspondence is the most specific attribute of absolute knowledge, so it does not contradict that specificity to its locus is the most specific attribute of an individual of it. So consider. His statement: (the first) the statement of al-Qadi: the summary of this statement is absolute affirmation, and the summary of the second statement is absolute negation, and the summary of the third is partial negation. His statement: (because all sciences are homogeneous) indicates that al-Qadi did not mean by homogeneity similarity, for it was already mentioned that two knowledges related to two different objects are different, not similar, except according to the father of Imam al-Razi. His statement: (and as for type difference, it is possible) The apparent meaning of the author's words is the certainty of type difference, built on the fact that dependence on acquisition is a constitutive differentia considered in the quiddity of theoretical knowledge, and its absence is a constitutive differentia considered in the quiddity of necessary knowledge. So what the commentator mentioned about the possibility of type difference, even if it is sufficient for the purpose of sticking to the denial based on the chain in reality, is not the import of the author's words, unless it is interpreted. So it was.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a classification and argument. It begins by stating the question: can necessary knowledge become theoretical, and vice versa? It then lists three doctrines (though only the first is detailed here). The first doctrine, attributed to al-Qadi and some theologians, holds that transformation is absolutely possible because all sciences are homogeneous—they share the genus 'knowledge'. Therefore, whatever is valid for one type (e.g., being theoretical) is valid for another. Al-Amidi objects by challenging the homogeneity claim: concepts like 'knowledge', 'perception', and 'comprehension' may be accidental attributes, not essential genera, so sciences may differ in type. He allows for both individual and type difference, and says this suffices to block the argument. The commentary then clarifies that the proof's premise (that some sciences are theoretical by virtue of being knowledge) is denied, and notes that al-Ghazali considered all conceptions necessary. The passage concludes with summaries of the three positions: absolute affirmation (first), absolute negation (second), and partial negation (third). It also clarifies that al-Qadi's 'homogeneity' does not mean numerical similarity, as sciences differ.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Transformation means re-acquiring the same knowledge through a different method.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that transformation does not mean obtaining knowledge that was already obtained through reflection; that is impossible and is not transformation.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of al-Qadi's argument for the absolute possibility of transformation?
A) That all sciences are exactly similar in every respect.
B) That all sciences share the genus 'knowledge', so what applies to one applies to another.
C) That necessary knowledge can become theoretical through repeated practice.
D) That the knower's ability determines the type of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadi's argument for transformation relies on the homogeneity of sciences, which al-Amidi challenges by denying that homogeneity is essential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the very nature of knowledge can be debated, how do you anchor your faith in a way that withstands such intellectual scrutiny? What practical step can you take this week to ground your certainty in revelation rather than mere intellectual assent?
?
Al-Amidi argues that calling different sciences by the same name does not make them the same in essence. Where in your life do you assume unity where there is only superficial similarity? How might this assumption be hiding a weakness in your faith or practice?

Understanding Homogeneity: Genus vs. Similarity
Commentary analyzing the concept of homogeneity and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentary's clarification that homogeneity means sharing a genus, not being identical, and the distinction between type and individual difference.

Core Idea
Homogeneity does not mean exact similarity — two sciences may differ in type yet share a genus.

The commentary goes deeper into what 'homogeneity' really means. It explains that al-Qadi did not claim all sciences are identical (متماثلة), but that they share a genus ('ilm). The discussion of type and individual difference refines the dialectic. This lesson explores those nuances and concludes with a summary of the three doctrines.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on homogeneity
Read the Arabic passage that clarifies the meaning of homogeneity. Note that it is distinguished from similarity (تماثل).
The passage also discusses type and individual difference.
2
Differentiate homogeneity from similarity
The commentary states that al-Qadi did not mean that all sciences are numerically similar. Two sciences about different objects are different, not similar.
Homogeneity is about sharing a genus, not being identical.
3
Understand type vs. individual difference
Al-Amidi allows type difference as possible, but not necessary. The commentary discusses whether type difference is actual or merely possible.
This affects the strength of al-Amidi's objection.
4
Note the three doctrines summary
The passage ends with a summary: first doctrine (absolute affirmation), second (absolute negation), third (partial negation). This frames the broader debate.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this distinction impacts the original argument: if type difference is possible, al-Qadi's homogeneity claim is weakened.

جوازاً ووقوعاً، أي لا شك في الاختلاف بالنوع جوازاً، وفي الاختلاف بالشخص وقوعاً، وإنما لم يحمل على وقوعه كما هو المتبادر مع تحقق نوعي العلم من التصور والتصديق، لأن نوعيتها غير متحقق عند الأصحاب، فإن قسمة العلم إليها باعتبار إيجابه الحكم وعدمه، بناء على أن العلم صفة توجب تمييزاً لا يحتمل النقيض. قوله : ( وذلك يكفيه إلخ) لأنه مانع . قوله : ( فلعل التنوع إلخ) وما ذكره المستدل أنه قد صح على بعض العلوم كونه نظرياً من حيث أنه علم ممنوع وقد ذهب الإمام إلى أن التصورات كلها ضرورية. فلا يقتضي الاختصاص بمحله وإلا لكان أخص الصفات هذا اللهم إلا أن يقال : المطابقة أخص صفات مطلق العلم ، فلا ينافي كون [The Thirteenth Objective: The Transformation of Necessary Knowledge into Theoretical Knowledge] (Can necessary knowledge be transformed) into theoretical (and) theoretical knowledge into necessary or not? As for the transformation of necessary into theoretical, there are (three doctrines). (The first: the statement of al-Qadi and some theologians: it is absolutely permissible, because all sciences) are homogeneous, sharing in their genus which is knowledge. So what is valid for one is valid for the other, and it has been valid for some sciences to be theoretical, so likewise for the rest. Al-Amidi said: If homogeneity is granted, and he indicated thereby that it is possible to deny homogeneity because knowledge, perception, comprehension, and others may be accidental concepts that attach to sciences, so they do not share in something that is a genus for them, but rather in something that is a universal accident relative to them. So there is no doubt about difference in type and in individual. As for individual difference, there is no uncertainty; as for type difference, it is possible, and that suffices him for his purpose. (Perhaps diversity and individuation without it). And the intended meaning is not that knowledge which was obtained without reflection becomes obtained through reflection, for that is impossible due to the impossibility of attaining what is already attained, and because it is not a transformation but rather obtaining knowledge through two ways. Nor that knowledge which was obtained by someone without reflection becomes obtained by another through reflection, for that is also not a transformation, and its occurrence is not hidden. His statement: (what is valid for the other) meaning considering it as knowledge. His statement: (it has been valid for some sciences) meaning considering it as knowledge, for the division of knowledge into necessary and theoretical is with respect to its essence, not by consideration of the particularity of the knower or the known. His statement: (as for individual difference etc.) is an indication of universalizing the matn: i.e., there is no doubt about the difference in possibility and occurrence, i.e., there is no doubt about difference in type as possible, and difference in individual as actual. And it was not taken as actual as is the immediate understanding given the actuality of the two types of knowledge—conception and assent—because their being actual types is not established with the scholars, for the division of knowledge into them is based on whether it necessitates judgment or not, on the basis that knowledge is an attribute that necessitates a distinction that does not admit contradiction. His statement: (and that suffices him etc.) because it is a preventer. His statement: (perhaps diversity etc.) And what the proponent mentioned—that it has been valid for some sciences to be theoretical by virtue of being knowledge—is denied. And the Imam held that all conceptions are necessary. So it does not necessitate specificity to its locus, otherwise it would be the most specific of attributes, except that it might be said: correspondence is the most specific attribute of absolute knowledge, so it does not contradict that specificity to its locus is the most specific attribute of an individual of it. So consider. His statement: (the first) the statement of al-Qadi: the summary of this statement is absolute affirmation, and the summary of the second statement is absolute negation, and the summary of the third is partial negation. His statement: (because all sciences are homogeneous) indicates that al-Qadi did not mean by homogeneity similarity, for it was already mentioned that two knowledges related to two different objects are different, not similar, except according to the father of Imam al-Razi. His statement: (and as for type difference, it is possible) The apparent meaning of the author's words is the certainty of type difference, built on the fact that dependence on acquisition is a constitutive differentia considered in the quiddity of theoretical knowledge, and its absence is a constitutive differentia considered in the quiddity of necessary knowledge. So what the commentator mentioned about the possibility of type difference, even if it is sufficient for the purpose of sticking to the denial based on the chain in reality, is not the import of the author's words, unless it is interpreted. So it was.

The Permissibility of Transforming Necessary Knowledge into Speculative
First opinion and its proof based on homogeneity of sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first opinion of the theologians on the transformation of necessary knowledge into speculative knowledge, its proof based on homogeneity, and al-Amidi's objection regarding the nature of the genus.

Core Idea
All sciences share a common genus, so what applies to one applies to all—but is knowledge truly a genus?

The question of whether necessary knowledge can become speculative hinges on the nature of knowledge itself. The theologians argue that since all sciences are homogeneous in their genus, any property of one science can extend to others. Al-Amidi challenges this by questioning the very homogeneity. This passage presents both sides succinctly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: opinion, proof, objection, clarification.
2
Identify the proof for the first opinion
The theologians' proof rests on the claim that all sciences are homogeneous in their genus (knowledge). Find the sentence that states this and understand its logic.
3
Examine al-Amidi's objection
Al-Amidi grants homogeneity but then denies it, arguing that concepts like knowledge may be accidental (ʿāriḍa) rather than a true genus. See how he introduces the idea of a general accident.
4
Understand the clarification
The passage ends by clarifying what transformation is not: it is not reacquiring the same knowledge (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) nor transferring knowledge between persons. This shows the intended meaning.
5
Reflect on the dialectic
Consider how this argument illustrates the method of kalām: a thesis, a proof, an objection, and a clarification. How does al-Amidi's objection weaken the theologians' position?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتكلمين: يجوز مطلقاً لأن العلوم بأسرها متجانسة متشاركة في جنسها الذي هو العلم فيصح على كل منها ما صح على الآخر وقد صح على بعض العلوم أن يكون نظرياً فكذا الباقي. قال الآمدي: إن سلم التجانس وأشار به إلى أنه يمكن منع التجانس لجواز أن يكون العلم والإدراك والإحاطة وغيرها مفهومات عارضة للعلوم فلا تكون متشاركة فيما يكون جنسا لها بل فيما هو عرض عام بالقياس إليها فلا شك في الاختلاف بالنوع والشخص أما الاختلاف الشخصي فلا ريبة فيه، وأما الاختلاف النوعي فهو جائز، وذلك يكفيه فيما هو بصدده (فلعل التنوع والتشخص بدونه وليس المراد أن العلم الذي حصل بلا نظر يصير حاصلا بالنظر فإنه محال لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل، ولأنه ليس بانقلاب بل حصول العلم بطريقين ولا أن العلم الذي حصل لأحد بلا نظر حاصلاً لآخر ينظر فإنه لا انقلاب أيضاً ولا خفاء في وقوعه.
The theologians [say]: It is absolutely permissible, because all sciences are homogeneous, sharing in their genus which is knowledge, so whatever is correct for one is correct for the other, and it has been correct for some sciences to be speculative, so likewise the rest. Al-Amidi said: If homogeneity is granted—and he indicated by this that it is possible to deny homogeneity because knowledge, perception, comprehension, and others may be accidental concepts to the sciences, so they do not share in what is a genus for them, but rather in what is a general accident relative to them—then there is no doubt about difference in species and individual. As for difference in individual, there is no doubt about it; as for difference in species, it is possible. And that suffices him for what he is about. (Perhaps the variety and individuation are without that. And it is not meant that knowledge acquired without reflection becomes acquired by reflection, for that is impossible due to the impossibility of obtaining the obtained, and because it is not a transformation but rather obtaining knowledge by two ways. Nor that knowledge which someone acquired without reflection is acquired by another who reflects, for that too is not a transformation, and there is no concealment in its occurrence.)
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the first opinion of the theologians (al-mutakallimūn) that it is absolutely permissible for necessary knowledge to transform into speculative knowledge. The proof is based on the homogeneity (tajānus) of all sciences in their genus, which is knowledge (ʿilm). Since it is established that some sciences (like speculative ones) are speculative, then by virtue of sharing the same genus, all sciences should be capable of being speculative. Al-Āmidī raises an objection: if we grant homogeneity, one might still deny it because concepts like knowledge, perception, and comprehension may be accidental (ʿāriḍa) to the sciences, not constituting a true genus but only a general accident (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm). Thus there is undoubted difference in species (nawʿ) and individual (shakhṣ). Individual difference is certain, species difference is possible, and that suffices for his refutation. The passage then clarifies that transformation does not mean the same knowledge is obtained twice (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil is impossible), nor that knowledge from one person passes to another—it merely means the possibility of a different mode of acquisition.

Key Terms
انقلاب العلم الضروري نظرياً
تجانس
جنس العلم
عرض عام
نوع
شخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The transformation means the same knowledge is reacquired by reflection after being acquired without reflection.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that this is impossible (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) and that transformation is not about obtaining the same knowledge twice, but about the possibility of a different mode of acquisition.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise in the proof for the first opinion that necessary knowledge can become speculative?
A) All sciences are homogeneous in their genus, which is knowledge.
B) Necessary knowledge never changes.
C) Speculative knowledge is impossible.
D) Knowledge is acquired only by divine creation.

Key Takeaway
The first opinion relies on the homogeneity of sciences, but al-Amidi shows that the shared concept may not be a true genus, leaving room for species differences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an area of your religious understanding that you treat as 'necessary'—so certain that you never question it? What would it take for you to honestly examine whether it might actually be speculative, like the theologians debated?
?
Al-Amidi warns us not to mistake a general accident for a true genus. In your own life, where have you assumed that two things share a deep essence when they only share a superficial similarity? How might that assumption be limiting your growth?
?
The theologians argued that all sciences are homogeneous, so what applies to one applies to all. Do you treat different aspects of your deen (e.g., beliefs, practices, inner states) as if they must all follow the same rules? Or do you recognize that some may be necessary and others speculative? Which part of your faith are you most resistant to subjecting to rational scrutiny, and why?

The Impossibility of Transforming Necessary Knowledge
Presents the second opinion, proof, qualifications, and defenses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second opinion that necessary knowledge cannot transform into speculative knowledge, and its proof that such transformation would force a rational reflector to be devoid of foundational necessary truths.

Core Idea
Transforming necessary knowledge is impossible because it would require the mind to lack what it already necessarily knows.

This passage presents the second opinion that the transformation of necessary knowledge into speculative is absolutely impossible. The proof centers on the incompatibility of reflection (naẓar) with already possessing knowledge of the object—a reflective state cannot coexist with certain knowledge. The argument leads to the startling consequence that if transformation were possible, a rational person engaged in reflection could lack knowledge of the impossibility of opposites, which is absurd. The commentator then defends this argument by clarifying its scope and addressing objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the opinion and its proof
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the claim that transformation is absolutely not permissible (lā yajūzu muṭlaqan) and the proof based on the incompatibility of reflection and knowledge.
The proof is built on a previously established principle: reflection (naẓar) is contrary to knowledge of the object under investigation.
2
Understand the consequence
If necessary knowledge could become speculative, then during reflection one would lack that knowledge. This would lead to the possibility of being devoid of knowledge of the impossibility of two opposites—a foundational necessary truth.
3
Note the qualification
The commentator specifies that the argument applies only to the 'rational reflector' (al-ʿāqil al-nāẓir). Non-rational beings (children, madmen) can lack necessary knowledge, and even a rational person who is not focusing can lack it. The argument targets the attentive reflector.
This qualification answers a potential counterexample.
4
Examine the response to al-Āmidī
The commentator addresses al-Āmidī's objection about union in genus, clarifying that the motive for rejecting similarity is that two sciences of different objects cannot be identical. Al-Āmidī's words may also be interpreted as conditional.
This part delves into the nature of sciences and their individuation.
5
Consider the Ashʿarī response
The passage addresses the objection based on Ashʿarī occasionalism: if God creates all sciences, He could create the conception without assent. The response is that the will to create the conception is necessarily concomitant with creating the assent, based on the principle of generation (tawlīd).
The text cuts off but the point is clear.

Commentary
This passage presents the second opinion that the transformation of necessary knowledge into speculative is absolutely impermissible (lā yajūzu muṭlaqan). The proof is that if it were possible, then during reflection one would be devoid of that necessary knowledge, because reflection (naẓar) is contrary to knowledge of the object under investigation. This would lead to the possibility that a rational person engaged in reflection could be devoid of knowledge of the impossibility of the conjunction of opposites—a foundational necessary truth. The commentator then explains various points: he clarifies that al-Āmidī's objection about union in genus is based on philosophical terminology, but the motive for rejecting similarity is that two sciences of different objects are necessarily dissimilar. If sciences were similar, they could not coexist in one locus. He notes that al-Āmidī's words do not necessarily imply sharing in genus; his meaning could be conditional. The commentator then explains the qualification of 'rational' and 'reflector': non-rational beings (children, madmen) can lack necessary knowledge, but the argument concerns the rational reflector who is attentive. The passage also addresses an objection from the Ashʿarī doctrine of acquisition (kash) and divine creation: if God creates all sciences directly, He could create the conception of the two opposites without the assent, even for primary truths. But the response is that the attachment of the will to create the conception is necessarily concomitant with the creation of the assent, based on the principle of generation (tawlīd). The text cuts off mid-sentence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of being devoid of necessary knowledge applies to all humans at all times.
Correction: The argument only applies to a rational person who is actively reflecting (nāẓir) and attentive; children, the insane, and even rational persons not focusing can lack necessary knowledge.

Checkpoint
Why does the second opinion argue that transformation of necessary knowledge into speculative is impossible?
A) Because necessary knowledge is eternal and cannot change.
B) Because reflection contradicts knowledge of the object, so one would be devoid of necessary knowledge during reflection, which is impossible for foundational truths.
C) Because the theologians unanimously agree that it is impossible.
D) Because necessary knowledge is only created by God and cannot be acquired by reflection.

Key Takeaway
The second opinion proves that necessary knowledge cannot transform into speculative because reflection preexists knowledge of the object, and the alternative leads to a rational being lacking foundational necessary truths—an absurdity.

How will you guard your heart from entertaining such doubts?
?
When you engage in deep reflection about matters of faith (like the nature of Allah's actions or the problem of evil), do you ever feel you are losing the simple certainty you once had? According to this passage, is that a sign of your reflection being flawed, or that you are not reflecting properly? What concrete step can you take to ensure your reflection rests on necessary principles?
?
The passage insists that a rational person cannot lack knowledge of the impossibility of opposites while reflecting. Yet many Muslims today accept contradictions in their beliefs (e.g., affirming both divine mercy and harsh predetermination without reconciling them). Are you among them? Will you commit to examining your own heart for any such contradictions and resolve to hold your faith with rational coherence?

Defending Knowledge Transformation: Objection and Answer
Objection and answer on permissibility of transforming necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection against the transformation of necessary knowledge is based on particularity of types/individuals and how al-Amidi answers it by denying similarity or attributing prevention to individualization.

Core Idea
Validity may be type-specific; homogeneity does not guarantee transferability.

This lesson presents a key objection to the claim that necessary knowledge can become theoretical: what holds for one type or person may not hold for another, even if they share a genus. The answer, attributed to al-Amidi, offers two strategies to defend the possibility of transformation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the argument that validity is tied to the particularity of a type or individual, preventing transfer across different types/individuals.
The example: human vs. horse, Zayd vs. Amr.
2
Read the answer
Learn al-Amidi's two-pronged response: either deny similarity (tamāthul) or attribute the prevention to individual particularities even among similar individuals.
The answer preserves the possibility of transformation.
3
Connect to the broader debate
See how this objection and answer fit into the larger discussion on whether necessary knowledge can become theoretical.
This is a defensive move for the view that transformation is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمنع ذلك الذي صح على النوع أو التشخص الآخر إذ لا أن على الإنسان ما يصح على الفرس ) وإن كانا متشاركين في الجنس ( ولا ) أن يصح ( على زيد ما يصح على عمرو مع تشاركهما في تمام الماهية فإن الصحة ربما كانت معللة بخصوصية نوع أو شخص وكانت خصوصية نوع أو شخص آخر مانعة منها، فإن قيل: الظاهر من التجانس على اصطلاحهم هو التماثل، لا ما ذكره الآمدي؟ قلنا : فله حينئذ أن يمنع التماثل أو ينسب منع الصحة إلى تشخص الأفراد المتماثلة، كما أشار إليه.
It is prevented that which is valid for one type or individual [applies] to another, for what is valid for a human is not valid for a horse, even if they share in genus; nor is what is valid for Zayd valid for Amr, despite their sharing in the complete essence. For validity may be caused by the particularity of a type or individual, and the particularity of another type or individual may prevent it. If it is said: The apparent meaning of homogeneity in their convention is similarity, not what al-Amidi mentioned? We say: Then he can deny similarity or attribute the prevention of validity to the individualization of similar individuals, as he indicated.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the first view (that transformation of necessary knowledge is permitted) and its answer. The objector argues that what holds for one type or individual (e.g., human) does not automatically hold for another (e.g., horse) even if they share a genus, because validity may depend on specific particularities. The answer, attributed to al-Amidi, is that one can either deny the supposed homogeneity (tamāthul) between different types or persons, or attribute the difference to individual particularities even among similar individuals. Thus, the objection fails to disprove the possibility of transformation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That homogeneity (tajānus) necessarily implies transferability of properties across types or individuals.
Correction: The passage shows that even with shared genus or essence, validity may be prevented by particularities of type or individual; thus homogeneity does not guarantee transferability.

Checkpoint

B) He either denies similarity or attributes the prevention to individual particularities.
C) He accepts the objection and abandons the first view.
D) He claims that validity is not caused by particularities.

Key Takeaway
The transformation of necessary knowledge is defended by denying similarity or attributing differences in validity to individual particularities, not by assuming homogeneity guarantees transferability.

Which of these two paths do you most need to practice in your own heart when you are tempted to judge others? Commit to one concrete step this week to apply it.

The Absolute Impossibility of Transforming Necessary Knowledge
The second school's proof for absolute impossibility of transformation of necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second school's arguments for the absolute impossibility of necessary knowledge transforming into theoretical knowledge, including the direct proof from the impossibility of being devoid of necessary truths, the indirect proof from the existence of theoretical sciences, and the methodological critique of relying on intuition.

Core Idea
Transformation of necessary knowledge is absolutely impossible because it would unravel all rational inquiry.

This passage presents a deeper layer of the debate on the transformation of knowledge. Having established that some theologians maintain absolute impossibility, Sanusi now provides the proof structure: a direct argument from the inability to be devoid of necessary truths, an indirect argument from the existence of theoretical sciences, and a subtle critique of relying on intuition. Let's walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Get an overall sense of the argument's flow before diving into details.
2
Identify the two main proofs
The passage offers two arguments: first, a direct argument that transformation would require the inquirer to be devoid of all necessary knowledge, which is impossible by intuition. Second, an indirect argument: if transformation were possible in one, it would be possible in all, leading to the impossibility of any theoretical science, contradicting the existence of such sciences.
3
Note the objection and its handling
The direct argument faces an objection: transformation might only occur in non-essential necessary truths. The second argument implicitly addresses this by showing that if any necessary truth can transform, then all can, leading to a universal breakdown.
The second argument is more robust against this objection.
4
Reflect on the concluding remark
Sanusi notes that the proof would be stronger if it relied on logical impossibility rather than intuition, because intuition only shows that we are not actually devoid, not that it is impossible to be so.
This highlights the difference between de facto and de jure impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذهب الثاني : وعليه آخرون من المتكلمين لا يجوز مطلقاً وإلا لجاز الخلو عن الضروري إذ قد مر أن النظر ينافي العلم بالمطلوب المنظور فيه فإذا انقلب الضروري نظرياً وجب أن يكون الناظر في ذلك النظري خالياً عن العلم به، وذلك يؤدي إلى جواز خلو العاقل الناظر في العلوم عن العلم باستحالة اجتماع الضدين، وبأنه لا واسطة بين النفي والإثبات وبأن الكل أعظم من الجزء إلى غير ذلك من الضروريات التي تلزم العاقل، وأنه محال بالوجدان الشاهد بأن أمثال ما ذكر من البديهيات يستحيل انفكاك العاقل عنها، وفيه بحث لجواز أن يكون الانقلاب فيما عداها من الضروريات التي يجوز فقدانها، وقد يستدل لهذا المذهب بأنه لو جاز الانقلاب في الضروري لجاز في الكل، وما هو جائز لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، فلنفرض أن الضروريات انقلبت نظرية وحينئذ يستحيل حصول شيء من العلوم النظرية، إذ لا بد من انتهائها إلى العلم الضروري دفعاً للدور والتسلسل وفيه ما قد عرفته آنفاً وأيضاً حصول العلوم النظرية واقع فدل على أن ذلك التقدير أعني : انقلاب الضروريات جميع جميع العلوم الضرورية التي هي شرط لأهلية النظر كما مر فيلزم أن يكون تلك العلوم شرطاً، وأن لا يكون شرطاً، وأنه محال وبهذا تبين أنه لو اكتفى بقوله : وأنه محال ولم يقيده بقوله : بالوجدان لكان أسد لئلا يرد البحث بأن الوجدان إنما يدل على عدم الخلو لا على عدم جوازه.
The second school: and upon it are others from the theologians: it is not permitted absolutely, otherwise it would be possible to be devoid of the necessary, for it has passed that inquiry contradicts knowledge of the sought object of inquiry. So if the necessary becomes theoretical, the inquirer in that theoretical matter must be devoid of knowledge of it, and that leads to the possibility of the rational inquirer in the sciences being devoid of knowledge of the impossibility of the conjunction of two opposites, and that there is no intermediary between negation and affirmation, and that the whole is greater than the part, and other necessary matters that are incumbent upon the rational [inquirer]. And it is impossible by intuition, which attests that examples like those mentioned of self-evident truths cannot be absent from the rational person. And in it there is discussion because it is possible that the transformation be in other than these necessary matters which it is permissible to lack. And it may be argued for this school that if transformation were possible in the necessary, it would be possible in all. And what is possible, from the assumption of its occurrence no impossibility necessarily follows. So let us assume that the necessary matters became theoretical, and then it would be impossible to obtain any theoretical science, since they must terminate in necessary knowledge to avoid regress and infinite series. And in it there is what you already know. Also, the occurrence of theoretical sciences is real, so it indicates that that estimation, namely the transformation of all necessary sciences which are a condition for the capability of inquiry as mentioned, entails that those sciences would be a condition and not a condition, and that is impossible. And by this it becomes clear that if he had been content with his statement 'and it is impossible' and did not qualify it with 'by intuition', it would be more sound, lest the objection arise that intuition only indicates non-absence, not the impossibility of [absence].
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents the second school's view that transformation of necessary knowledge into theoretical knowledge is absolutely impossible. The proof is: if it were possible, it would lead to the possibility of a rational thinker being entirely devoid of all necessary knowledge (e.g., the impossibility of opposites, the whole being greater than the part, etc.), which is impossible by intuitive experience (wijdān). An objection is raised: transformation might be possible only for non-essential necessary truths. The passage then provides another indirect proof: if transformation were possible in one necessary truth, it would be possible in all; assuming all necessary truths become theoretical, no theoretical science could be established due to infinite regress, but theoretical sciences do exist, so the assumption is false. The passage concludes that the argument would be stronger if it relied solely on impossibility without appealing to intuition, since intuition only shows de facto absence, not impossibility.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the impossibility of transformation is proven solely by intuition (wijdān).
Correction: The passage shows that intuition only indicates de facto absence, not absurdity. The stronger proof is the logical contradiction that arises from assuming transformation of all necessary truths.

Checkpoint
What is the main proof given for the second school's position that transformation of necessary knowledge is impossible?
A) Transformation would require an infinite regress of theoretical sciences.
B) It would lead to the possibility of a rational inquirer being devoid of all necessary knowledge, which is impossible.
C) Intuition shows that no necessary knowledge ever becomes theoretical.
D) The occurrence of theoretical sciences proves that necessary knowledge must be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The absolute impossibility of transforming necessary knowledge is proven by both a direct argument (impossible to be devoid of necessary truths) and an indirect argument (transformation would undermine all theoretical science), with the latter being more logically robust.

?

?

This means your certainty about many things might be based on habit, not proof. Name one Islamic belief you hold that you have never seriously doubted. If you discovered one of them was merely theoretical, would you feel a sense of betrayal? This lesson asks: on what foundation are you actually building your life, and is it strong enough to hold the weight of your eternal soul?
